**Critical Test — The Period Substitution Test:** If you are unsure whether a comma + FANBOYS is correct, try replacing the comma + FANBOYS with a period. If both resulting sentences are grammatically complete, the comma + FANBOYS is correct. If either resulting sentence is a fragment, it is incorrect.

> *I wanted to go to the party. I had to study for my exam.* ✓ (Both are complete sentences → comma + "but" is correct.)

> *Running down the street. The trees were beautiful.* ✗ (First part is a fragment → comma + FANBOYS would be wrong here.)

**ACT Trap — The FANBOYS Trap:** The ACT frequently places a FANBOYS word in a context where it does NOT join two independent clauses. In such cases, the comma is **incorrect**.

> *She opened the door **and** walked outside.* (No comma — "walked outside" is not an independent clause because it lacks a subject.)

> *He studied hard **but** failed the test.* (No comma — "failed the test" has no subject; it shares the subject "He" with "studied hard.")

The rule is precise: **comma + FANBOYS is only correct when what follows the FANBOYS is an independent clause** (i.e., it could stand alone as a complete sentence with its own subject and verb).

**ACT Trap — The Comma Without FANBOYS:** Sometimes the ACT gives you two independent clauses joined by a comma but NO conjunction. This is a **comma splice** and is always wrong.

> *She was tired, she went to bed.* ✗ (Comma splice — always incorrect.)

The fix requires one of three options:
1. Replace the comma with a period: *She was tired. She went to bed.*
2. Replace the comma with a semicolon: *She was tired; she went to bed.*
3. Add a FANBOYS conjunction after the comma: *She was tired, so she went to bed.*

---

#### Rule 3: Comma After an Introductory Element

When a sentence begins with a dependent clause, a long prepositional phrase, a participial phrase, or a transitional expression, a comma must follow the introductory element before the main clause begins.

**3a: Introductory Dependent Clauses (Subordinating Conjunctions)**

When a sentence begins with a dependent clause (introduced by a subordinating conjunction like *although, because, since, when, if, while, after, before, unless, until, even though, though, whereas, as*), place a comma after the clause.

> ***Although** it was raining**,** we decided to go hiking.*

> ***Because** the data were incomplete**,** the researchers could not draw firm conclusions.*

> ***When** the concert ended**,** the crowd dispersed slowly.*

> ***If** you study consistently**,** you will improve your score.*

**Exception — Dependent clause at the END of the sentence:** When the dependent clause comes at the end of the sentence, a comma is generally **not** used.

> *We decided to go hiking **although it was raining**.* (No comma needed.)

> *The researchers could not draw firm conclusions **because the data were incomplete**.* (No comma needed.)

**Nuance:** Some style guides recommend a comma before a trailing *although* or *because* clause for emphasis or clarity, but the ACT follows the standard rule: **no comma before a trailing dependent clause.**

**3b: Introductory Prepositional Phrases**

Short introductory prepositional phrases (fewer than about four words) often do not require a comma, but longer ones do. The ACT generally requires a comma after any introductory prepositional phrase that is long enough to cause misreading.

> ***In the morning**,** I like to drink coffee.*

> ***After a long and exhausting day at the office**,** she collapsed on the couch.*

> ***During the summer of 1969**,** millions watched the moon landing on television.*

**3c: Introductory Participial Phrases**

When a sentence begins with a participial phrase (an -ing or -ed phrase that functions as an adjective), place a comma after it. The subject of the sentence must be the logical "doer" of the action in the participial phrase.

> ***Running down the street**,** I noticed the beautiful trees.* ("I" was running.)

> ***Exhausted from the hike**,** they decided to rest.* ("They" were exhausted.)

> ***Having finished the exam**,** she left the room.* ("She" finished the exam.)

**3d: Introductory Transitional Words and Phrases**

When a sentence begins with a transitional word or phrase (*however, therefore, moreover, furthermore, nevertheless, consequently, for example, for instance, in addition, on the other hand, as a result*), it is typically followed by a comma.

> ***However**,** the results were inconclusive.*

> ***For example**,** many species have adapted to urban environments.*

> ***As a result**,** the policy was revised.*

---

#### Rule 4: Commas Around Nonessential (Nonrestrictive) Elements

This is one of the most commonly tested comma rules on the ACT, and it requires careful analysis.

A **nonessential element** is a word, phrase, or clause that adds extra information but is **not necessary** for the sentence to make sense or for the noun to be identified. If you remove it, the sentence still works and the core meaning is unchanged. Nonessential elements must be set off with **a pair of commas** (or dashes or parentheses).

An **essential element** provides information that is **necessary** to identify the noun or clarify the meaning. Essential elements do **not** use commas.

**The Removal Test:** To determine whether an element is essential or nonessential, mentally remove it. If the sentence still makes sense and the identity of the noun is clear, the element is nonessential and needs commas. If the sentence becomes unclear or the noun can no longer be identified, the element is essential and does not need commas.

**4a: Nonessential Relative Clauses**

> *My brother**,** who lives in Boston**,** is visiting us.* (Nonessential — I have only one brother; "who lives in Boston" is extra information.)

> *The book **that I borrowed from the library** was fascinating.* (Essential — "that I borrowed from the library" identifies which book; no commas.)

> *The Eiffel Tower**,** which was built in 1889**,** is a famous landmark.* (Nonessential — there is only one Eiffel Tower; the date is extra information.)

**4b: Nonessential Appositives**

An appositive is a noun or noun phrase that renames or identifies another noun. If the appositive is nonessential (the noun is already clearly identified), it must be set off with commas.

> *My friend **David** is a doctor.* (Essential — I have multiple friends; "David" identifies which one. No commas.)

> *My best friend**,** David**,** is a doctor.* (Nonessential — "best friend" already identifies the person; "David" is extra information. Commas required.)

> *Paris**,** the capital of France**,** is a beautiful city.* (Nonessential — "Paris" is already identified; "the capital of France" is extra information.)

**The Name Rule (Specific vs. Non-Specific Identifier):**

This is a powerful shortcut for appositive comma questions:

- If the identifier **before** the name points to one specific person or thing (e.g., *my best friend, my youngest sister, the capital of Paris*), the name is **nonessential** → use commas.
- If the identifier **before** the name is **not** specific to one person (e.g., *my friend, a resident of Paris*), the name is **essential** → no commas.

> *My best friend**,** Sarah**,** is a doctor.* ("Best friend" = specific → commas.)

> *My friend Sarah is a doctor.* ("My friend" = not specific (I have many friends) → no commas.)

**4c: Nonessential Parenthetical Expressions and Interjections**

Words and phrases like *of course, to be sure, in fact, indeed, naturally, admittedly, fortunately,* and *unfortunately* are typically set off with commas when they interrupt the flow of a sentence.

> *The results**,** of course**,** were unexpected.*

> *He is**,** in fact**,** the best candidate for the position.*

**4d: Direct Address**

When directly addressing someone by name, set off the name with commas.

> *John**,** can you help me with this?*

> *I believe**,** Senator**,** that the bill should be revised.*

---

#### Rule 5: Commas Between Coordinate Adjectives

When two or more adjectives independently modify the same noun (i.e., each adjective separately describes the noun), they are called **coordinate adjectives** and must be separated by a comma.

**The "And" Test:** If you can insert the word "and" between the adjectives without changing the meaning, they are comma-worthy.

**The Reversal Test:** If you can reverse the order of the adjectives without changing the meaning, they are comma-worthy.

> *It was a **long, difficult** test.* → "long and difficult test" ✓ / "difficult, long test" ✓ → **Use a comma.**

> *She wore a **big, red** shirt.* → "big and red shirt" ✓ / "red, big shirt" ✓ → **Use a comma.**

> *He drove a **small red** car.* → "small and red car" ✓ but "red small car" ✗ → **No comma.** ("Small" modifies "red car" as a unit, not the noun independently.)

> *She is a **talented young** actress.* → "talented and young actress" ✓ but "young talented actress" ✗ → **No comma.** ("Young" modifies "talented actress" as a unit.)

**Cumulative Adjectives (No Comma):** When adjectives build upon each other to modify the noun as a unit (cumulative adjectives), no comma is used. The typical order is: quantity → opinion → size → age → shape → color → origin → material → purpose → noun.

> *She bought a beautiful old Italian sports car.* (No commas — cumulative adjectives.)

> *The tall young man in the blue suit.* (No commas — cumulative.)

---

#### Rule 6: Commas with Conjunctive Adverbs and Transitional Expressions Mid-Sentence

When a conjunctive adverb (*however, therefore, moreover, furthermore, consequently, nevertheless, nonetheless, thus, hence, accordingly, meanwhile, otherwise, instead, indeed, still*) or a transitional expression appears **in the middle of a sentence** connecting two independent clauses, it must be preceded by a **semicolon** and followed by a **comma**.

**Formula:** Independent Clause **;** + conjunctive adverb **,** + Independent Clause

> *She studied hard**;** therefore**,** she passed the exam.*

> *The data were inconclusive**;** however**,** the researchers did not give up.*

> *He was exhausted**;** nevertheless**,** he continued working.*

**Critical Distinction:** Conjunctive adverbs are **NOT** conjunctions. They cannot by themselves join two independent clauses. A comma before a conjunctive adverb joining two independent clauses creates a **comma splice**, which is always wrong.

> *She was tired**,** however she continued working.* ✗ (Comma splice — always wrong.)

> *She was tired**;** however**,** she continued working.* ✓ (Semicolon + comma — correct.)

---

### Comma Prohibitions: When NOT to Use a Comma

Equally important as knowing when to use a comma is knowing when **not** to use one. The ACT frequently tests these prohibitions:

**1. Never place a comma between a subject and its verb:**

> *The **box of chocolates is** on the table.* (NOT: *The box of chocolates, is on the table.*)

> *The **collection of rare stamps was** donated to the museum.* (NOT: *The collection of rare stamps, was donated...*)

**2. Never place a comma between a verb and its direct object or complement:**

> *She **said that she was leaving**.* (NOT: *She said, that she was leaving.*)

> *He **believes that the earth is round**.* (NOT: *He believes, that the earth is round.*)

**3. Never place a comma before "that" when it introduces a noun clause:**

> *I know **that you are busy**.* (NOT: *I know, that you are busy.*)

> *She said **that she would come**.* (NOT: *She said, that she would come.*)

**4. Never place a comma between two subjects joined by "and" that share the same verb:**

> *John **and** Mary went to the store.* (NOT: *John, and Mary went to the store.*)

**5. Never place a comma between two verbs that share the same subject:**

> *She ran **to the store and** bought some milk.* (NOT: *She ran to the store, and bought some milk.* — UNLESS the comma is needed for clarity or to prevent misreading.)

**6. Never place a comma between an adjective and the noun it modifies:**

> *She is a **talented actress**.* (NOT: *She is a talented, actress.*)

**7. Never place a comma between an adverb and the adjective or verb it modifies:**

> *He is **extremely talented**.* (NOT: *He is extremely, talented.*)

> *She **quickly ran** to the store.* (NOT: *She quickly, ran to the store.*)

**8. Never place a comma before a coordinating conjunction when it joins two phrases (not independent clauses):**

> *She likes to swim **and** to run.* (NOT: *She likes to swim, and to run.*)

---

## 3.2 THE SEMICOLON (;)

The semicolon is the second most tested punctuation mark on the ACT. Its function is simple but precise: it joins two **closely related independent clauses** without a conjunction.

### The Core Rule

**A semicolon can replace a period when the two independent clauses are closely related in meaning.**

**Formula:** Independent Clause **;** Independent Clause

> *She was bored**;** she filled her spare time with inventing.*

> *The data were inconclusive**;** further research was needed.*

> *He didn't study**;** he failed the test.*

**The Period Substitution Test:** If you can replace the semicolon with a period and both resulting sentences are grammatically complete, the semicolon is correct. If either resulting sentence is a fragment, the semicolon is incorrect.

> *She was bored. She filled her spare time with inventing.* ✓ → Semicolon is correct.

> *Running down the street. The trees were beautiful.* ✗ → Semicolon would be incorrect.

### Semicolons with Conjunctive Adverbs

When a conjunctive adverb (*however, therefore, moreover, furthermore, consequently, nevertheless, nonetheless, thus, hence, accordingly, meanwhile, otherwise, instead, indeed, still*) connects two independent clauses, the first clause must end with a **semicolon**, and the conjunctive adverb must be followed by a **comma**.

**Formula:** Independent Clause **;** + conjunctive adverb **,** + Independent Clause

> *She studied hard**;** therefore**,** she passed the exam.*

> *The experiment failed**;** however**,** the team learned valuable lessons.*

> *He was exhausted**;** nevertheless**,** he continued working.*

> *The budget was cut**;** consequently**,** several programs were eliminated.*

**Common Conjunctive Adverbs Tested on the ACT:**

| Adverb | Meaning/Function |
|---|---|
| however | contrast |
| therefore | result/consequence |
| moreover | addition |
| furthermore | addition |
| consequently | result |
| nevertheless | contrast/despite |
| nonetheless | contrast/despite |
| thus | result/accordingly |
| hence | result/for this reason |
| accordingly | result/accordingly |
| meanwhile | time/simultaneous |
| otherwise | alternative |
| instead | alternative |
| indeed | emphasis/confirmation |
| still | contrast/despite |

**ACT Trap — Comma Splice with Conjunctive Adverb:** The ACT frequently presents sentences where a comma appears before a conjunctive adverb joining two independent clauses. This is always wrong.

> *She was tired**,** however she continued working.* ✗ (Comma splice.)

> *She was tired**;** however**,** she continued working.* ✓ (Semicolon + comma.)

### Semicolons in Complex Lists (The "Super Comma")

When items in a list themselves contain commas, use semicolons to separate the items. This prevents confusion.

> *We visited Paris, France**;** Rome, Italy**;** and Madrid, Spain.*

> *The committee includes Dr. Smith, the chair**;** Ms. Jones, the secretary**;** and Mr. Brown, the treasurer.*

> *The conference was held in Chicago, Illinois**;** Austin, Texas**;** and Portland, Oregon.*

### Semicolon Prohibitions

**1. Never use a semicolon to join an independent clause and a dependent clause:**

> *Although she was tired**;** she continued working.* ✗ (Use a comma, not a semicolon.)

> *Because the data were incomplete**;** the researchers could not draw conclusions.* ✗ (Use a comma.)

**2. Never use a semicolon to join a clause and a phrase:**

> *She was exhausted**;** having worked for twelve hours.* ✗ (Use a comma or rephrase.)

**3. Never use a semicolon to introduce a list directly after a verb or preposition:**

> *The challenges include**;** poverty, inequality, and climate change.* ✗ (Use a colon or no punctuation.)

---

## 3.3 THE COLON (:)

The colon is tested less frequently than commas and semicolons, but when it appears, it is a decisive test of whether you understand sentence completeness.

### The Core Rule

**The text before the colon must be a complete independent clause.** The text after the colon can be a word, a phrase, a list, or another independent clause — it introduces, explains, elaborates, or amplifies what came before.

**Formula:** Complete Independent Clause **:** + List / Explanation / Elaboration / Quotation

> *There are several challenges**:** an increase in the dependency ratio, discriminatory hiring practices, and stigmas toward older individuals.*

> *She had one goal**:** to win the championship.*

> *The conclusion was clear**:** the hypothesis had been confirmed.*

> *He offered one piece of advice**:** "Never give up."*

### The Colon Completeness Test

**The Left-Side Test:** The text to the LEFT of the colon must be an independent clause. If it is not, the colon is incorrect.

> *The three priorities are**:** growth, innovation, and sustainability.* ✗ ("The three priorities are" is NOT an independent clause — it needs a complement to be complete. The colon should be removed: *The three priorities are growth, innovation, and sustainability.*)

> *The three priorities are as follows**:** growth, innovation, and sustainability.* ✓ ("The three priorities are as follows" IS an independent clause — it can stand alone as a complete sentence.)

> *She enjoys**:** swimming, running, and biking.* ✗ ("She enjoys" is NOT an independent clause.)

> *She enjoys three activities**:** swimming, running, and biking.* ✓ ("She enjoys three activities" IS an independent clause.)

### Colon vs. Semicolon

| Feature | Colon | Semicolon |
|---|---|---|
| Left side | Must be independent clause | Must be independent clause |
| Right side | Can be anything (word, phrase, list, clause) | Must be independent clause |
| Function | Introduces, explains, amplifies | Connects equal ideas |
| Relationship | Dependent (right depends on left) | Equal (both sides are equal) |

> *She had one goal**:** to win the championship.* (Colon — right side explains left.)

> *She had one goal**;** she would not be denied.* (Semicolon — two equal independent clauses.)

### Colon Prohibitions

**1. Never use a colon directly after a verb:**

> *The colors are**:** red, blue, and green.* ✗ (Remove the colon: *The colors are red, blue, and green.*)

> *My favorite foods are**:** pizza, sushi, and tacos.* ✗ (Remove the colon.)

**2. Never use a colon directly after a preposition:**

> *I am interested in**:** science, math, and literature.* ✗ (Remove the colon.)

> *She is responsible for**:** hiring, training, and evaluation.* ✗ (Remove the colon.)

**3. Never use a colon when the text before it is not a complete independent clause:**

> *The reason is because**:** he was tired.* ✗ (Rephrase: *The reason is that he was tired.* or *He was tired.*)

---

## 3.4 THE EM DASH (—)

The em dash is tested on the ACT primarily as an alternative to commas and parentheses for setting off nonessential information, and as an alternative to a colon for introducing information with emphasis.

### The Core Functions

**Function 1: Paired Dashes Setting Off Nonessential Information**

A pair of em dashes functions exactly like a pair of commas, setting off nonessential (extra) information in the middle of a sentence. The information between the dashes can be removed without affecting the grammatical completeness of the sentence.

**Formula:** Sentence Part 1 **—** Nonessential Information **—** Sentence Part 2

> *The CEO**—**who was appointed last year**—**announced the merger.*

> *My brother**—**the one who lives in Boston**—**is visiting us.*

> *The new policy**—**which was implemented in January**—**has already shown results.*

**The "Cross-Out" Test:** If you see paired dashes in an answer choice, you can mentally cross out the words between the dashes and check whether the remaining sentence is grammatically complete. If it is, the dashes are likely correct.

> *The CEO announced the merger.* ✓ (Complete sentence → dashes are correct.)

**Function 2: Single Dash Introducing Information (Like a Colon with Emphasis)**

A single dash at the end of a clause can introduce an explanation, elaboration, or dramatic addition. It functions like a colon but with more emphasis or informality.

**Formula:** Independent Clause **—** Explanation / Elaboration / Dramatic Addition

> *She had one goal**—**to win the championship.*

> *The truth was undeniable**—**they had been wrong all along.*

> *He finally found it**—**the one thing he had been searching for.*

**Function 3: Dash Indicating an Abrupt Change in Thought or Tone**

> *I was going to tell him the truth**—**but then I changed my mind.*

> *The weather was perfect**—**or so we thought.*

### Dash vs. Comma vs. Parentheses

All three can set off nonessential information, but they differ in emphasis:

| Punctuation | Emphasis Level | Effect |
|---|---|---|
| **Parentheses ( )** | Lowest | De-emphasizes the information; treats it as an aside |
| **Commas , ,** | Moderate | Standard; integrates information smoothly into the sentence |
| **Em Dashes — —** | Highest | Emphasizes the information; draws attention to it |

> *The results **(which were unexpected)** surprised everyone.* (De-emphasized — almost like an afterthought.)

> *The results**,** which were unexpected**,** surprised everyone.* (Standard — smoothly integrated.)

> *The results**—**which were unexpected**—**surprised everyone.* (Emphasized — the unexpectedness is highlighted.)

### Dash Rules on the ACT

**1. Never mix and match punctuation for the same function:**

> *The CEO**—**who was appointed last year**,** announced the merger.* ✗ (Dash + comma = mixing.)

> *The CEO**—**who was appointed last year**—**announced the merger.* ✓ (Dash + dash = matching.)

> *The CEO**,** who was appointed last year**,** announced the merger.* ✓ (Comma + comma = matching.)

**2. The ACT will not give you both a correct comma pair and a correct dash pair as options.** You must choose based on context — whether the information is smoothly integrated (commas) or emphasized (dashes).

**3. Never use more than one pair of dashes in a single sentence on the ACT.** If a sentence already has dashes, a second pair would create confusion.

---

## 3.5 THE APOSTROPHE (')

Apostrophe questions appear on nearly every ACT English section, typically 2–4 times. The rules are straightforward, but the ACT uses specific traps.

### Apostrophe Rule 1: Possession

**Singular Nouns:** Add **'s** to form the possessive, regardless of the final letter.

> *the dog**'s** bone*

> *the witness**'s** testimony*

> *James**'s** car*

> *the boss**'s** office*

> *Chris**'s** book*

**Plural Nouns Ending in S:** Add only an apostrophe after the s.

> *the students**'** grades*

> *the teachers**'** lounge*

> *the cars**'** wheels*

> *the Joneses**'** house*

**Plural Nouns NOT Ending in S (Irregular Plurals):** Add **'s**.

> *the children**'s** toys*

> *the women**'s** rights*

> *the people**'s** choice*

> *the mice**'** nest*

**The "Of the" Test for Possession:** To determine whether a noun is possessive, try rephrasing with "of the":

> *the musicians' brains* → *the brains of the musicians* ✓ (Possessive — apostrophe needed.)

> *the musicians performed* → *the performed of the musicians* ✗ (Not possessive — no apostrophe.)

### Apostrophe Rule 2: Contractions

An apostrophe replaces missing letters in contractions.

| Contraction | Full Form |
|---|---|
| it's | it is / it has |
| they're | they are |
| we're | we are |
| you're | you are |
| he's | he is / he has |
| she's | she is / she has |
| who's | who is / who has |
| don't | do not |
| doesn't | does not |
| didn't | did not |
| can't | cannot |
| won't | will not |
| isn't | is not |
| aren't | are not |
| wasn't | was not |
| weren't | were not |
| hasn't | has not |
| haven't | have not |
| hadn't | had not |
| wouldn't | would not |
| shouldn't | should not |
| couldn't | could not |

**The Contraction Expansion Test:** When you see a contraction in an ACT question, expand it to its full form. If the full form makes sense, the contraction is correct. If it doesn't, the contraction is wrong.

> *The dog wagged **it's** tail.* → "The dog wagged **it is** tail." ✗ → Should be **its** (possessive).

> *The dog wagged **its** tail.* ✓ (Possessive — no apostrophe.)

> *They're going to the store.* → "**They are** going to the store." ✓

> *Their going to the store.* ✗ (Should be **they're**.)

### Apostrophe Rule 3: NEVER Use Apostrophes to Form Plurals

This is one of the most common errors in English, and the ACT tests it regularly. **Apostrophes never, ever make a word plural.**

> *The 1990**'s** were a time of change.* ✗ → **The 1990s were a time of change.**

> *The CEO**'s** met yesterday.* ✗ → **The CEOs met yesterday.**

> *She has two PhD**'s**.* ✗ → **She has two PhDs.**

> *The dog wagged **it's** tail.* ✗ → **The dog wagged its tail.**

> *I bought five apple**'s**.* ✗ → **I bought five apples.**

**Exception — Apostrophes to Form Plurals of Single Lowercase Letters:** To avoid confusion, use an apostrophe when forming the plural of single lowercase letters.

> *Mind your p**'**s and q**'**s.* (Without apostrophes, "ps" and "qs" could be misread.)

> *She received all A**'**s this semester.* (Without apostrophe, "As" could be confused with the word "as.")

> *There are two m**'**s in "accommodate."*

**Note:** This exception applies ONLY to single lowercase letters. It does not apply to capital letters, numbers, or words.

### Apostrophe Rule 4: Possessive Pronouns NEVER Take Apostrophes

This is a critical rule that the ACT tests repeatedly. The following possessive pronouns **never** take apostrophes:

| Possessive Pronoun | Common Confusion |
|---|---|
| **its** | it's = it is |
| **theirs** | they're = they are |
| **ours** | our's (never exists) |
| **yours** | your's (never exists) |
| **his** | hi's (never exists) |
| **hers** | her's (never exists) |
| **whose** | who's = who is |

> *The company changed **its** policy.* ✓ (Possessive — no apostrophe.)

> *The company changed **it's** policy.* ✗ (This means "it is policy" — wrong.)

> *The book is **hers**.* ✓ (Possessive — no apostrophe.)

> *The book is **her's**.* ✗ (This word does not exist.)

> ***Whose** book is this?* ✓ (Possessive — no apostrophe.)

> ***Who's** book is this?* ✗ (This means "Who is book is this?" — wrong.)

### Apostrophe Rule 5: Joint vs. Separate Possession

**Joint possession (two people share ownership):** Add **'s** to the last name only.

> *John and Mary**'s** house.* (They share one house.)

> *Smith and Wesson**'s** factory.* (One factory they share.)

**Separate possession (each person owns separately):** Add **'s** to each name.

> *John**'s** and Mary**'s** houses.* (Each has their own house.)

> *Smith**'s** and Wesson**'s** factories.* (Each has their own factory.)

---

## 3.6 QUOTATION MARKS (" ")

Quotation marks are tested less frequently than other punctuation marks on the ACT, but they do appear, and the rules are specific.

### Quotation Mark Rule 1: Direct Quotations

Quotation marks enclose the **exact words** spoken or written by someone.

> *The dentist said, "**You need to floss.**"*

> *"**I will be there**," she promised.*

> *"**To be or not to be**," Shakespeare wrote, "**that is the question.**"*

**Reporting verbs** (said, stated, wrote, declared, announced, claimed, argued, noted, observed) typically introduce direct quotations and are followed by a comma before the opening quotation mark.

> *The president declared, "**We will not give up.**"*

> *She noted, "**The results are promising.**"*

### Quotation Mark Rule 2: Titles of Short Works

Quotation marks enclose the titles of works that are shorter than a full-length book:

| Use Quotation Marks | Do NOT Use Quotation Marks |
|---|---|
| Articles | Books |
| Short stories | Newspapers |
| Poems | Magazines |
| Songs | Novels |
| Chapters | Plays (sometimes italicized) |
| TV episodes | Movies (sometimes italicized) |
| Essays | |

> *"The Lottery" is a short story by Shirley Jackson.* ✓

> *"The Road Not Taken" is a poem by Robert Frost.* ✓

> *"Bohemian Rhapsody" is a song by Queen.* ✓

### Quotation Mark Rule 3: Punctuation Placement with Quotation Marks (American English)

The ACT follows **American English** conventions for punctuation placement with quotation marks:

**Commas and periods** go **inside** quotation marks:

> *"I will be there," she said.*

> *She called it "a masterpiece."*

**Semicolons and colons** go **outside** quotation marks:

> *She described it as "a masterpiece"; however, others disagreed.*

> *He called it "a masterpiece": a word he rarely used.*

**Question marks and exclamation points:**
- If the question mark or exclamation point is **part of the quoted material**, it goes **inside** the quotation marks.
- If the question mark or exclamation point is **not part of the quoted material** (i.e., the whole sentence is a question, but the quoted material is not), it goes **outside** the quotation marks.

> *She asked, "Are you coming?"* (The quote is a question — inside.)

> *Did she really say, "I quit"?* (The whole sentence is a question, but the quote is not — outside.)

> *He shouted, "Watch out!"* (The quote is an exclamation — inside.)

> *I can't believe she said, "I quit"!* (The whole sentence is an exclamation, but the quote is not — outside.)

### Quotation Mark Rule 4: Single Quotation Marks (' ')

Single quotation marks are used for a quotation within a quotation:

> *Maria said, "My mother always told me, 'Be kind to others.'"*

> *The witness testified, "The officer asked, 'Do you know the way?' before driving off."*

---

## 3.7 PUTTING IT ALL TOGETHER: THE PUNCTUATION DECISION TREE

When you encounter a punctuation question on the ACT, use this decision tree to determine the correct answer:

**Step 1: Identify the logic of the sentence.**
- Are the two parts equal in importance? → Consider semicolon or comma + FANBOYS.
- Does the second part explain or introduce something from the first? → Consider colon or dash.
- Is the information extra (nonessential)? → Consider commas, dashes, or parentheses.
- Is there a list? → Consider commas (or semicolons for complex lists).
- Is there possession or contraction? → Consider apostrophe.

**Step 2: Apply the specific rule.**
- Independent clause + independent clause (equal) → semicolon OR comma + FANBOYS
- Independent clause + list/explanation → colon
- Nonessential information → commas, dashes, or parentheses
- Introductory element → comma after the element
- Items in a series → commas (including Oxford comma)

**Step 3: Eliminate wrong answers.**
- Comma splice (two independent clauses joined by only a comma) → always wrong
- Semicolon joining a clause and a phrase → always wrong
- Colon after a non-independent clause → always wrong
- Apostrophe with possessive pronoun → always wrong
- Apostrophe to form a plural → always wrong
- Mixed punctuation (dash + comma for the same function) → always wrong

**Step 4: If multiple answers seem correct, choose the one that best matches the context.**
- If the information is smoothly integrated → commas
- If the information is emphasized → dashes
- If the information is de-emphasized → parentheses
- If the two clauses are very closely related → semicolon
- If the second clause explains the first → colon

---

## 3.8 PUNCTUATION PATTERNS THE ACT LOVES TO TEST

### Pattern 1: The Comma Splice Disguised as a Correct Answer

The ACT frequently presents a comma splice as an answer choice, hoping you will not notice that two independent clauses are joined by only a comma.

> *She was tired, she went to bed.* ✗ (Comma splice.)

> *She was tired, so she went to bed.* ✓ (Comma + FANBOYS.)

> *She was tired; she went to bed.* ✓ (Semicolon.)

> *She was tired. She went to bed.* ✓ (Period.)

### Pattern 2: The Semicolon Used Incorrectly

The ACT frequently presents a semicolon joining an independent clause and a phrase or dependent clause.

> *Although she was tired; she continued working.* ✗ (Should be a comma.)

> *She was exhausted; having worked for twelve hours.* ✗ (Should be a comma or dash.)

> *She was tired; however, she continued working.* ✓ (Semicolon + conjunctive adverb + comma.)

### Pattern 3: The Colon After a Non-Independent Clause

> *The three priorities are: growth, innovation, and sustainability.* ✗ (Remove the colon.)

> *The three priorities are growth, innovation, and sustainability.* ✓

> *The three priorities are as follows: growth, innovation, and sustainability.* ✓

### Pattern 4: The Missing Oxford Comma

> *I bought apples, oranges and bananas.* ✗ (Missing Oxford comma.)

> *I bought apples, oranges, and bananas.* ✓

### Pattern 5: The Unnecessary Comma

> *The box of chocolates, is on the table.* ✗ (Comma between subject and verb.)

> *She said, that she was leaving.* ✗ (Comma before "that.")

> *He ran to the store, and bought milk.* ✗ (Comma between two verbs sharing a subject — unless needed for clarity.)

### Pattern 6: The Apostrophe Error

> *The dog wagged it's tail.* ✗ (Should be **its**.)

> *The student's studied hard.* ✗ (Should be **students** — plural, not possessive.)

> *Who's book is this?* ✗ (Should be **whose**.)

> *The 1990's were a time of change.* ✗ (Should be **1990s**.)

### Pattern 7: The Mixed Punctuation Pair

> *The CEO—who was appointed last year, announced the merger.* ✗ (Dash + comma = mixed.)

> *The CEO—who was appointed last year—announced the merger.* ✓ (Dash + dash = matching.)

> *The CEO, who was appointed last year, announced the merger.* ✓ (Comma + comma = matching.)

### Pattern 8: The Essential vs. Nonessential Clause

> *The book that I borrowed was fascinating.* (Essential — identifies which book; no commas.)

> *The book, which I borrowed, was fascinating.* (Nonessential — adds extra info; commas needed.)

> *My brother who lives in Boston is visiting.* (Essential — I have multiple brothers; identifies which one; no commas.)

> *My brother, who lives in Boston, is visiting.* (Nonessential — I have one brother; extra info; commas needed.)

---

## 3.9 ADVANCED PUNCTUATION CONCEPTS

### The "No-Comma" Preference

The ACT generally prefers clear, direct sentences. If you are unsure whether to use a comma and the meaning of the sentence is clear without one, it is often better to leave it out. "When in doubt, leave it out" is a reliable strategy for comma questions on the ACT.

### The "Matching Punctuation" Rule

When setting off nonessential information, the opening punctuation must match the closing punctuation:

| Opening | Closing | Correct? |
|---|---|---|
| comma | comma | ✓ |
| dash | dash | ✓ |
| parenthesis | parenthesis | ✓ |
| comma | dash | ✗ |
| dash | comma | ✗ |
| comma | parenthesis | ✗ |

### The "Conjunctive Adverb" Trap

The ACT loves to test whether you know that conjunctive adverbs (however, therefore, moreover, etc.) are **not** conjunctions and cannot join two independent clauses by themselves. A comma before a conjunctive adverb joining two independent clauses is always a comma splice.

> *She was tired, however, she continued working.* ✗ (Comma splice.)

> *She was tired; however, she continued working.* ✓ (Semicolon + comma.)

> *She was tired. However, she continued working.* ✓ (Period + comma.)

### The "Due To" Rule

"Due to" functions as an adjective (it modifies a noun). "Because of" functions as an adverb (it modifies a verb). The ACT occasionally tests this distinction.

> *The cancellation was due to rain.* ✓ ("Due to rain" modifies the noun "cancellation.")

> *The game was canceled because of rain.* ✓ ("Because of rain" modifies the verb "was canceled.")

> *He succeeded due to his hard work.* ✗ (Should be "because of" — modifying the verb "succeeded.")

### The "Would Of" Trap

"Would of," "could of," "should of," "might of," and "must of" are **always wrong** on the ACT. The correct forms are "would have," "could have," "should have," "might have," and "must have." (The confusion arises because "would've" sounds like "would of" in speech.)

> *I would of gone if I had known.* ✗ → **I would have gone if I had known.**

> *She could of won the race.* ✗ → **She could have won the race.**

### Double Negatives

Two negatives in one clause are always wrong on the ACT. Words that count as negatives include: *hardly, barely, scarcely, never, neither, nobody, nothing, nowhere, no one.*

> *I hardly never see him.* ✗ (Double negative.)

> *I hardly ever see him.* ✓

> *She doesn't need no help.* ✗ (Double negative.)

> *She doesn't need any help.* ✓

---

## 3.10 SUMMARY TABLE: PUNCTUATION AT A GLANCE

| Mark | Primary Function | Key Rule |
|---|---|---|
| **Comma (,)** | Separates, connects, sets off | Six uses: series, FANBOYS, intro, nonessential, coordinate adjectives, conjunctive adverbs |
| **Semicolon (;)** | Joins two independent clauses | Both sides must be independent clauses; used with conjunctive adverbs |
| **Colon (:)** | Introduces list, explanation, elaboration | Left side must be an independent clause |
| **Em Dash (—)** | Sets off nonessential info (emphasis) or introduces with emphasis | Must match (dash-dash); never mix with commas |
| **Apostrophe (')** | Possession or contraction | Never for plurals; never with possessive pronouns |
| **Quotation Marks (" ")** | Direct speech, short titles, special meaning | Commas/periods inside; semicolons/colons outside |

---

## 3.11 FREQUENCY OF PUNCTUATION QUESTIONS ON THE ACT

| Punctuation Mark | Approximate Frequency | Typical Question Types |
|---|---|---|
| **Comma** | Most frequent (~40-45% of punctuation questions) | Series, FANBOYS, introductory elements, nonessential clauses, coordinate adjectives |
| **Semicolon** | Very frequent (~20-25%) | Joining independent clauses, with conjunctive adverbs, in complex lists |
| **Colon** | Moderate (~10-15%) | Introducing lists, after independent clauses |
| **Dash** | Moderate (~10-15%) | Paired dashes for nonessential info, single dash for emphasis |
| **Apostrophe** | Moderate (~10-15%) | Possession, contractions, common errors (its/it's, whose/who's) |
| **Quotation Marks** | Less frequent (~5-10%) | Direct quotations, titles, punctuation placement |

---

This chapter has provided a comprehensive, exhaustive treatment of every punctuation mark tested on the ACT English section. Mastery of these rules — combined with the ability to identify which rule is being tested by examining the differences among answer choices — is essential for achieving a high score. The punctuation rules are not merely mechanical; they reflect the logic of the sentence. When you understand *why* a particular punctuation mark is correct, you can answer any punctuation question the ACT throws at you.

---


# Chapter 4: Subject-Verb Agreement and Pronoun Rules — Core Formulas and Trap-Avoidance Tactics

---

## 4.0 — Why This Chapter Exists and What It Will Do for Your Score

Subject-verb agreement and pronoun rules together account for roughly **22–27% of all ACT English questions**. That means approximately 1 in every 4 questions you encounter on test day will test whether you can identify the correct subject, match it to the correct verb form, or select the precise pronoun that unambiguously refers to a specific antecedent. Mastering the content in this single chapter can therefore be the difference between a score in the low 20s and a score in the low 30s.

But here is the critical insight most students miss: the ACT does not test these rules in isolation. The test writers deliberately construct sentences that *obscure* the subject, that bury the verb deep within a thicket of modifiers, and that introduce multiple possible antecedents for a single pronoun. The grammar rule itself is simple. The *trap* is in the sentence architecture.

This chapter will teach you both layers. First, you will learn the foundational rules — the non-negotiable formulas that govern subject-verb agreement and pronoun usage. Then, you will learn the specific trap architectures the ACT uses to confuse you, along with step-by-step tactics to dismantle those traps before they cost you points.

By the end of this chapter, you will be able to:

- Identify the true subject of any sentence, no matter how many intervening phrases or clauses separate it from the verb.
- Instantly recognize and correct every major category of subject-verb disagreement.
- Determine pronoun case (subjective, objective, possessive) with mechanical precision.
- Resolve ambiguous pronoun references by applying a systematic elimination protocol.
- Deploy "shortcut hacks" (subtraction, substitution, bracketing) that allow you to answer questions in under 15 seconds.

Let us begin.

---

## 4.1 — The Foundational Architecture of Subject-Verb Agreement

### 4.1.1 — The Core Formula

Every sentence in English has a subject (who or what the sentence is about) and a verb (what the subject does or is). The subject and verb must agree in **number**:

- **Singular subject → Singular verb**
- **Plural subject → Plural verb**

This sounds trivially simple. And at its core, it is. Consider:

- *The dog **runs** fast.* (singular subject "dog" → singular verb "runs")
- *The dogs **run** fast.* (plural subject "dogs" → plural verb "run")

No competent English speaker would ever say "The dog run fast" or "The dogs runs fast." So why does the ACT dedicate so many questions to this concept? Because the test never gives you sentences this simple.

### 4.1.2 — The ACT's Primary Weapon: Intervening Phrases

The ACT separates the subject from the verb with long, distracting phrases designed to make you forget what the subject was. The most common intervening elements are:

1. **Prepositional phrases** (beginning with words like *of, in, on, by, for, with, from, to, between, among, through, during, after, before, around, about, under, over*)
2. **Relative clauses** (beginning with *who, whom, whose, which, that*)
3. **Participial phrases** (beginning with an -ed or -ing verb form)
4. **Appositives** (nouns or noun phrases that rename another noun)
5. **Parenthetical expressions** (set off by commas, dashes, or parentheses)

Consider this ACT-style sentence:

> *The collection of rare stamps and coins from the estate of the late philanthropist **were** donated to the museum.*

What is the subject? If you said "stamps and coins," you fell for the trap. "Stamps and coins" is the object of the preposition "of" — it lives inside a prepositional phrase and therefore **cannot** be the subject. The true subject is "collection," which is singular. The correct verb is "was," not "were":

> *The collection of rare stamps and coins from the estate of the late philanthropist **was** donated to the museum.*

### 4.1.3 — The Bracketing Method (Your Primary Tactical Tool)

To handle any subject-verb agreement question on the ACT, use the **Bracketing Method**:

**Step 1:** Locate the verb in question.

**Step 2:** Ask "Who or what [verbs]?" to find the subject.

**Step 3:** Mentally bracket out (or physically cross out on your test booklet) every word between the subject and the verb that is part of a prepositional phrase, relative clause, participial phrase, appositive, or parenthetical.

**Step 4:** Check whether the remaining subject is singular or plural.

**Step 5:** Confirm the verb matches.

Let us apply this to a more complex example:

> *The analysis of the data collected from the surveys conducted in three different regions **reveal** a significant trend.*

**Step 1:** The verb in question is "reveal."

**Step 2:** What reveals? "Analysis."

**Step 3:** Bracket out the intervening material: "~~of the data collected from the surveys conducted in three different regions~~"

**Step 4:** "Analysis" is singular.

**Step 5:** The verb should be "reveals," not "reveal."

Corrected: *The analysis of the data collected from the surveys conducted in three different regions **reveals** a significant trend.*

This method works on every single subject-verb agreement question the ACT can throw at you. Practice it until it becomes automatic.

### 4.1.4 — The "Prepositional Phrase Never Contains the Subject" Rule

This is one of the most reliable rules on the entire ACT English section: **the subject of a sentence is never found inside a prepositional phrase.**

If you can identify prepositional phrases — and you must, because they appear in roughly 40% of all subject-verb agreement questions — you can eliminate the distractor nouns and find the true subject.

Common prepositions to watch for:

| Category | Prepositions |
|---|---|
| **Location** | in, on, at, under, over, above, below, beside, between, among, behind, in front of, inside, outside, near, by, around, through, throughout, across |
| **Time** | during, before, after, since, until, for, in, on, at |
| **Direction/Movement** | to, from, toward, into, onto, out of, up, down, along, past |
| **Association/Inclusion** | of, with, without, including, except, besides, along with, together with, as well as, in addition to, accompanied by, in spite of, regarding, concerning, despite |

When you see a noun immediately before a verb but that noun is the object of one of these prepositions, it is **not** the subject. Cross it out and look further left.

### 4.1.5 — The "There Is / There Are" Inversion Trap

Sentences beginning with "there is," "there are," "there was," "there were," "there has been," or "there have been" invert the normal subject-verb order. The subject comes **after** the verb.

> *There **is** several reasons for this decision.*

What is the real subject? "Reasons" — which is plural. The correct verb is "are":

> *There **are** several reasons for this decision.*

**Tactic:** When you see "there is/are/was/were" in an underlined portion, flip the sentence mentally to normal order: "Several reasons are there for this decision." Now the agreement is obvious.

The same logic applies to sentences beginning with "here":

> *Here **is** the documents you requested.* → "The documents are here." → *Here **are** the documents you requested.*

---

## 4.2 — Advanced Subject-Verb Agreement Rules

### 4.2.1 — Compound Subjects Joined by "And"

When two or more subjects are joined by "and," the subject is almost always **plural** and requires a plural verb.

> *The teacher and the students **are** in the classroom.*

**Exception:** When the two nouns joined by "and" refer to a single person or thing, the subject is singular.

> *The founder and CEO of the company **is** speaking tonight.* (One person who is both founder and CEO)
> *Macaroni and cheese **is** my favorite dish.* (One dish)

**ACT Tip:** This exception is rare on the test. When in doubt, treat "and" subjects as plural.

### 4.2.2 — Compound Subjects Joined by "Or" / "Nor" (The Proximity Rule)

When subjects are joined by "or" or "nor," the verb agrees with the subject **closest to it**. This is called the **Proximity Rule**.

> *Neither the manager nor the employees **were** satisfied.* (plural "employees" is closer → plural verb)
> *Neither the employees nor the manager **was** satisfied.* (singular "manager" is closer → singular verb)
> *Either the paintings or the sculpture **is** the centerpiece.* (singular "sculpture" is closer → singular verb)
> *Either the sculpture or the paintings **are** the centerpiece.* (plural "paintings" is closer → plural verb)

**Tactic:** When you see "or" or "nor," ignore the first subject entirely. Look only at the subject nearest the verb and match the verb to that one.

### 4.2.3 — Indefinite Pronouns: The Singular/Plural/Variable Classification

Indefinite pronouns are pronouns that do not refer to a specific person or thing. The ACT tests them relentlessly because they are a common source of agreement errors. You must memorize which are singular, which are plural, and which are variable.

**Always Singular (require singular verbs):**

| Pronoun | Example |
|---|---|
| everyone | Everyone **is** here. |
| everybody | Everybody **has** arrived. |
| everything | Everything **was** destroyed. |
| each | Each of the students **has** a book. |
| either | Either of the options **is** acceptable. |
| neither | Neither of the candidates **was** qualified. |
| anyone | Anyone **is** welcome. |
| anybody | Nobody **was** injured. |
| anything | Anything **is** possible. |
| no one | No one **has** responded. |
| nobody | Nobody **knows** the truth. |
| nothing | Nothing **matters** anymore. |
| someone | Someone **is** at the door. |
| somebody | Somebody **left** a message. |
| something | Something **needs** to change. |

**Critical note about "each" and "every":** Even when "each" or "every" is followed by a plural noun (as in "each of the students"), the pronoun itself is singular. The prepositional phrase "of the students" does not change the number of the subject.

> *Each of the participants **has** received a certificate.* (not "have")
> *Every one of the paintings **was** sold.* (not "were")

**Always Plural (require plural verbs):**

| Pronoun | Example |
|---|---|
| both | Both **are** correct. |
| few | Few **understand** the concept. |
| many | Many **have** tried and failed. |
| several | Several **were** selected. |

**Variable (depends on the object of the preposition that follows):**

| Pronoun | Rule | Example |
|---|---|---|
| all | Singular if the "of" object is singular; plural if the object is plural | All of the cake **is** gone. / All of the cakes **are** gone. |
| any | Same logic | Any of the information **is** useful. / Any of the books **are** available. |
| most | Same logic | Most of the water **has** evaporated. / Most of the students **have** passed. |
| none | Same logic (traditionally singular, but the ACT accepts plural when the "of" object is plural) | None of the milk **is** left. / None of the answers **are** correct. |
| some | Same logic | Some of the money **was** stolen. / Some of the coins **were** stolen. |

**ACT Strategy for "None":** The ACT has historically treated "none" as singular, but in recent years, it has accepted plural usage when the context clearly refers to a plural noun. If you encounter a "none" question, check the object of the preposition. If it is plural and the answer choices include both singular and plural verbs, the plural verb is usually correct.

### 4.2.4 — Collective Nouns: Singular in American English

Collective nouns refer to a group of people or things as a single unit. In American English (which the ACT follows), collective nouns are **singular** and take singular verbs.

Common collective nouns:

| Noun | Example |
|---|---|
| team | The team **is** winning. |
| group | The group **meets** every Tuesday. |
| family | The family **has** a long history. |
| committee | The committee **votes** tomorrow. |
| audience | The audience **was** captivated. |
| class | The class **is** taking a test. |
| jury | The jury **has** reached a verdict. |
| government | The government **is** considering new legislation. |
| company | The company **employs** 500 people. |
| staff | The staff **is** well-trained. |
| crowd | The crowd **was** growing restless. |
| orchestra | The orchestra **performs** tonight. |
| band | The band **plays** at 8 PM. |
| council | The council **meets** monthly. |
| board | The board **approves** the budget. |
| faculty | The faculty **is** divided on the issue. |
| majority | The majority **supports** the measure. |
| minority | A minority **opposes** the change. |
| public | The public **is** concerned. |
| generation | This generation **faces** unique challenges. |

**Important nuance:** If the members of the collective are acting as individuals rather than as a unit, British English sometimes uses a plural verb. However, the ACT follows American English conventions and will always treat these as singular. Do not overthink this.

### 4.2.5 — "The Number of" vs. "A Number of"

This is a classic ACT distinction:

- **"The number of"** → **singular** (refers to the number itself as a quantity)
  > *The number of applicants **has** increased this year.*

- **"A number of"** → **plural** (means "several" or "many")
  > *A number of applicants **have** already been rejected.*

**Memory trick:** "The number" = one specific number = singular. "A number" = many = plural.

### 4.2.6 — Phrases Like "Along With," "As Well As," "In Addition To," "Together With," "Accompanied By"

These phrases do **not** create compound subjects. They are essentially prepositional phrases in disguise. The verb agrees with the main subject, not the noun in the trailing phrase.

> *The president, along with the board members, **is** attending the conference.* (not "are")
> *The students, as well as the teacher, **were** excited about the field trip.* (Wait — "were"? No. The subject is "students," which is plural, so "were" is correct. But if the subject were singular: *The teacher, as well as the students, **was** excited.*)

**Tactic:** Mentally delete everything after the comma (or between the commas) that follows these phrases. The remaining subject determines the verb.

### 4.2.7 — Titles, Works of Art, Company Names, and Words Referred to as Words

These are always singular, even if they contain plural words:

> *"War and Peace" **is** a long novel.* (title of a book)
> *The New York Times **is** a respected newspaper.* (name of a publication)
> *General Motors **employs** thousands of workers.* (company name)
> *"Children" **is** a plural noun.* (word referred to as a word)

### 4.2.8 — Relative Clauses: The Verb Inside Agrees with the Relative Pronoun's Antecedent

When a relative clause (introduced by *who, which, that*) contains a verb, that verb must agree with the noun the relative pronoun refers to:

> *She is one of the students who **have** passed the exam.* (Who passed? "Students" — plural → "have")
> *She is the only one of the students who **has** passed the exam.* (Who passed? "One" — singular → "has")

**The "Only One Of" Trap:** This is one of the most frequently tested constructions on the ACT. The key is whether "only" is present:

- **"One of the [plural noun] who [plural verb]"** — The relative clause modifies the plural noun.
- **"The only one of the [plural noun] who [singular verb]"** — The relative clause modifies "one."

**Tactic:** Look for the word "only." If it is present, the verb in the relative clause is singular. If it is absent, the verb is plural.

### 4.2.9 — Fractions, Percentages, and Portions

When a fraction or percentage is followed by "of + a noun," the verb agrees with that noun:

> *Half of the cake **is** gone.* (singular "cake" → singular verb)
> *Half of the cookies **are** gone.* (plural "cookies" → plural verb)
> *Fifty percent of the budget **has** been spent.* (singular "budget" → singular verb)
> *Fifty percent of the employees **have** resigned.* (plural "employees" → plural verb)

### 4.2.10 — "Every" and "Many a" Before a Singular Noun

- **"Every"** before a compound subject (even if joined by "and") is singular:
  > *Every man and woman **has** the right to vote.*

- **"Many a"** before a singular noun is singular:
  > *Many a student **has** struggled with this concept.*

---

## 4.3 — Subject-Verb Agreement: The Complete Trap Taxonomy

Now that you know the rules, let us catalog every specific trap the ACT uses to test them. For each trap, you will see the structure, a concrete example, and the counter-tactic.

### Trap #1: The Long Prepositional Phrase Bridge

**Structure:** [Singular Subject] + [Long Prepositional Phrase with Plural Object] + [Plural Verb]

**Example:** *The box of chocolates **are** on the table.*

**Why students miss it:** The plural noun "chocolates" is right next to the verb, creating a false sense of agreement.

**Counter-tactic:** Bracket out the prepositional phrase. "The box ~~of chocolates~~ is on the table." The subject is "box" — singular.

### Trap #2: The Relative Clause Interrupter

**Structure:** [Subject] + [Relative Clause with its own Subject and Verb] + [Main Verb]

**Example:** *The scientists who **studies** climate change **publishes** their findings regularly.*

**Why students miss it:** The relative clause "who studies climate change" has its own subject-verb pair, and students may incorrectly match the main verb to the nearest noun.

**Counter-tactic:** Identify the relative clause and treat it as a separate unit. "Who" refers to "scientists" (plural), so the verb inside the relative clause should be "study." The main verb "publishes" should be "publish" to agree with "scientists."

### Trap #3: The Inverted Sentence

**Structure:** There/Here + [Verb] + [Real Subject]

**Example:** *There **was** three cats in the alley.*

**Why students miss it:** Students match the verb to "there" (which has no number) or to the nearest noun without checking if it is the real subject.

**Counter-tactic:** Flip to normal order: "Three cats were in the alley." The subject is "cats" — plural → "were."

### Trap #4: The "Each of the [Plural]" Construction

**Structure:** Each/Every/Either/Neither + of + [Plural Noun] + [Plural Verb]

**Example:** *Each of the students **have** completed the assignment.*

**Why students miss it:** The plural noun "students" is right next to the verb.

**Counter-tactic:** "Each" is the subject. "Each" is always singular. The correct verb is "has."

### Trap #5: The "One of the [Plural] Who" Construction

**Structure:** One of + [Plural Noun] + who/that + [Singular Verb]

**Example:** *She is one of the few engineers who **has** expertise in quantum computing.*

**Why students miss it:** Students assume "who" refers to "one" (singular) rather than "engineers" (plural).

**Counter-tactic:** Ask: Who has expertise? The answer is "the few engineers" — plural. The verb should be "has" only if "the only one" is specified. Without "only," use the plural verb: "who **have** expertise."

### Trap #6: The Collective Noun Disguise

**Structure:** [Collective Noun] + [Prepositional Phrase with Plural Noun] + [Plural Verb]

**Example:** *The team of researchers **are** presenting their findings.*

**Why students miss it:** The plural noun "researchers" tricks students into using a plural verb.

**Counter-tactic:** The subject is "team" — a collective noun that is singular in American English. The correct verb is "is."

### Trap #7: The "Neither...Nor" Proximity Reversal

**Structure:** Neither + [Singular Noun] + nor + [Plural Noun] + [Singular Verb]

**Example:** *Neither the manager nor the employees **was** informed of the change.*

**Why students miss it:** Students may match the verb to the first noun ("manager") instead of the nearest one ("employees").

**Counter-tactic:** Apply the Proximity Rule. The verb agrees with the nearest subject: "employees" (plural) → "were."

### Trap #8: The Appositive Mimic

**Structure:** [Singular Subject] + , + [Plural Appositive] + , + [Plural Verb]

**Example:** *The CEO, the highest-paid executives in the company, **are** meeting today.*

**Why students miss it:** The appositive "the highest-paid executives" is plural and sits right next to the verb.

**Counter-tactic:** Appositives between commas are non-essential. Remove them: "The CEO ~~, the highest-paid executives in the company,~~ is meeting today." The subject is "CEO" — singular.

### Trap #9: The "More Than One" Idiom

**Structure:** More than one + [Singular Noun] + [Plural Verb]

**Example:** *More than one student **have** complained about the policy.*

**Why students miss it:** Logically, "more than one" implies plurality.

**Counter-tactic:** Despite the logical plurality, the phrase "more than one" takes a singular verb in formal English: "More than one student **has** complained." This is an idiom — memorize it.

### Trap #10: The Distance Subject

**Structure:** [Subject] + [Very Long Modifier] + [Verb far from Subject]

**Example:** *The impact of the new regulations on small businesses, particularly those in rural areas with limited access to capital and technical expertise, **are** expected to be significant.*

**Why students miss it:** The subject "impact" is separated from the verb "are" by over 20 words.

**Counter-tactic:** Bracket out everything between the subject and the verb. "The impact ~~of the new regulations on small businesses, particularly those in rural areas with limited access to capital and technical expertise~~ is expected to be significant."

---

## 4.4 — Pronoun Rules: The Foundational Framework

### 4.4.1 — What Is a Pronoun?

A pronoun is a word that takes the place of a noun (called its **antecedent**) to avoid repetition. For example, instead of saying "John said that John was tired," we say "John said that **he** was tired." Here, "he" is the pronoun and "John" is the antecedent.

The ACT tests pronouns in four major ways:

1. **Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement** (number, person, gender)
2. **Pronoun Case** (subjective, objective, possessive)
3. **Ambiguous Pronoun Reference** (unclear antecedent)
4. **Pronoun Consistency** (shifts in point of view)

We will address each in exhaustive detail.

### 4.4.2 — Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement

A pronoun must agree with its antecedent in three dimensions:

**Number:** Singular antecedent → singular pronoun; plural antecedent → plural pronoun.

> *The student turned in **her** assignment.* (singular → singular)
> *The students turned in **their** assignments.* (plural → plural)

**Person:** First person (I, we), second person (you), third person (he, she, it, they). The pronoun must match the person of the antecedent.

> *If a person wants to succeed, **they** must work hard.* (This is technically a person shift — "a person" is third person, "they" is third person, but the number is the issue. See below.)

**Gender:** Masculine, feminine, or neuter. The pronoun must match the gender of the antecedent when applicable.

> *James brought **his** laptop.* (masculine)
> *Maria brought **her** laptop.* (feminine)
> *The company released **its** earnings report.* (neuter)

### 4.4.3 — The Singular "They" and the ACT's Position

In informal English, "they" is commonly used as a singular pronoun to refer to a person of unspecified gender:

> *Someone left **their** umbrella in the classroom.*

However, the ACT generally considers this usage incorrect in formal writing. The test prefers:

> *Someone left **his or her** umbrella in the classroom.*

**ACT Rule:** When the antecedent is a singular indefinite pronoun (*everyone, everybody, someone, anyone, no one, nobody, each, either, neither*), the ACT expects a singular pronoun. "His or her" is the standard fix, though in some cases, the sentence may be rewritten to avoid the issue entirely.

**Exception:** If the answer choices do not include "his or her" and the only options are "his," "her," or "their," the ACT may accept "their" in certain contexts. But this is rare. When in doubt, choose the singular option.

### 4.4.4 — Pronoun Case: The Three Cases

Pronouns change form depending on their function in a sentence. There are three cases:

**Subjective (Nominative) Case** — Used when the pronoun is the subject of a verb or a subject complement (after a linking verb):

| Singular | Plural |
|---|---|
| I | we |
| he | they |
| she | they |
| it | they |
| who | who |

> ***She** and I went to the store.* (subjects)
> *It was **he** who called.* (subject complement after linking verb "was")

**Objective (Accusative) Case** — Used when the pronoun is the direct object, indirect object, or object of a preposition:

| Singular | Plural |
|---|---|
| me | us |
| him | them |
| her | them |
| it | them |
| whom | whom |

> *The teacher praised **him**.* (direct object)
> *She gave **me** the book.* (indirect object)
> *Between you and **me**, this is a bad idea.* (object of preposition)

**Possessive (Genitive) Case** — Used to show ownership:

| Singular | Plural |
|---|---|
| my / mine | our / ours |
| his | their / theirs |
| her / hers | their / theirs |
| its | their / theirs |
| whose | whose |

> *That is **my** car.* (possessive adjective modifying a noun)
> *That car is **mine.*** (possessive pronoun standing alone)

### 4.4.5 — The "Remove the Other Person" Hack for Pronoun Case

This is the single most useful hack for pronoun case questions on the ACT. When you see a compound construction (two pronouns, or a pronoun and a noun, joined by "and" or "or"), remove the other person and test the pronoun alone:

**Example:** *The award was given to (Maria and) ___ .*

Remove "Maria and": *The award was given to ___ .* → "me" sounds right, not "I." → *The award was given to Maria and **me**.*

**Example:** *(My brother and) ___ went to the store.*

Remove "My brother and": *___ went to the store.* → "I" sounds right, not "me." → ***My brother and I** went to the store.*

**Example:** *The secret remains between you and (John) ___ .*

Remove "John": *The secret remains between you and ___ .* → "me" sounds right. → *The secret remains between you and **me**.*

**Politeness Rule:** When referring to yourself and another person, always mention the other person first: "Maria and I," "you and he," "the teacher and she." Never "I and Maria" or "me and him."

### 4.4.6 — Who vs. Whom: The Complete Decision Protocol

"Who" and "whom" are among the most commonly tested pronouns on the ACT. The distinction is simple in principle but requires a reliable method for application.

**The Rule:**
- **Who** = subject pronoun (functions like *he, she, they*)
- **Whom** = object pronoun (functions like *him, her, them*)

**The Substitution Test:**
Replace "who/whom" with "he" or "him." If "he" works, use "who." If "him" works, use "whom."

> *"___ did you call?"* → "Did you call **him**?" → "**Whom** did you call?"
> *"___ called you?"* → "**He** called you?" → "**Who** called you?"

**The Next-Word Trick:**
- "Who" is always followed by a **verb** (because it is the subject of that verb).
- "Whom" is always followed by a **noun or pronoun** (because it is the object, and the next word is the subject).

> *"___ **is** coming?"* → "who" (followed by verb "is")
> *"___ **the teacher** praised"* → "whom" (followed by noun "the teacher")

**ACT Tip:** "Whom" is the correct answer on the ACT less often than students expect. If you are genuinely unsure, "who" is the safer guess — but always apply the substitution test first.

### 4.4.7 — Ambiguous Pronoun Reference

A pronoun is ambiguous when it could refer to more than one antecedent. The ACT considers this a serious error and tests it frequently.

**Example of ambiguity:**

> *John told Mark that **he** was wrong.*

Who is wrong? John or Mark? The pronoun "he" could refer to either.

**Fixes:**
1. Repeat the noun: *John told Mark, "**You** were wrong."* or *John told Mark that **John** was wrong.*
2. Use direct quotation: *John told Mark, "I was wrong."*
3. Restructure the sentence: *John admitted to Mark that he himself was wrong.*

**ACT Strategy:** If an answer choice contains a pronoun and you can identify more than one possible antecedent, that answer is wrong. The correct answer will either repeat the noun or restructure the sentence to eliminate ambiguity.

**Common ambiguous pronouns on the ACT:**
- *it, they, this, that, which, he, she*

**Note on "this" and "that":** The ACT frequently tests vague uses of "this" and "that" without a clear referent. For example:

> *The company invested heavily in new technology. **This** led to increased profits.*

What does "this" refer to? The investment? The technology? The ACT prefers specificity:

> *The company's **investment in new technology** led to increased profits.*

### 4.4.8 — Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement with Indefinite Pronouns

As discussed in the subject-verb agreement section, indefinite pronouns like *everyone, everybody, each, either, neither, no one, nobody, someone, somebody* are singular. The pronouns that refer to them must also be singular.

> *Everyone should bring **his or her** own lunch.* (not "their")
> *Each of the students submitted **his or her** essay on time.* (not "their")
> *Neither of the candidates presented **his or her** platform clearly.* (not "their")

**ACT Note:** The test will sometimes offer answer choices that include "their" as a singular pronoun. Do not select it. The ACT maintains the traditional rule that "their" is plural and cannot refer to a singular antecedent in formal writing.

### 4.4.9 — Pronoun Consistency (Avoiding Shifts)

Once a passage establishes a point of view, it must maintain that point of view consistently. The ACT penalizes shifts between:

- **First person** (I, we) ↔ **Second person** (you) ↔ **Third person** (he, she, it, they, one)

**Incorrect shift:**

> *If **one** wants to succeed, **you** must work hard.* (shifts from "one" to "you")

**Corrected:**

> *If **one** wants to succeed, **one** must work hard.*
> *If **you** want to succeed, **you** must work hard.*

**ACT Strategy:** When you see a pronoun underlined, check the surrounding sentences for the established point of view. If the passage uses "one," the underlined pronoun should also be "one" (or "he or she," depending on context). If the passage uses "you," maintain "you."

### 4.4.10 — Reflexive Pronouns: Proper Usage

Reflexive pronouns end in *-self* (singular) or *-selves* (plural):

| Singular | Plural |
|---|---|
| myself | ourselves |
| yourself | yourselves |
| himself | themselves |
| herself | themselves |
| itself | themselves |

**Rule:** A reflexive pronoun is used only when the subject and the object of the sentence refer to the same person or thing:

> *I hurt **myself**.* (I = subject, myself = object — same person)
> *She prepared **herself** for the interview.* (She = subject, herself = object — same person)
> *The cat cleaned **itself**.* (cat = subject, itself = object — same thing)

**Common ACT Error:** Using a reflexive pronoun when a simple personal pronoun is correct:

> *Please send the report to **myself**.* → **Incorrect.** The subject is implied "you" and the object is "me" — they are not the same. → *Please send the report to **me**.*
> *The gift was given to **himself**.* → **Incorrect.** The subject is implied "someone" and the object is "him" — they are not the same. → *The gift was given to **him**.*

**ACT Tip:** If the reflexive pronoun is not reflecting back on the subject of the sentence, it is wrong. Replace it with the appropriate personal pronoun.

### 4.4.11 — Possessive Pronouns vs. Contractions

This is one of the most frequently tested distinctions on the entire ACT English section. You must be able to instantly distinguish possessive pronouns from their contraction homophones:

| Possessive Pronoun (NO apostrophe) | Contraction (HAS apostrophe) | Expands To |
|---|---|---|
| its | it's | it is / it has |
| their | they're | they are |
| whose | who's | who is / who has |
| your | you're | you are |
| theirs | — | — |
| ours | — | — |
| hers | — | — |
| mine | — | — |

**The "Expansion Test":** If you can replace the word with the full contraction and the sentence still makes sense, use the contraction (with apostrophe). If not, use the possessive pronoun (without apostrophe).

> *The dog wagged (its/it's) tail.* → "The dog wagged **it is** tail"? No. → *The dog wagged **its** tail.*
> *(Its/It's) raining outside.* → "**It is** raining outside"? Yes. → ***It's** raining outside.*
> *(Their/They're/There) going to the park.* → "**They are** going to the park"? Yes. → ***They're** going to the park.*
> *(Whose/Who's) jacket is this?* → "**Who is** jacket is this"? No. → ***Whose** jacket is this?*
> *(Who's/Whose) at the door?* → "**Who is** at the door"? Yes. → ***Who's** at the door?*

**Critical Rule:** Possessive pronouns **never** take apostrophes. "It's" with an apostrophe is always a contraction, never a possessive. This is one of the most common errors in English writing, and the ACT tests it on virtually every test administration.

---

## 4.5 — Pronoun Rules: The Complete Trap Taxonomy

### Trap #1: The Ambiguous "It"

**Structure:** A sentence with multiple possible antecedents for "it."

**Example:** *The committee reviewed the proposal and the budget, but it was ultimately rejected.*

What was rejected? The proposal? The budget? Both?

**Counter-tactic:** Replace "it" with the specific noun: *...but the **proposal** was ultimately rejected.* If the context does not make it clear which noun "it" refers to, the pronoun is ambiguous and the answer is wrong.

### Trap #2: The Missing Antecedent

**Structure:** A pronoun appears with no clear antecedent at all.

**Example:** *In the manual, **it** explains how to assemble the furniture.*

What does "it" refer to? "The manual" is the object of the preposition "in," not the subject. There is no grammatical antecedent for "it."

**Counter-tactic:** Replace the pronoun with a specific noun or restructure: *The manual explains how to assemble the furniture.*

### Trap #3: The "Each Other" vs. "One Another" Distinction

**Rule:** Use "each other" when referring to **two** people or things. Use "one another" when referring to **three or more**.

> *The two sisters supported **each other**.* (two)
> *The members of the team encouraged **one another**.* (more than two)

**ACT Note:** This distinction is tested less frequently than it used to be, but it still appears occasionally. If the ACT tests it, the context will make the number clear.

### Trap #4: The Compound Subject/Object Pronoun Error

**Structure:** A pronoun in a compound construction is in the wrong case.

**Example:** *The manager promoted (John and) ___ .*

**Why students miss it:** "John and I" sounds formal and correct, so students default to "I" even when the pronoun is an object.

**Counter-tactic:** Remove "John and": *The manager promoted ___ .* → "me" → *The manager promoted John and **me**.*

### Trap #5: The "Who/Whom" Preposition Trap

**Structure:** A preposition immediately precedes the pronoun, requiring "whom."

**Example:** *The person to ___ I spoke was very helpful.*

**Why students miss it:** Students see "who" and assume it is correct because it is a subject pronoun. But the preposition "to" requires an object.

**Counter-tactic:** The preposition "to" needs an object. "Whom" is the object form. → *The person to **whom** I spoke was very helpful.*

**Alternative test:** "I spoke to **him**" → "him" = "whom" → "to whom."

### Trap #6: The "Than" Comparison Pronoun Error

**Structure:** A pronoun after "than" in a comparison is in the wrong case.

**Example:** *She is taller than ___ .*

**Why students miss it:** In formal English, "than" can function as a conjunction, and the pronoun that follows should be in the case it would be if the clause were completed.

**Counter-tactic:** Complete the clause: "She is taller than **I** [am]." → "I" is correct (subjective case). However, in informal English, "than me" is also acceptable. The ACT generally accepts both, but if the choice is between "than I" and "than me" and the sentence is formal, "than I" is preferred.

### Trap #7: The "Its/It's" Apostrophe Trap

**Structure:** A possessive pronoun is incorrectly written with an apostrophe.

**Example:** *The company released **it's** annual report.*

**Why students miss it:** Students associate apostrophes with possession (e.g., "the dog's bone") and incorrectly apply this to "its."

**Counter-tactic:** Apply the Expansion Test: "The company released **it is** annual report"? No. → *The company released **its** annual report.*

### Trap #8: The "Their/There/They're" Homophone Trap

**Structure:** One of the three homophones is used incorrectly.

**Example:** *The students left **there** books on the table.*

**Why students miss it:** The words sound identical, and students rely on sound rather than meaning.

**Counter-tactic:** Determine the function:
- **Their** = possessive (their books = the books belonging to them)
- **There** = location or existential (there are books / put them there)
- **They're** = contraction of "they are" (they're leaving = they are leaving)

*The students left **their** books on the table.* (possessive → "their")

### Trap #9: The "Who's/Whose" Apostrophe Trap

**Structure:** "Who's" (contraction) is used where "whose" (possessive) is needed, or vice versa.

**Example:** *The student **who's** essay won the prize is in my class.*

**Why students miss it:** Students assume the apostrophe indicates possession.

**Counter-tactic:** Apply the Expansion Test: "The student **who is** essay won the prize"? No. → *The student **whose** essay won the prize is in my class.*

### Trap #10: The Reflexive Pronoun Substitution

**Structure:** A reflexive pronoun is used where a simple personal pronoun is needed.

**Example:** *The manager gave the assignment to (my colleague and) **myself**.*

**Why students miss it:** "Myself" sounds more formal and polite than "me," so students use it to avoid seeming too direct.

**Counter-tactic:** Check if the subject and object are the same. The subject is "the manager" and the object is "me" — they are different. → *The manager gave the assignment to my colleague and **me**.*

---

## 4.6 — Worked Example: Applying Both Skill Sets Simultaneously

On the ACT, subject-verb agreement and pronoun questions often appear in the same passage and sometimes in the same sentence. Consider this example that combines multiple traps:

> *Each of the employees who **were** hired last year **have** submitted **their** reports, but neither the manager nor the department heads **has** reviewed **them** yet.*

Let us analyze each underlined portion:

1. **"were"** — The relative clause "who were hired last year" modifies "employees" (plural), so "were" is correct. (If it said "the only one of the employees who was hired," then "was" would be correct.)

2. **"have"** — The subject is "Each" (singular), not "employees." The correct verb is "has": *Each of the employees... **has** submitted...*

3. **"their"** — The antecedent is "Each" (singular). The ACT requires a singular pronoun: *Each... has submitted **his or her** report.* (Note: "report" should also be singular to match "his or her.")

4. **"has"** — The subjects are joined by "nor": "neither the manager nor the department heads." The nearest subject is "department heads" (plural), so the verb should be "have": *...neither the manager nor the department heads **have** reviewed...*

5. **"them"** — The antecedent is "reports" (plural). "Them" is correct.

This example illustrates why you must analyze each underlined portion independently, even when they appear in the same sentence. The ACT does not test one rule per sentence — it tests multiple rules simultaneously.

---

## 4.7 — The "Odd One Out" Strategy for Subject-Verb Agreement Questions

When all four answer choices are different verb forms (e.g., *is, are, was, were*), you can use the **"Odd One Out"** strategy:

1. Identify the subject by bracketing out intervening phrases.
2. Determine whether the subject is singular or plural.
3. Look at the answer choices. Usually, three choices will be one number (e.g., three singular verbs) and one will be the other (e.g., one plural verb), or vice versa.
