4. The "odd one out" is usually the correct answer — because the ACT includes the wrong number as a trap and the right number as the correct answer, with two other distractors.

**Example:** If the subject is singular and the choices are:
- A) is
- B) are
- C) was
- D) were

Three choices are present tense (A, B) and two are past tense (C, D). But more importantly, two are singular (A, C) and two are plural (B, D). If the subject is singular, the answer is either A or C. Now you need to determine the correct tense from context.

**The "They/She" Substitution Trick:** To determine whether the subject is singular or plural, substitute "she" or "they" for the subject and see which sounds correct:

> *The box of chocolates...* → "She is on the table"? No. "It is on the table"? Yes. → Singular.
> *The boxes of chocolate...* → "They are on the table"? Yes. → Plural.

If both "she" and "they" sound acceptable, the question is testing verb tense, not subject-verb agreement.

---

## 4.8 — The "Sandwich Skip" Hack for Pronoun Questions

When a pronoun is underlined and the sentence contains a "comma sandwich" (a non-essential clause or appositive set off by commas), skip the sandwiched material and work backward from the pronoun to find its antecedent.

**Example:**

> *The CEO, **who** had been with the company for over two decades, announced **her** retirement.*

To find the antecedent of "her," skip the sandwiched clause "who had been with the company for over two decades" and look at the main clause: "The CEO announced ___ retirement." The antecedent is "CEO." If the CEO is female, "her" is correct. If the CEO's gender is unspecified, "his or her" would be needed.

**Why this works:** Non-essential clauses (set off by commas) do not affect the grammatical structure of the main clause. The pronoun's antecedent is always in the main clause, not in the sandwiched material.

---

## 4.9 — Special Pronoun Constructions

### 4.9.1 — "One" as a Pronoun

When "one" is used as a pronoun (meaning "a person" or "any person"), it must be used consistently throughout the sentence:

> *If **one** studies diligently, **one** will succeed.* (correct — consistent)
> *If **one** studies diligently, **you** will succeed.* (incorrect — shift from "one" to "you")

**ACT Note:** The pronoun "one" is formal and appears primarily in the Argument/Persuasive and Humanities passages. If the passage uses "one," maintain it.

### 4.9.2 — "That" vs. "Which" (Revisited from a Pronoun Perspective)

While this distinction is covered in detail in the punctuation chapter, it is worth noting here from a pronoun standpoint:

- **"That"** introduces essential (restrictive) clauses — information necessary to identify the noun.
- **"Which"** introduces non-essential (nonrestrictive) clauses — extra information set off by commas.

> *The book **that** I borrowed was fascinating.* (Which book? The one I borrowed. Essential — no commas.)
> *The book, **which** I borrowed from the library, was fascinating.* (Extra information — commas required.)

**ACT Tip:** If you see "that" and "which" as answer choices, check for commas. If there is a comma before the blank, use "which." If there is no comma, use "that."

### 4.9.3 — "Who" vs. "That" for People

- Use **"who"** (or "whom") when referring to people.
- Use **"that"** or **"which"** when referring to things or animals.

> *The scientist **who** discovered the vaccine was awarded a Nobel Prize.* (person → "who")
> *The theory **that** she proposed revolutionized the field.* (thing → "that")

**ACT Exception:** "That" can refer to people in essential clauses, but "who" is always preferred for people on the ACT. If both "who" and "that" are options and the antecedent is a person, "who" is almost always correct.

### 4.9.4 — "Where" and "When" as Relative Pronouns

- **"Where"** refers to places.
- **"When"** refers to times.

> *This is the town **where** I grew up.* (place)
> *I remember the day **when** we first met.* (time)

**ACT Note:** "Where" should not be used to refer to a book, case, or situation in formal writing. Use "in which" instead:

> *The case **in which** the court ruled...* (not "the case where the court ruled")

However, the ACT does not always enforce this distinction strictly. If "where" is the only option that makes sense in context, it may be accepted.

---

## 4.10 — The "Denial/Confirmation" Protocol for Pronoun Questions

When you are unsure about a pronoun question, use the **Denial/Confirmation Protocol**:

1. **Identify the pronoun's claimed antecedent** (the noun the test writer wants you to think it refers to).
2. **Check if the pronoun could logically refer to any other noun** in the preceding context.
3. **If another noun is a possible antecedent**, the pronoun is ambiguous → the answer is wrong.
4. **If only one noun is a possible antecedent**, confirm that the pronoun agrees with it in number, person, and gender.
5. **If the pronoun agrees with its antecedent and no ambiguity exists**, the answer is correct.

**Example:**

> *The committee reviewed the proposal and the budget, but **it** was ultimately rejected.*

**Step 1:** The claimed antecedent is "proposal" (the nearest noun).
**Step 2:** Could "it" also refer to "budget"? Yes. Could "it" refer to "committee"? No (committee is a collective noun, but "it" could theoretically refer to it).
**Step 3:** Multiple possible antecedents → ambiguous → the answer is wrong.
**Step 4:** The correct answer must specify which noun was rejected.

---

## 4.11 — Summary: The Master Decision Tree for Subject-Verb Agreement and Pronoun Questions

When you encounter a question testing subject-verb agreement or pronoun rules, follow this decision tree:

### For Subject-Verb Agreement:

```
START
│
├─ Is the verb underlined?
│  │
│  ├─ YES → Find the subject.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is there a prepositional phrase between subject and verb?
│  │  │  ├─ YES → Bracket it out. The noun inside is NOT the subject.
│  │  │  └─ NO → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the sentence inverted (starts with "there" or "here")?
│  │  │  ├─ YES → The real subject comes AFTER the verb. Flip the sentence.
│  │  │  └─ NO → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the subject joined by "and"?
│  │  │  ├─ YES → Usually plural (unless it refers to one person/thing).
│  │  │  └─ NO → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the subject joined by "or" or "nor"?
│  │  │  ├─ YES → Apply the Proximity Rule (match the nearest subject).
│  │  │  └─ NO → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the subject an indefinite pronoun (everyone, each, etc.)?
│  │  │  ├─ YES → Singular verb.
│  │  │  └─ NO → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the subject a collective noun (team, group, etc.)?
│  │  │  ├─ YES → Singular verb (American English).
│  │  │  └─ NO → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  └─ Match the verb to the subject's number.
│  │
│  └─ NO → This is not a subject-verb agreement question. Move to pronoun analysis.
```

### For Pronoun Questions:

```
START
│
├─ Is a pronoun underlined?
│  │
│  ├─ YES → Identify the antecedent.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the antecedent clear and unambiguous?
│  │  │  ├─ NO → The pronoun is wrong. Choose the answer that specifies the noun.
│  │  │  └─ YES → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Does the pronoun agree with the antecedent in number?
│  │  │  ├─ NO → Choose the pronoun that matches the antecedent's number.
│  │  │  └─ YES → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the pronoun in the correct case (subjective/objective/possessive)?
│  │  │  ├─ NO → Choose the correct case.
│  │  │  │  ├─ Compound construction? → Remove the other person and test.
│  │  │  │  ├─ Who/whom? → Substitute he/him.
│  │  │  │  └─ Possessive? → No apostrophe for possessive pronouns.
│  │  │  └─ YES → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  ├─ Is the pronoun consistent with the passage's point of view?
│  │  │  ├─ NO → Choose the pronoun that matches the established point of view.
│  │  │  └─ YES → Continue.
│  │  │
│  │  └─ If all checks pass, the pronoun is correct → NO CHANGE.
│  │
│  └─ NO → This is not a pronoun question. Move to another rule category.
```

---

## 4.12 — Final Notes: The Psychology of Trap Questions

Understanding the rules is necessary but not sufficient. You must also understand the **psychology** of how the ACT constructs its traps. Here are the key principles:

1. **Proximity is the enemy.** The ACT places the wrong answer as close to the verb or pronoun as possible. Your job is to look past the nearest noun and find the true subject or antecedent.

2. **Sound is the enemy.** Many wrong answers "sound right" because they reflect common speech patterns. The ACT tests **formal written English**, not colloquial speech. "Everyone should bring their lunch" sounds fine in conversation but is wrong on the ACT.

3. **Length is the enemy.** The ACT makes wrong answers longer and more complex to make them seem more "sophisticated." The correct answer is often the simplest one.

4. **Familiarity is the enemy.** The ACT uses words you see every day (*its, it's, their, there, they're, who's, whose*) because you are less likely to scrutinize them. The most dangerous errors are the ones you have been making for years without noticing.

5. **Speed is the enemy.** When you rush, you rely on instinct rather than analysis. The Bracketing Method, the Substitution Test, and the Expansion Test exist to slow you down just enough to apply the rules correctly.

Master these tactics, and you will convert subject-verb agreement and pronoun questions from your weakest areas into your strongest. These are the most "mechanical" questions on the test — they reward systematic analysis over intuition. Every question in this category is, in principle, solvable in under 30 seconds with the right method.

---

*End of Chapter 4.*

---


# Chapter 5: Verb Tense, Mood, Voice, and Verbals — Consistency Frameworks and Decision Trees

---

## 5.0 — Introduction: Why Verb Questions Dominate the ACT English Section

Verb-related questions constitute one of the most heavily tested categories on the ACT English section. The test makers at ACT, Inc. understand that verb errors are among the most common mistakes in student writing, and they exploit this by constructing answer choices that include incorrect tense shifts, improper mood usage, unnecessary passive constructions, and verbals masquerading as finite verbs. A single verb error can render an entire sentence ungrammatical, and the ACT frequently tests whether you can identify not only *that* a verb is wrong but *why* it is wrong and *how* to fix it within the broader context of a passage that may span multiple time frames.

This chapter provides an exhaustive, textbook-level treatment of every verb-related concept tested on the ACT English section. We will begin with the architecture of the English tense system, move through the logic of tense consistency and shift, explore the subjunctive mood, examine active versus passive voice, dissect verbals (gerunds, participles, and infinitives), and conclude with decision trees and diagnostic frameworks that will allow you to approach any verb question on the ACT with surgical precision.

By the end of this chapter, you will be able to:
- Identify all major verb tenses and their appropriate contextual usage
- Determine when a tense shift is logically required versus when it represents an error
- Apply the subjunctive mood correctly in hypothetical and demand contexts
- Choose active voice over passive voice when the ACT requires conciseness and clarity
- Distinguish between gerunds, participles, and infinitives and identify their syntactic roles
- Use decision tree frameworks to diagnose verb errors systematically

---

## 5.1 — The English Tense System: A Complete Architecture

English verbs encode information about **time** (when an action occurs), **aspect** (whether the action is completed, ongoing, or habitual), and **mood** (whether the action is factual, hypothetical, or imperative). Together, these dimensions create the tense-aspect system that the ACT tests.

### 5.1.1 — The Three Time Frames

Every verb in English relates to one of three fundamental time frames:

| Time Frame | Signal | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Present** | Now, currently, habitually | *She **walks** to school.* |
| **Past** | Yesterday, last year, completed | *She **walked** to school.* |
| **Future** | Tomorrow, will, going to | *She **will walk** to school.* |

These three time frames are the backbone of every tense. However, the ACT does not simply test whether you can identify present versus past versus future. The test goes deeper, probing whether you understand **aspect**: the internal structure of the action within that time frame.

### 5.1.2 — The Four Aspects

Each time frame can express itself through one of four aspects:

1. **Simple** (also called "zero aspect"): A habitual, general, or single completed action with no particular emphasis on its internal structure.
2. **Progressive** (also called "continuous"): An action that is, was, or will be in progress at a specific time.
3. **Perfect**: An action that was completed before another time or event.
4. **Perfect Progressive** (also called "perfect continuous"): An action that was ongoing over a period of time before another time or event.

Combining three time frames with four aspects yields the full English tense system. While not all twelve combinations are equally common in everyday speech, the ACT can test any of them, particularly the distinctions between simple past and present perfect, and between past perfect and simple past.

### 5.1.3 — The Complete Tense Map

Below is the full conjugation map for a regular verb (*walk*) and the irregular verb *go*, across all major tenses the ACT may reference.

#### Regular Verb: *walk*

| Tense | Structure | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Simple Present** | walk / walks | *She walks to school every day.* |
| **Present Progressive** | am/is/are + walking | *She is walking to school now.* |
| **Present Perfect** | has/have + walked | *She has walked to school many times.* |
| **Present Perfect Progressive** | has/have + been + walking | *She has been walking to school for years.* |
| **Simple Past** | walked | *She walked to school yesterday.* |
| **Past Progressive** | was/were + walking | *She was walking to school when it rained.* |
| **Past Perfect** | had + walked | *She had walked to school before the bus arrived.* |
| **Past Perfect Progressive** | had + been + walking | *She had been walking to school for an hour before the bus came.* |
| **Simple Future** | will + walk | *She will walk to school tomorrow.* |
| **Future Progressive** | will be + walking | *She will be walking to school at 8 AM.* |
| **Future Perfect** | will have + walked | *She will have walked to school a thousand times by graduation.* |
| **Future Perfect Progressive** | will have been + walking | *By next year, she will have been walking to school for a decade.* |

#### Irregular Verb: *go*

| Tense | Forms | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Base** | go | *They go to the park.* |
| **Simple Past** | went | *They went to the park.* |
| **Past Participle** | gone | *They have gone to the park.* |
| **Present Participle** | going | *They are going to the park.* |

#### Irregular Verb: *write*

| Tense | Forms | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Base** | write | *She writes every day.* |
| **Simple Past** | wrote | *She wrote yesterday.* |
| **Past Participle** | written | *She has written for hours.* |
| **Present Participle** | writing | *She is writing now.* |

#### Irregular Verb: *begin*

| Tense | Forms | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Base** | begin | *The concert begins at noon.* |
| **Simple Past** | began | *The concert began at noon.* |
| **Past Participle** | begun | *The concert has already begun.* |
| **Present Participle** | beginning | *The concert is beginning.* |

#### Irregular Verb: *drink*

| Tense | Forms | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Base** | drink | *He drinks coffee.* |
| **Simple Past** | drank | *He drank coffee.* |
| **Past Participle** | drunk | *He has drunk three cups.* |
| **Present Participle** | drinking | *He is drinking coffee.* |

#### Irregular Verb: *break*

| Tense | Forms | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Base** | break | *They break the rules.* |
| **Simple Past** | broke | *They broke the rules.* |
| **Past Participle** | broken | *They have broken the rules.* |
| **Present Participle** | breaking | *They are breaking the rules.* |

#### Irregular Verb: *choose*

| Tense | Forms | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Base** | choose | *We choose carefully.* |
| **Simple Past** | chose | *We chose carefully.* |
| **Past Participle** | chosen | *We have chosen wisely.* |
| **Present Participle** | choosing | *We are choosing now.* |

### 5.1.4 — The ACT's Favorite Tense Distinctions

While the ACT can theoretically test any tense distinction, certain patterns appear with much higher frequency than others:

**A. Simple Past vs. Present Perfect**
This is the single most tested tense distinction on the ACT. The key differentiator:
- **Simple past** references a specific, finished time in the past (*yesterday, last year, in 1995, when I was young*). The action is completely disconnected from the present.
- **Present perfect** connects a past action to the present moment. It is used with *since* (a specific starting point), *for* (a duration of time), or when the time of the action is unspecified but the result is relevant now.
- *"I **lived** in Chicago for three years."* → I no longer live there; the time period is finished.
- *"I **have lived** in Chicago for three years."* → I still live here; the time period continues into the present.

**B. Simple Past vs. Past Perfect**
- **Past perfect** is the "past of the past." It describes the *earlier* of two past actions. If you see two past actions in a sentence and one clearly happened before the other, the earlier one needs past perfect.
- *"By the time the teacher **arrived**, the students **had already started** the experiment."*
  - Had started = earlier action (past perfect)
  - Arrived = later action (simple past)

**C. Present Progressive vs. Simple Present**
- **Simple present** describes habits, general truths, and repeated actions. (*She walks to school every day.*)
- **Present progressive** describes an action happening right now or temporarily. (*She is walking to school right now.*)
- The ACT will occasionally test whether a sentence mixes these up, particularly in sentences that contain time markers.

**D. Past Progressive vs. Simple Past**
- **Past progressive** describes an ongoing action in the past, often interrupted by another event. (*She was walking to school when it rained.*)
- **Simple past** describes a completed past action. (*She walked to school yesterday.*)
- The progressive form provides the "background action" while the simple past provides the "interruption."

---

## 5.2 — Tense Consistency: The Cardinal Rule and Its Exceptions

### 5.2.1 — The Cardinal Rule

The ACT's default expectation is that verb tenses within a sentence and within a passage will remain **consistent** unless there is a logical reason for a shift. This means:

- If a passage is written primarily in the present tense, all verbs should be in the present tense unless the content requires a different time reference.
- If a passage narrates events in the past, all verbs should be in the past tense unless the content references an earlier or later time.
- Unnecessary or illogical tense shifts are almost always wrong on the ACT.

**Example of an Illogical Tense Shift:**
- ❌ *"The scientist **explains** the theory yesterday and **publishes** her results today."*
  - "Explains" (present) clashes with "yesterday" (past). The correct form is "explained."
- ✅ *"The scientist **explained** the theory yesterday and **published** her results today."*

**Example of When Tense Consistency Requires Caution:**
- ✅ *"Justin **bought** a Honda so that he **can** save money on gas."*
  - Here, the shift from past (*bought*) to present (*can*) is logically justified: the buying happened in the past, but the saving of money is an ongoing present reality.
  - This is an **acceptable** tense shift on the ACT because the timeline genuinely changes.

### 5.2.2 — When Tense Shifts Are Required

There are specific situations where a tense shift is not only acceptable but **required** for logical accuracy:

| Situation | Shift Required | Example |
|---|---|---|
| A past action explained a present result | Past → Present | *She studied hard, so she **is** ready for the test.* |
| A past action preceded another past action | Past Perfect → Simple Past | *He **had finished** dinner before she **arrived**.* |
| A general truth appears within a past-tense narrative | Present maintained throughout the clause | *The teacher explained that water **boils** at 100°C.* |
| A future consequence follows a past or present condition | Past/Present → Future | *If she passed the test, she **will celebrate**.* |
| A flashback within a past-tense narrative | Past Perfect for the earlier events, Simple Past for the main timeline | *She remembered the day they **had first met**. He **was** wearing a blue shirt.* |

### 5.2.3 — The "General Truth" Exception

When a clause expresses a **general truth**, a **scientific fact**, or something that is **always true**, the present tense should be used even if the rest of the passage is in the past tense.

- ❌ *"The professor explained that water **froze** at 0°C."* (incorrect—makes freezing sound like it was true only in the past)
- ✅ *"The professor explained that water **freezes** at 0°C."* (correct—freezing is an eternal scientific fact)

This exception is tested on the ACT with surprising regularity. The rule is: **general truths always take the present tense**.

### 5.2.4 — Diagnostic Procedure for Tense Consistency Questions

When you encounter a verb tense question on the ACT, follow this procedure:

**Step 1: Identify the verb in question and its current tense.**
Ask yourself: What tense is the underlined verb in? What does that tense imply about time?

**Step 2: Look for time markers in the sentence.**
Words like *yesterday, last year, since, for, by the time, before, after, when, in 2010, during the war* are signals that constrain which tense is possible.

**Step 3: Check the surrounding sentences.**
What tense is the passage using in adjacent sentences? If the passage is in the past, the verb is likely in the past unless a shift is logically required.

**Step 4: If two actions are described, determine their chronological order.**
The earlier action needs a "more past" tense (past perfect, present perfect) and the later action needs a "less past" tense (simple past, present).

**Step 5: Check each answer choice against your analysis.**
Eliminate any answer that violates the timeline logic you have established.

---

## 5.3 — The Subjunctive Mood: Hypotheticals, Wishes, and Demands

### 5.3.1 — What Is Mood?

In grammar, **mood** refers to the attitude a verb expresses toward the reality of the action it describes. English has three moods:

| Mood | Function | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Indicative** | States a fact or asks a question (most verbs) | *She **walks** to school.* / *Did she walk?* |
| **Imperative** | Gives a command | ***Walk** to school.* |
| **Subjunctive** | Expresses hypothetical, wishful, contrary-to-fact, or demanded situations | *If I **were** taller, I would play basketball.* |

The ACT tests the subjunctive mood regularly, and it is one of the areas where students make the most errors because the subjunctive often sounds "wrong" to ears accustomed to colloquial English.

### 5.3.2 — The Hypothetical/Wishful Subjunctive: "If I Were"

The most common subjunctive tested on the ACT appears in **contrary-to-fact conditions** — situations that are not real, not true, or purely hypothetical.

**Rule:** In contrary-to-fact conditional sentences, use **"were"** instead of "was," regardless of the subject.

- ❌ *"If I **was** you, I would study more."*
- ✅ *"If I **were** you, I would study more."*

- ❌ *"If she **was** here, she would help us."*
- ✅ *"If she **were** here, she would help us."*

- ❌ *"I wish I **was** taller."*
- ✅ *"I wish I **were** taller."*

This is the famous "I were" construction. The subjunctive **were** signals that the speaker is NOT actually the person in question (or NOT actually taller). The use of **were** is what makes the sentence subjunctive; the use of **was** makes it indicative (stating a fact), which changes the meaning entirely.

**Critical distinction:** Not every sentence beginning with "if" requires the subjunctive. If the condition is **possible or real**, use the indicative mood:

- ✅ *"If it **rains** tomorrow, we will cancel the picnic."* (Rain is possible; this is a real condition.)
- ✅ *"If she **is** at the party, I will say hello."* (She might be there; this is a real possibility.)
- ✅ *"If I **was** rude yesterday, I apologize."* (I may actually have been rude; I'm acknowledging a possible fact.)

The subjunctive **were** is only used when the condition is **contrary to fact** — when the speaker knows the condition is not true.

**ACT Tip:** When you see "If I ___ you" or "I wish I ___" on the ACT, the answer is almost always **were**, not was.

### 5.3.3 — The Mandative Subjunctive: "Demand That," "Recommend That," "Insist That"

The second major subjunctive category tested on the ACT is the **mandative subjunctive**, used after verbs and adjectives of demand, recommendation, requirement, and urgency.

**Rule:** After expressions of demand, recommendation, or requirement, use the **base form of the verb** (the infinitive without "to"). Do not add *-s*, *-ed*, or use *should*.

**Trigger words for the mandative subjunctive:**
- Verbs: *demand, insist, suggest, recommend, require, ask, propose, order, prefer, mandate, stipulate, urge, advise*
- Adjectives: *essential, important, necessary, imperative, crucial, vital, critical*
- Nouns: *demand, requirement, recommendation, necessity*

**Examples:**
- ❌ *"The committee recommends that she **is** admitted."*
- ✅ *"The committee recommends that she **be** admitted."*

- ❌ *"It is essential that he **arrives** on time."*
- ✅ *"It is essential that he **arrive** on time."*

- ❌ *"The rules require that every student **wears** a uniform."*
- ✅ *"The rules require that every student **wear** a uniform."*

- ❌ *"The doctor suggested that she **takes** the medication."*
- ✅ *"The doctor suggested that she **take** the medication."*

**Common Pattern on the ACT:** The test will offer "be" vs. "is," "arrive" vs. "arrives," or "take" vs. "takes." The base form is always correct after these trigger words.

### 5.3.4 — "Whether It Be": The Concessive Subjunctive

A less common but testable subjunctive form is the concessive subjunctive using **"whether it be"** or **"be it."** This is used to express "no matter whether" or "regardless of whether."

- ✅ *"Whether it **be** rain or shine, the event will proceed."*
- ✅ *"Be it** ever so humble, there's no place like home."*

These are more archaic constructions, but the ACT may include them in formal or literary passages.

### 5.3.5 — Subjunctive Decision Tree

To determine whether the subjunctive mood is required, ask these questions in order:

1. **Is the sentence expressing a wish about a present/future contrary-to-fact situation?** → Use **were**.
2. **Is the sentence containing a verb of demand, recommendation, or requirement?** → Use the **base form of the verb** (no *-s*, no *-ed*).
3. **Is the sentence expressing a real/possible condition?** → Use the **indicative** (normal verb forms: *was, is, arrives*).

---

## 5.4 — Active Voice vs. Passive Voice

### 5.4.1 — Definitions and Structural Differences

**Active voice** means the **subject** of the sentence is the **doer** of the action. The subject actively performs what the verb describes.

- *"The researchers **conducted** the study."*
  - Subject (The researchers) → Verb (conducted) → Object (the study).
  - The subject is performing the action.

**Passive voice** means the **subject** of the sentence is the **receiver** of the action. The subject is being acted upon, and the doer is either absent from the sentence or introduced by "by."

- *"The study **was conducted** by the researchers."*
  - Subject (The study) → Verb (was conducted) → Agent (by the researchers).
  - The subject is receiving the action; the researchers are the "agent" at the end.

**Structural formula for passive voice:**
- **Be-verb (conjugated for tense) + Past Participle**
- The agent (doer) is optionally introduced with *by*.

| Tense | Active | Passive |
|---|---|---|
| Present Simple | *They **build** houses.* | *Houses **are built** (by them).* |
| Past Simple | *They **built** the house.* | *The house **was built** (by them).* |
| Present Perfect | *They **have built** the house.* | *The house **has been built** (by them).* |
| Past Perfect | *They **had built** the house.* | *The house **had been built** (by them).* |
| Future Simple | *They **will build** the house.* | *The house **will be built** (by them).* |
| Present Progressive | *They **are building** the house.* | *The house **is being built** (by them).* |

### 5.4.2 — The ACT's Preference for Active Voice

The ACT English section consistently prefers **active voice** over passive voice for several reasons:

1. **Conciseness:** Active voice typically requires fewer words. "The team completed the report" (5 words) vs. "The report was completed by the team" (7 words). The ACT rewards conciseness.

2. **Clarity:** Active voice makes it immediately clear who is performing the action. Passive voice often obscures agency.

3. **Directness:** Active voice creates a stronger, more assertive sentence, which aligns with the ACT's preference for clear, effective writing.

**However, passive voice is not grammatically wrong on the ACT.** It is acceptable when:
- The doer is unknown or unimportant: *"The bank was robbed last night."* (We don't know who robbed it.)
- The receiver of the action is more important than the doer: *"The president was elected by a landslide."* (The president is the focus.)
- Scientific/formal writing conventions call for it: *"The solution was heated to 100°C."* (The focus is on the process, not the person doing it.)

### 5.4.3 — The Dangling Modifier Connection

Passive voice is particularly dangerous on the ACT because it frequently creates **dangling modifiers**. When you use an opening participial phrase, its implied subject must match the grammatical subject of the main clause. Passive voice constructions often break this requirement.

- ❌ *"Walking through the park, **the flowers** were beautiful."*
  - The sentence is passive in structure: "the flowers" weren't walking. This is a classic dangling modifier.
  - The ACT frequently uses passive constructions to create this error.

- ✅ *"Walking through the park, **I** noticed the flowers were beautiful."*
  - Active voice: "I" am the one walking. The modifier is correct.

**Key Insight:** When you see a sentence on the ACT that combines an opening participial phrase with a passive verb, check for a dangling modifier.

### 5.4.4 — "By" as a Clue for Passive Voice

When you see the word **"by"** immediately before or after a verb phrase, it often signals the agent of a passive construction:

- *"The novel **was written** by Toni Morrison."* → Passive
- *"The committee **was annoyed** by the protesters."* → Passive (but see note below on "annoyed by" as a prepositional phrase, not passive)

**Caution:** Not every use of "by" indicates passive voice. "By" can be a preposition meaning "near," "through," or "because of" without creating a passive construction:

- *"She stood **by** the door."* → Prepositional phrase; not passive.
- *"He succeeded **by** working hard."* → Prepositional phrase; not passive.

---

## 5.5 — Verbals: When Verbs Become Something Else

This is one of the most conceptually challenging areas of ACT grammar. **Verbals** are words that originate from verbs but function as a different part of speech within the sentence. The ACT tests three types of verbals: **gerunds**, **participles**, and **infinitives**. Each has distinct grammatical roles and distinct error patterns.

### 5.5.1 — Gerunds

**Definition:** A gerund is a verb form ending in **-ing** that functions as a **noun**. It can serve as a subject, direct object, object of a preposition, or predicate nominative.

**Examples by syntactic role:**

| Role | Example |
|---|---|
| **Subject** | ***Swimming** is great exercise.* |
| **Direct Object** | *I enjoy **reading**.* |
| **Object of Preposition** | *She is interested in **painting**.* |
| **Predicate Nominative** | *His favorite activity is **fishing**.* |

**ACT Trap: Gerunds as Sentence Fragments**

The ACT frequently tests whether you recognize that a gerund phrase alone does not constitute a complete sentence. A gerund phrase has no finite verb — the *-ing* form is a noun, not a verb in this context.

- ❌ *"Running through the rain without an umbrella."* → Fragment. "Running" is a gerund/participle, not a finite verb. There is no subject performing a finite action.
- ✅ *"Running through the rain without an umbrella **was** a bad idea."* → Complete sentence. "Running" is the subject (gerund), and "was" is the finite verb.

**ACT Trap: Possessive + Gerund**

When a pronoun or noun modifies a gerund, it should technically be in the **possessive case**:

- ✅ *"I appreciate **your** helping me."* (The gerund "helping" is modified by the possessive "your.")
- ❌ *"I appreciate **you** helping me."* (Colloquial but technically incorrect in formal English.)

However, the ACT rarely tests this distinction directly. More commonly, it tests whether you can identify a gerund's role in a sentence to determine subject-verb agreement.

**Example:**
- *"Running marathons **requires** incredible endurance."*
  - Subject = "Running marathons" (gerund phrase, singular)
  - Verb = "requires" (singular)
  - The ACT might try to trick you into using "are" or "require" by making "marathons" seem like the subject. It is not — "Running marathons" is a single activity.

### 5.5.2 — Participles

**Definition:** A participle is a verb form that functions as an **adjective**. There are two types:

**Present Participle (-ing form):**
- *"The **burning** building collapsed."* ("burning" describes "building")
- *"She heard **barking** dogs next door."* ("barking" describes "dogs")
- *"The **soothing** music calmed the baby."* ("soothing" describes "music")

**Past Participle (-ed form for regular verbs; irregular forms for irregulars):**
- *"The **broken** window needs repair."* ("broken" describes "window")
- *"A **written** agreement is more reliable."* ("written" describes "agreement")
- *"The **frozen** lake was perfect for skating."* ("frozen" describes "lake")
- *"The **chosen** candidate will start Monday."* ("chosen" describes "candidate")

**ACT Trap: Dangling Participles**

This is one of the most frequently tested errors on the entire ACT English section. A participial phrase at the beginning of a sentence must logically modify the **subject** of the main clause. If it does not, the modifier "dangles."

- ❌ *"Walking through the park, **the flowers** were beautiful."*
  - The participial phrase "Walking through the park" implies a person is walking. But the subject is "the flowers." Flowers don't walk. This is a dangling modifier.

- ✅ *"Walking through the park, **I** noticed the flowers were beautiful."*
  - Now "I" is the subject, and "I" is the one walking. The modifier is correct.

- ❌ *"Having finished the assignment, **the TV** was turned on."*
  - The TV didn't finish the assignment. Dangling.

- ✅ *"Having finished the assignment, **Jill** turned on the TV."*
  - Jill finished the assignment. Correct.

**ACT Trap: Misplaced Participles**

A participial phrase can also be misplaced if it is too far from the noun it modifies:

- ❌ *"The girl **wearing the red dress** was spotted **by the window** eating an ice cream cone."*
  - Is the girl eating ice cream, or is the window eating ice cream? The participial phrase "eating an ice cream cone" is misplaced.

- ✅ *"The girl **wearing the red dress** was eating an ice cream cone **by the window**."*
  - Now "eating an ice cream cone" clearly modifies "the girl."

### 5.5.3 — Infinitives

**Definition:** An infinitive is **"to" + base form of the verb** (e.g., *to run, to eat, to study, to have, to be*). Infinitives can function as **nouns**, **adjectives**, or **adverbs**.

**Examples by syntactic role:**

| Role | Example |
|---|---|
| **Noun (subject)** | ***To err** is human.* |
| **Noun (direct object)** | *She wants **to leave**.* |
| **Adjective** | *I need a book **to read**.* (describes "book") |
| **Adverb** | *He studied **to pass** the exam.* (modifies "studied" — tells why) |

**ACT Trap: Split Infinitives**

A split infinitive occurs when a word (usually an adverb) is placed between "to" and the verb:

- *"She asked me **to quickly finish** the report."* → Split infinitive ("to quickly finish")
- *"She asked me **to finish** the report **quickly**."* → Not split

While split infinitives are increasingly accepted in modern English (and are sometimes necessary for clarity or emphasis), the ACT generally prefers **unsplit infinitives** when both options are grammatically correct and equally clear. If you see an answer choice with a split infinitive and another without, the non-split version is usually correct.

**ACT Trap: Infinitive vs. Gerund After Certain Verbs**

Some verbs can be followed by either an infinitive or a gerund, but the meaning changes:

| Verb | + Gerund | + Infinitive |
|---|---|---|
| **stop** | *She stopped **smoking**.* (She quit the habit.) | *She stopped **to smoke**.* (She paused in order to smoke.) |
| **remember** | *I remember **locking** the door.* (I have a memory of doing it.) | *I remembered **to lock** the door.* (I didn't forget to do it.) |
| **forget** | *I'll never forget **meeting** her.* (I have a memory of it.) | *I forgot **to meet** her.* (I didn't remember to do it.) |
| **try** | *Try **eating** less.* (Experiment with this.) | *Try **to eat** less.* (Make an effort to do this.) |
| **regret** | *I regret **telling** her.* (I'm sorry I did it.) | *I regret **to tell** you.* (I'm sorry, but I must inform you.) |

The ACT occasionally tests these distinctions, particularly with "stop" and "remember."

**Verbs that take ONLY a gerund (no infinitive):**
*enjoy, avoid, consider, discuss, finish, keep, mind, miss, practice, quit, suggest, deny, admit, appreciate, delay, imagine, risk, tolerate*

- ✅ *"I enjoy **reading**."*
- ❌ *"I enjoy **to read**."*

**Verbs that take ONLY an infinitive (no gerund):**
*afford, agree, appear, arrange, ask, claim, decide, demand, expect, fail, hope, learn, manage, need, offer, plan, prepare, promise, refuse, seem, threaten, want, wish*

- ✅ *"I want **to leave**."*
- ❌ *"I want **leaving**."*

### 5.5.4 — Verbal Decision Tree

When you encounter a verbal (a word derived from a verb but functioning as another part of speech), use this decision tree:

1. **Does it end in -ing and function as a noun?** → It is a **gerund**.
2. **Does it end in -ing or -ed (or an irregular past participle form) and function as an adjective?** → It is a **participle**.
3. **Does it begin with "to" + base verb and function as a noun, adjective, or adverb?** → It is an **infinitive**.
4. **Is it functioning as the main verb of a clause?** → It is a **finite verb**, not a verbal.

---

## 5.6 — Commonly Confused Verb Pairs

The ACT frequently tests verb pairs that are easily confused because of their similar forms or meanings. Mastering these pairs is essential.

### 5.6.1 — Lie vs. Lay

This is one of the most commonly confused verb pairs in English, and the ACT tests it regularly.

**Lie** (intransitive — no direct object):
| Form | Tense | Example |
|---|---|---|
| lie | Present | *I lie on the couch every evening.* |
| lay | Past | *I lay on the couch yesterday.* |
| lain | Past Participle | *I have lain on the couch all day.* |
| lying | Present Participle | *I am lying on the couch right now.* |

**Lay** (transitive — requires a direct object):
| Form | Tense | Example |
|---|---|---|
| lay | Present | *I lay the book on the table.* |
| laid | Past | *I laid the book on the table yesterday.* |
| laid | Past Participle | *I have laid the book on the table.* |
| laying | Present Participle | *I am laying the book on the table.* |

**Memory Aid:** "Lie" = recline (no object). "Lay" = put/place (needs an object). Think: "You **lay** something down; you **lie** down yourself."

**ACT Trap:** The past tense of "lie" is "lay" — which is identical to the present tense of "lay." This creates enormous confusion. The ACT exploits this by offering "lay" and "laid" as answer choices and testing whether you know which is correct.

- ❌ *"I laid down on the couch."* (If you mean "reclined," this is wrong. "Laid" requires an object.)
- ✅ *"I lay down on the couch."* (Past tense of "lie" = "lay.")
- ✅ *"I laid the blanket on the couch."* (Past tense of "lay" = "laid." Object = "blanket.")

### 5.6.2 — Sit vs. Set

**Sit** (intransitive — no direct object):
| Form | Tense | Example |
|---|---|---|
| sit | Present | *I sit in the front row.* |
| sat | Past | *I sat in the front row yesterday.* |
| sat | Past Participle | *I have sat in the front row all semester.* |
| sitting | Present Participle | *I am sitting in the front row.* |

**Set** (transitive — requires a direct object):
| Form | Tense | Example |
|---|---|---|
| set | Present | *I set the vase on the shelf.* |
| set | Past | *I set the vase on the shelf yesterday.* |
| set | Past Participle | *I have set the vase on the shelf.* |
| setting | Present Participle | *I am setting the vase on the shelf.* |

**Memory Aid:** "Sit" = be seated (no object). "Set" = put/place (needs an object). Think: "You **set** something down; you **sit** down yourself."

### 5.6.3 — Rise vs. Raise

**Rise** (intransitive — no direct object):
| Form | Tense | Example |
|---|---|---|
| rise | Present | *The sun rises in the east.* |
| rose | Past | *The sun rose at 6 AM.* |
| risen | Past Participle | *The sun has risen.* |
| rising | Present Participle | *The sun is rising.* |

**Raise** (transitive — requires a direct object):
| Form | Tense | Example |
|---|---|---|
| raise | Present | *I raise my hand in class.* |
| raised | Past | *I raised my hand yesterday.* |
| raised | Past Participle | *I have raised my hand three times.* |
| raising | Present Participle | *I am raising my hand.* |

**Memory Aid:** "Rise" = go up on its own (no object). "Raise" = lift something up (needs an object). Think: "The sun **rises** on its own; I **raise** the curtain."

### 5.6.4 — Affect vs. Effect

While this is technically a word choice (diction) issue rather than a verb form issue, it is so frequently tested on the ACT that it belongs in any comprehensive verb discussion.

**Affect** (verb) = to influence:
- *"The weather **affects** my mood."*
- *"The new policy will **affect** all employees."*

**Effect** (noun) = a result:
- *"The **effect** of the new policy was immediate."*
- *"What are the long-term **effects** of climate change?"*

**Exceptions (rare but real):**
- **Effect** can be a verb meaning "to bring about" or "to cause to happen": *"The new manager hopes to **effect** positive change."*
- **Affect** can be a noun in psychology referring to emotional expression: *"The patient displayed a flat **affect**."*

**ACT Tip:** In 95%+ of cases on the ACT, **affect** = verb and **effect** = noun. If you can replace the word with "influence," use **affect**. If you can replace it with "result," use **effect**.

### 5.6.5 — Other Commonly Confused Verbs

| Pair | Distinction | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **ensure** vs. **insure** | *ensure* = make certain; *insure* = provide insurance | *"Please **ensure** the door is locked."* / *"I need to **insure** my car."* |
| **accept** vs. **except** | *accept* = receive; *except* = excluding | *"I **accept** your apology."* / *"Everyone **except** John came."* |
| **precede** vs. **proceed** | *precede* = come before; *proceed* = continue | *"The introduction **precedes** the first chapter."* / *"Let's **proceed** with the plan."* |
| **allude** vs. **elude** | *allude* = refer to indirectly; *elude* = escape | *"She **alluded** to the scandal."* / *"The criminal **eluded** police."* |
| **elicit** vs. **illicit** | *elicit* = draw out (verb); *illicit* = illegal (adjective) | *"The joke **elicited** laughter."* / *"He was arrested for **illicit** activities."* |

---

## 5.7 — Comprehensive Verb Decision Tree for the ACT

When you encounter any verb-related question on the ACT, use this master decision tree to diagnose the error:

```
START: Is the underlined word a verb or verbal?
│
├── YES, it is a finite verb (main verb of a clause):
│   ├── Is the verb tense consistent with the passage's timeline?
│   │   ├── NO → Check for time markers. Is a shift logically required?
│   │   │   ├── YES (e.g., past action before another past action) → Use past perfect
│   │   │   └── NO (unnecessary shift) → Change to match the passage tense
│   │   └── YES → Tense is fine. Check other issues.
│   │
│   ├── Does the verb agree with its subject in number?
│   │   ├── Cross out prepositional phrases between subject and verb
│   │   ├── Identify the true subject
│   │   └── Match singular subject → singular verb; plural subject → plural verb
│   │
│   ├── Is the sentence in a context requiring the subjunctive mood?
│   │   ├── "If I ___ you" / "I wish I ___" → Use WERE (not was)
│   │   ├── "Demand/recommend/require that..." → Use BASE FORM of verb
│   │   └── Otherwise → Use indicative (normal verb forms)
│   │
│   └── Is the verb in active or passive voice?
│       ├── Both are grammatically correct
│       ├── ACT prefers ACTIVE voice (more concise, more direct)
│       ├── Passive is acceptable when doer is unknown/unimportant
│       └── Check for dangling modifiers with passive constructions
│
└── NO, it is a verbal (gerund, participle, or infinitive):
    ├── Does it end in -ing and function as a NOUN? → GERUND
    │   ├── Check: Is the gerund the subject? → Subject-verb agreement
    │   ├── Check: Is the gerund part of a fragment? → Needs a finite verb
    │   └── Check: Is the gerund modified by a possessive? → "your helping" not "you helping"
    │
    ├── Does it end in -ing or -ed (or irregular form) and function as an ADJECTIVE? → PARTICIPLE
    │   ├── Check: Does the participle logically modify the nearest noun?
    │   │   ├── NO → DANGLING MODIFIER → Rewrite to fix
    │   │   └── YES → Check for misplaced modifier (too far from noun)
    │   └── Check: Is the participle part of a progressive verb or passive construction?
    │
    └── Does it begin with "to" + base verb? → INFINITIVE
        ├── Check: Is the infinitive functioning as a noun, adjective, or adverb?
        ├── Check: Is it a split infinitive? → ACT prefers unsplit
        └── Check: Does the preceding verb require infinitive or gerund?
            ├── enjoy, avoid, consider, suggest → GERUND only
            ├── want, need, plan, hope, decide → INFINITIVE only
            └── stop, remember, forget, try → Meaning changes
```

---

## 5.8 — Tense Consistency Across Passages: A Deeper Analysis

### 5.8.1 — The "Tense Zone" Concept

Think of each passage on the ACT as existing within a **tense zone** — a dominant tense that governs the passage. Most passages fall into one of three zones:

1. **Present-tense zone:** The passage describes current events, general truths, or ongoing situations. Most verbs are in the present tense.
2. **Past-tense zone:** The passage narrates historical events, personal anecdotes, or completed stories. Most verbs are in the past tense.
3. **Mixed-tense zone:** The passage shifts between time frames (e.g., a present-tense discussion that references historical examples). Multiple tenses are used, but each must be logically justified.

### 5.8.2 — How the ACT Tests Tense Consistency

The ACT tests tense consistency in several ways:

**Type 1: The Unnecessary Shift**
A sentence in a past-tense passage suddenly shifts to present tense for no reason.
- ❌ *"The colonists **fought** for their independence. The war **is** long and brutal."*
- ✅ *"The colonists **fought** for their independence. The war **was** long and brutal."*

**Type 2: The Missing Shift**
A sentence that references a different time frame fails to shift tenses.
- ❌ *"She **walked** to school yesterday and **eats** lunch in the cafeteria."*
- ✅ *"She **walked** to school yesterday and **ate** lunch in the cafeteria."*

**Type 3: The Wrong Perfect Tense**
Using present perfect when simple past is required, or vice versa.
- ❌ *"I **have walked** to school yesterday."* ("Yesterday" requires simple past.)
- ✅ *"I **walked** to school yesterday."*
- ✅ *"I **have walked** to school many times."* (Unspecified time → present perfect.)

**Type 4: The Missing Past Perfect**
Using simple past for the earlier of two past actions.
- ❌ *"By the time the teacher **arrived**, the students **already started** the experiment."*
- ✅ *"By the time the teacher **arrived**, the students **had already started** the experiment."*

### 5.8.3 — The "By the Time" Signal

The phrase **"by the time"** is one of the strongest signals on the ACT that past perfect is required. It explicitly establishes that one action was completed before another.

- *"By the time we **arrived**, the movie **had** already **started**."*
- *"By the time she **submitted** her essay, she **had revised** it three times."*
- *"By the time the rescue team **reached** the hikers, they **had been** stranded for two days."*

### 5.8.4 — The "Since" and "For" Signals

The words **"since"** and **"for"** are strong signals that the **present perfect** or **present perfect progressive** is required.

- *"She **has lived** here **since** 2010."* (Present perfect with "since" — started in the past, continues now.)
- *"She **has been living** here **for** ten years."* (Present perfect progressive with "for" — duration from past to present.)
- ❌ *"She **lived** here **since** 2010."* (Wrong — "since" requires present perfect.)
- ✅ *"She **lived** here **for** ten years."* (This is acceptable if she no longer lives here — simple past with "for" for a finished period.)

**Key distinction:**
- *"She **has worked** here **for** five years."* → She still works here.
- *"She **worked** here **for** five years."* → She no longer works here.

---

## 5.9 — Verb Tense in Complex Sentences

### 5.9.1 — Sequence of Tenses in Subordinate Clauses

When a sentence contains a main clause and a subordinate clause, the tenses of the two clauses must be logically related:

- **If the main clause is in the present tense**, the subordinate clause can be in any tense that is logically appropriate:
  - *"She **says** that she **is** tired."* (present + present)
  - *"She **says** that she **was** tired."* (present + past — she was tired at some earlier time)
  - *"She **says** that she **will be** tired."* (present + future)
  - *"She **says** that she **has been** tired."* (present + present perfect)

- **If the main clause is in the past tense**, the subordinate clause usually shifts to a past tense as well (this is called **backshifting**):
  - *"She **said** that she **was** tired."* (past + present → past + past)
  - *"She **said** that she **had been** tired."* (past + present perfect → past + past perfect)
  - *"She **said** that she **would be** tired."* (past + future → past + conditional)

**Exception:** General truths in subordinate clauses remain in the present tense regardless of the main clause's tense:
- *"The teacher **explained** that water **freezes** at 0°C."* (Main clause past, subordinate clause present — because freezing is an eternal truth.)

### 5.9.2 — Conditional Sentences

Conditional sentences (if-then sentences) follow specific tense patterns:

| Type | If-Clause | Then-Clause | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Real/Zero Conditional** (general truth) | Present | Present | *"If you **heat** water, it **boils**."* |
| **First Conditional** (possible future) | Present | Future | *"If it **rains**, we **will cancel**."* |
| **Second Conditional** (unreal present) | Past subjunctive | Would + base verb | *"If I **were** rich, I **would travel**."* |
| **Third Conditional** (unreal past) | Past perfect | Would have + past participle | *"If I **had studied**, I **would have passed**."* |

The ACT most commonly tests the **second conditional** (which requires the subjunctive "were") and the distinction between the second and third conditionals.

---

## 5.10 — Verbals and Sentence Completeness

### 5.10.1 — The Fragment Test

One of the most important things the ACT tests regarding verbals is whether a sentence has a **finite verb**. A sentence with only a verbal (and no finite verb) is a **fragment**.

**How to test:** Ask yourself: "What is the main action of the sentence? Is there a verb that shows tense (past, present, future)?"

- ❌ *"The students studying for the exam in the library."*
  - "Studying" is a present participle (verbal). There is no finite verb. This is a fragment.
  - Fix: *"The students **were** studying for the exam in the library."* (Now "were" is the finite verb.)

- ❌ *"Running quickly down the hallway."*
  - "Running" is a present participle (verbal). No finite verb. Fragment.
  - Fix: *"She **was** running quickly down the hallway."*

- ❌ *"To understand the complex theory of relativity."*
  - "To understand" is an infinitive (verbal). No finite verb. Fragment.
  - Fix: *"The students **wanted** to understand the complex theory of relativity."*

### 5.10.2 — The "Helping Verb" Solution

Sometimes a sentence appears to have a verbal but is actually complete because a **helping verb** (auxiliary verb) precedes it:

| Helping Verb | + Verbal | = Finite Verb Phrase |
|---|---|---|
| is, am, are | studying | *is studying* (present progressive) |
| was, were | walking | *was walking* (past progressive) |
| has, have, had | written | *has written* (present perfect) |
| has, have, had | been writing | *has been writing* (present perfect progressive) |
| will | be | *will be* (future) |
| can, could, should, would, may, might, must | be | *should be* (modal + base) |

The key is that the **helping verb** carries the tense and makes the verb phrase finite. Without the helping verb, the verbal alone is not a finite verb.

---

## 5.11 — Advanced Verb Topics for High Scorers

### 5.11.1 — The "Would Of" Trap

One of the most common errors the ACT tests is the substitution of **"of"** for **"have"** in conditional and modal constructions:

- ❌ *"I **would of** studied harder."* → ✅ *"I **would have** studied harder."*
- ❌ *"She **could of** won the race."* → ✅ *"She **could have** won the race."*
- ❌ *"They **should of** left earlier."* → ✅ *"They **should have** left earlier."*
- ❌ *"He **might of** forgotten."* → ✅ *"He **might have** forgotten."*

This error arises because "have" in rapid speech sounds like "of" after "would," "could," "should," and "might." On the ACT, **"would of," "could of," "should of," and "might of" are ALWAYS wrong.** The correct forms are **would have, could have, should have, might have.**

Some ACT answer choices may use contractions instead: *would've, could've, should've, might've.* These are grammatically correct (the contraction "ve" = "have"), but the ACT rarely uses contractions in correct answer choices because it prefers formal English.

### 5.11.2 — Double Verbs and Redundant Constructions

The ACT occasionally tests whether you can identify redundant verb constructions:

- ❌ *"The results **were** **had** by the team."* (Nonsensical double verb.)
- ✅ *"The results **were** surprising."* (Correct.)
- ✅ *"The team **had** the results."* (Correct.)

- ❌ *"The book **was** **is** on the shelf."* (Two finite verbs competing.)
- ✅ *"The book **is** on the shelf."*

### 5.11.3 — Verb Agreement with "Each," "Every," and "Everyone"

These words are **always singular** on the ACT, even though they may seem to refer to multiple people:

- ❌ *"Everyone **are** welcome to attend."*
- ✅ *"Everyone **is** welcome to attend."*

- ❌ *"Each of the students **have** completed the assignment."*
- ✅ *"Each of the students **has** completed the assignment."*

- ❌ *"Every participant **receive** a certificate."*
- ✅ *"Every participant **receives** a certificate."*

### 5.11.4 — Verb Agreement with Collective Nouns

Collective nouns (*team, group, family, committee, audience, class, jury, government*) are **singular** in American English when the group acts as a unit:

- ✅ *"The team **is** winning."* (The team as a whole is winning.)
- ✅ *"The committee **has** made its decision."*

However, if the members of the group are acting individually, British English uses plural verbs. The ACT follows **American English conventions**, so collective nouns are almost always singular.

### 5.11.5 — Verb Agreement with "The Number of" vs. "A Number of"

- **"The number of"** → **Singular verb** (refers to the quantity itself as a single figure)
  - *"The number of students **is** increasing."*

- **"A number of"** → **Plural verb** (means "several" or "many")
  - *"A number of students **are** absent today."*

---

## 5.12 — Putting It All Together: The Verb Error Identification Protocol

When you encounter a verb underlined on the ACT, follow this systematic protocol:

### Step 1: Identify the Verb's Role
- Is it the **main verb** of a clause?
- Is it a **helping verb** + main verb combination?
- Is it a **verbal** (gerund, participle, infinitive)?

### Step 2: Check Tense Consistency
- What is the **dominant tense** of the passage?
- Does the verb's tense match the passage's timeline?
- Are there **time markers** that require a specific tense?
- If two actions are described, is the **chronological order** correctly reflected in the tenses?

### Step 3: Check Subject-Verb Agreement
- What is the **true subject** of the verb?
- Have you **crossed out** all intervening prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and participial phrases?
- Is the subject singular or plural?
- Does the verb match?

### Step 4: Check Mood
- Is the sentence expressing a **contrary-to-fact** condition? → Use subjunctive (*were*).
- Is the sentence following a **verb of demand/recommendation**? → Use base form.
- Otherwise → Use indicative (normal forms).

### Step 5: Check Voice
- Is the verb in **active** or **passive** voice?
- If both are grammatically correct, **active** is usually preferred.
- Check for **dangling modifiers** if the sentence has an opening participial phrase.

### Step 6: Check for Common Confusions
- Is the verb *lie* or *lay*? *Sit* or *set*? *Rise* or *raise*?
- Is the verb *affect* or *effect*?
- Is it *would have* (not *would of*)?

### Step 7: Check for Verbals Creating Fragments
- If the underlined portion contains a verbal, does the sentence also contain a **finite verb**?
- If not, the sentence is a **fragment** and needs a finite verb added.

---

## 5.13 — Summary: The Verb Concepts Most Likely to Appear

Based on analysis of official ACT tests and the frequency data from our source materials, here are the verb-related concepts ranked by testing frequency:

| Rank | Concept | Frequency | Key Signal |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | Tense consistency/shift | Very High | Time markers, passage context |
| 2 | Subject-verb agreement | Very High | Intervening phrases, indefinite pronouns |
| 3 | Dangling/misplaced participles | High | Opening -ing phrases |
| 4 | Simple past vs. present perfect | High | "Since," "for," "yesterday" |
| 5 | Subjunctive mood ("were") | Moderate-High | "If I ___ you," "I wish" |
| 6 | Active vs. passive voice | Moderate | "By" + agent, conciseness |
| 7 | Lie/lay, sit/set, rise/raise | Moderate | Direct objects |
| 8 | Gerund as subject (SVA) | Moderate | -ing subject + singular verb |
| 9 | Past perfect | Moderate | "By the time," two past actions |
| 10 | Infinitive vs. gerund | Low-Moderate | Verb + ___ (stop, remember, try) |
| 11 | "Would of" → "would have" | Low-Moderate | Modal + of/have |
| 12 | Split infinitives | Low | "to quickly ___" vs. "to ___ quickly" |

---

## 5.14 — Final Notes on Verb Mastery

Mastering verbs on the ACT English section requires more than memorizing rules. It requires developing an **instinct for how verbs function in context**. The ACT does not test verbs in isolation; it tests them within passages that have logical timelines, consistent tones, and coherent structures. Every verb question you encounter should be evaluated against the backdrop of the passage's overall meaning and temporal framework.

The most successful ACT test-takers approach verb questions with a **diagnostic mindset**: they don't just ask "Is this verb correct?" but rather "What is this verb *doing* in this sentence, and does that function make sense given everything else in the passage?"

Train yourself to:
1. **Read the full sentence and at least one sentence before and after** before evaluating a verb.
2. **Identify the passage's dominant tense** early in your reading.
3. **Spot time markers** (yesterday, since, by the time, for, now, currently) that constrain verb choice.
4. **Recognize the most common traps** (would of, unnecessary tense shifts, subjunctive errors, dangling participles).
5. **Use the decision trees** in this chapter as diagnostic tools until the process becomes second nature.

With systematic practice and the frameworks outlined in this chapter, verb questions — which constitute one of the largest categories on the ACT English section — can become among the most predictable and manageable.

---

---


# Chapter 6: Modifier Placement, Parallel Structure, and Comparative Logic — Precision Rules and Diagnostic Methods

---

## 6.0 — Introduction: Why Precision Grammar Dominates the ACT English Section

The ACT English section does not merely test whether you can "hear" whether something sounds right. It tests whether you can **diagnose** with surgical precision why a sentence is wrong and **prescribe** the exact correction. Three categories of error—modifier placement, parallel structure, and comparative logic—account for a staggering proportion of the questions on every single ACT English test. These are not obscure, once-in-a-while topics. They are the **backbone** of the Conventions of Standard English domain, and they appear with such regularity that mastery of them alone can move your score multiple points.

This chapter leaves nothing unexplored. We begin with modifiers—words, phrases, and clauses that describe other elements in a sentence—and dissect every variety of error the ACT constructs around them. We turn next to parallel structure, the principle that demands grammatical symmetry in any series, list, or comparison. Finally, we tackle comparative logic, the rules governing how we compare things without creating illogical or ambiguous statements.

By the end of this chapter, you will not simply recognize these errors. You will **anticipate** them, **categorize** them, and **eliminate** wrong answers with mechanical efficiency.

---

## PART I: MODIFIER PLACEMENT — THE GOLDEN PROXIMITY PRINCIPLE

---

## 6.1 — What Is a Modifier?

A modifier is any word, phrase, or clause that **describes, limits, or qualifies** another word in the sentence. Modifiers are the adjectives, adverbs, prepositional phrases, participial phrases, and relative clauses that add detail and nuance to the core statement of a sentence.

Consider this simple sentence:

> *The woman walked.*

Now observe what modifiers do:

> *The woman **in the red coat** walked **slowly** **down the hill**.*

- "in the red coat" = prepositional phrase modifying "woman"
- "slowly" = adverb modifying "walked"
- "down the hill" = prepositional phrase modifying "walked"

Every modifier must be placed **as close as possible** to the word it modifies. This is the **Golden Proximity Principle**, and it is the single most important concept in modifier placement.

**The Golden Proximity Principle:** A modifier must be placed directly adjacent to—or as close as grammatically possible to—the word it modifies, so that no other word could reasonably be mistaken as the thing being modified.

When this principle is violated, two categories of error emerge:

1. **Misplaced Modifiers** — the modifier is in the wrong position, creating ambiguity or unintended meaning, but the sentence can still be (mis)interpreted.
2. **Dangling Modifiers** — the modifier has no logical word to modify at all, because the intended target is missing from the sentence.

---

## 6.2 — Misplaced Modifiers: The Distance Problem

A misplaced modifier occurs when a modifier is separated from the word it is supposed to describe, creating ambiguity, absurdity, or a meaning the writer never intended.

### 6.2.1 — The Classic Misplacement Pattern

Consider:

> *She almost drove her kids to school every day.*

What does this sentence communicate? It says that she "almost drove" them—meaning she came close to performing the act of driving but never actually did it. She nearly drove them, but didn't follow through. But the intended meaning is almost certainly:

> *She drove her kids to school almost every day.*

Here, "almost" modifies "every day," meaning on nearly every day (but not literally every single day), she drove her kids. The placement of "almost" completely changes the meaning.

**Diagnostic Method:** When you see a limiting word (*almost, nearly, only, even, just, merely, hardly, scarcely*), immediately ask: **What word is this word supposed to modify?** Then check whether it is physically adjacent to that word. If not, the modifier is misplaced.

### 6.2.2 — The "Only" Problem

The word "only" is the most frequently tested misplaced modifier on the ACT. Its placement is extraordinarily sensitive:

- *I **only** eat pizza on Fridays.* (I do nothing else with pizza on Fridays—I only eat it, I don't bake it, buy it, etc.)
- *I eat **only** pizza on Fridays.* (I eat nothing else on Fridays—only pizza.)
- *I eat pizza **only** on Fridays.* (I eat pizza on no other day—only Fridays.)

Each placement creates a different meaning. The ACT tests whether you can identify which placement conveys the intended meaning based on context.

**Strategy:** When "only" appears in an underlined portion, read the surrounding sentences to determine what the author intends "only" to restrict. Then find the answer choice that places "only" immediately before that word.

### 6.2.3 — Misplaced Prepositional Phrases

> *The man was seen walking a dog in a tuxedo.*

Who is wearing the tuxedo? The sentence structure suggests the dog is. But the intended meaning is almost certainly:

> *The man in a tuxedo was seen walking a dog.*

Or, if the dog truly was in a tuxedo (perhaps in a humorous passage):

> *The man was seen walking a dog that was in a tuxedo.*

**Diagnostic Method:** When a prepositional phrase appears at the end of a sentence, check whether it could logically modify more than one noun. If so, reposition it next to the noun it is meant to describe.

### 6.2.4 — Misplaced Participial Phrases (Non-Dangling)

A participial phrase can be misplaced without fully dangling, when the intended subject exists in the sentence but is not positioned immediately after the modifier.

> *Covered in frosting, the children devoured the cupcakes.*

This sentence technically has a subject ("the children") after the participial phrase, but it creates the absurd image that the children are covered in frosting. The intended meaning is:

> *The children devoured the cupcakes, which were covered in frosting.*

Or:

> *The children devoured the cupcakes covered in frosting.*

Here, "covered in frosting" is placed directly before "cupcakes," the noun it modifies.

### 6.2.5 — The "Squinting" Modifier

A squinting modifier is one that could modify either the word before it or the word after it, creating genuine ambiguity.

> *Students who study occasionally get good grades.*

Does this mean:
- Students who **occasionally study** get good grades? (study = occasionally)
- Students who study get good grades **occasionally**? (get = occasionally)

**Correct (if the first meaning is intended):** *Students who occasionally study get good grades.*

**Correct (if the second meaning is intended):** *Students who study get good grades occasionally.*

**Diagnostic Method:** If a modifier sits between two words and could logically attach to either one, it is "squinting." Reposition it so it clearly modifies only one word.

---

## 6.3 — Dangling Modifiers: The Missing Target Problem

A dangling modifier is more severe than a misplaced modifier. In a dangling modifier construction, the word the modifier is supposed to describe **does not appear in the sentence at all**. The modifier is left "dangling" with nothing logical to attach to.

### 6.3.1 — The Classic Dangling Modifier Pattern

The most common dangling modifier on the ACT involves an **introductory participial phrase** (a phrase beginning with an -ing or -ed verb form) followed by a comma and then a subject that is **not** the logical doer of the action in the phrase.

> *Running down the street, the trees were beautiful.*

Analysis: The participial phrase "Running down the street" must describe the subject that follows the comma. The subject is "the trees." But trees cannot run down the street. The modifier is dangling—there is no person in the sentence who is running.

**Correct:** *Running down the street, I noticed the beautiful trees.*

Now "I" is the subject, and "I" is logically the one running.

**Correct:** *As I was running down the street, the trees were beautiful.*

By converting the participial phrase into a full dependent clause with its own subject ("I"), the dangling problem is resolved.

### 6.3.2 — The Diagnostic Test for Dangling Modifiers

When you encounter an introductory phrase followed by a comma, perform this three-step diagnostic:

**Step 1:** Identify the action implied by the introductory phrase. What is the action? Who or what would logically perform it?

**Step 2:** Look at the subject immediately after the comma. Ask: **Is this subject the logical doer of the action?**

**Step 3:** If the answer is NO, the modifier is dangling. The correct answer will either:
- (a) Replace the subject with the logical doer, or
- (b) Convert the introductory phrase into a full clause with its own subject.

**Example:**

> *Having finished the assignment, the TV was turned on.*

Step 1: The action is "having finished the assignment." Who finishes an assignment? A person.

Step 2: The subject after the comma is "the TV." Does a TV finish assignments? No.

Step 3: The modifier is dangling.

**Correct:** *Having finished the assignment, I turned on the TV.*

### 6.3.3 — Dangling Modifiers with Passive Voice

The ACT frequently creates dangling modifiers by pairing an active participial phrase with a passive-voice main clause:

> *After reading the book, the movie seemed disappointing.*

Analysis: "After reading the book" implies a person did the reading. But the subject is "the movie," and a movie cannot read.

**Correct:** *After reading the book, I found the movie disappointing.*

**Correct:** *After I read the book, the movie seemed disappointing.*

**Key Insight:** When the main clause is in passive voice, the grammatical subject is the **receiver** of the action, not the doer. This creates a natural trap for dangling modifiers, because participial phrases require an **active doer** as the subject.

### 6.3.4 — Dangling Modifiers with "Upon" and "On"

> *Upon entering the room, the chandelier caught my eye.*

This is technically acceptable in some informal contexts, but on the ACT, it can be problematic if the subject is not the logical entrant. The phrase "upon entering the room" needs a person as the subject.

**Correct:** *Upon entering the room, I noticed the chandelier.*

### 6.3.5 — Dangling Modifiers at the End of Sentences

While most dangling modifiers appear at the beginning of sentences, they can also occur at the end:

> *The essay was finally completed, working late into the night.*

Analysis: "Working late into the night" is a participial phrase that should describe the person who worked. But the subject is "the essay," and an essay cannot work.

**Correct:** *Working late into the night, I finally completed the essay.*

**Correct:** *I finally completed the essay, working late into the night.*

In the second correct version, the participial phrase at the end of the sentence modifies "I," which is the subject of the main clause. This works because "I" is present in the sentence and is the logical doer.

### 6.3.6 — The "Having Been" and "Being" Traps

The ACT loves to use "having been" and "being" in introductory phrases to create dangling modifiers:

> *Having been built in 1920, the architect considered the building a masterpiece.*

Analysis: "Having been built in 1920" should describe the building. But the subject is "the architect," and the architect was not built in 1920.

**Correct:** *Having been built in 1920, the building was considered a masterpiece by the architect.*

> *Being a careful researcher, the data was double-checked.*

Analysis: "Being a careful researcher" should describe a person. But the subject is "the data," and data cannot be a researcher.

**Correct:** *Being a careful researcher, she double-checked the data.*

---

## 6.4 — The "Modifier Front Flip" Hack

This is one of the most powerful diagnostic techniques for modifier questions on the ACT. It works as follows:

**When a modifier question has a comma before the blank and each answer choice starts with a different subject or modifier, "flip" the answer choices to the beginning of the sentence to see which one makes logical sense.**

**Example:**

> *__________, the audience gave a standing ovation.*

> A) Moved by the performance
> B) Moving the performance
> C) The performance moving
> D) The performance was moved

**Front Flip Test:** Try each answer choice as the introductory phrase:

- A) *Moved by the performance, the audience gave a standing ovation.* → The audience was moved by the performance. Logical.
- B) *Moving the performance, the audience gave a standing ovation.* → The audience was moving the performance? Illogical.
- C) *The performance moving, the audience gave a standing ovation.* → Awkward and ungrammatical.
- D) *The performance was moved, the audience gave a standing ovation.* → Comma splice; also illogical.

The front flip immediately reveals A as the correct answer.

---

## 6.5 — Limiting Modifiers: A Deep Dive

Limiting modifiers are words that **restrict or limit** the meaning of the word they modify. The most common ones tested on the ACT are:

- **only**
- **almost**
- **nearly**
- **just**
- **even**
- **merely**
- **hardly**
- **scarcely**
- **barely**

Each of these words must be placed **immediately before** the word it limits. Misplacement creates meaning shifts that the ACT exploits.

### 6.5.1 — "Only" in Detail

The word "only" can function as an adjective (meaning "sole") or as an adverb (meaning "exclusively"). Its placement determines what it restricts:

| Placement | Meaning |
|---|---|
| *Only John stole the money.* | No one else stole the money. |
| *John only stole the money.* | John stole nothing else. |
| *John stole only the money.* | John stole the money and nothing else. |

On the ACT, the context will always signal which meaning is intended. Read the surrounding sentences carefully.

### 6.5.2 — "Almost" vs. "Nearly"

Both "almost" and "nearly" mean "very close to but not completely." They are interchangeable in most contexts, but their placement must still follow the proximity principle:

**Incorrect:** *I almost ran ten miles.* (I didn't run them; I almost ran them.)

**Correct:** *I ran almost ten miles.* (I ran, and the distance was nearly ten miles.)

### 6.5.3 — "Even," "Just," and "Merely"

These words are often used for emphasis and must be placed before the word they emphasize:

**Incorrect:** *She even speaks three languages.* (Ambiguous: what else does she do?)

**Correct:** *She speaks even three languages.* (Emphasizes the surprising number.)

**Incorrect:** *I just need a minute.* (Ambiguous: "just" could mean "only" or "recently.")

**Correct:** *I need just a minute.* (Clearly means "only a minute.")

---

## 6.6 — Absolute Phrases: A Special Modifier Category

An absolute phrase is a modifier that consists of a **noun + participle** (or noun + adjective) and modifies the **entire main clause** rather than a single word. Absolute phrases are set off by commas and are grammatically independent of the main clause.

**Examples:**

- *The sun having set, we headed home.* (Noun + present participle)
- *His work finished, he went to bed.* (Noun + past participle)
- *Her eyes filled with tears, she spoke.* (Noun + adjective phrase)

Absolute phrases are **not** dangling modifiers because they do not modify a single noun—they modify the entire situation described in the main clause. The ACT rarely tests absolute phrases as errors, but you should be able to recognize them so you don't mistake them for dangling modifiers.

**Key Distinction:** A dangling modifier fails because there is no logical subject for it. An absolute phrase succeeds because it describes the **circumstances** of the whole clause.

---

## 6.7 — Summary: The Modifier Diagnostic Checklist

When you encounter an underlined portion that includes or is adjacent to a modifier, run through this checklist:

1. **Identify the modifier.** What word, phrase, or clause is doing the describing?
2. **Identify the modified.** What word is the modifier supposed to describe?
3. **Check proximity.** Is the modifier as close as possible to the modified word?
4. **Check for ambiguity.** Could the modifier logically describe any other word in the sentence?
5. **Check for dangling.** If the modifier is an introductory phrase, is the subject after the comma the logical doer of the action?
6. **Check limiting words.** If the modifier is a limiting word (*only, almost, nearly, just*), is it placed immediately before the word it limits?

If any of these checks fail, the modifier is in error, and you must find the answer choice that corrects the specific problem.

---

## PART II: PARALLEL STRUCTURE — THE SYMMETRY REQUIREMENT

---

## 6.8 — The Core Principle of Parallelism

Parallel structure (also called parallelism) is the grammatical requirement that **items in a series, list, pair, or comparison must be in the same grammatical form**. If you list three activities, all three must be nouns, or all three must be gerunds, or all three must be infinitives—but not a mixture. If you compare two things, both sides of the comparison must be the same part of speech.

This principle is not merely a stylistic preference; it is a **grammatical rule**, and the ACT tests it relentlessly.

**The Parallelism Formula:** If items are connected by a coordinating conjunction (*and, or, nor*), a correlative conjunction (*either...or, neither...nor, not only...but also, both...and*), or are in a list, they must share the **same grammatical form**.

---

## 6.9 — Parallelism in Lists and Series

### 6.9.1 — Gerunds vs. Infinitives in Lists

One of the most common parallelism errors on the ACT involves mixing gerunds (-ing forms) and infinitives (to + verb) in a list.
