**Incorrect:** *She likes swimming, to run, and biking.*

Analysis: The list contains three items connected by "and":
1. swimming (gerund)
2. to run (infinitive)
3. biking (gerund)

These forms are not parallel.

**Correct (all gerunds):** *She likes swimming, running, and biking.*

**Correct (all infinitives):** *She likes to swim, to run, and to bike.*

**ACT Note:** Both correct versions are acceptable. The ACT will not make you choose between gerunds and infinitives on the basis of style alone. Instead, it will present one parallel option and one or more non-parallel options. Your job is to identify the non-parallel one and eliminate it.

### 6.9.2 — Parallelism with Noun Phrases

**Incorrect:** *The job requires experience in marketing, a degree in business, and you must have strong communication skills.*

Analysis: The three items in the list are:
1. experience in marketing (noun phrase)
2. a degree in business (noun phrase)
3. you must have strong communication skills (independent clause)

The third item breaks the parallelism.

**Correct:** *The job requires experience in marketing, a degree in business, and strong communication skills.*

Now all three items are noun phrases.

### 6.9.3 — Parallelism with Verb Phrases

**Incorrect:** *The company plans to expand its operations, reducing costs, and the improvement of customer service.*

Analysis: The three items are:
1. to expand its operations (infinitive phrase)
2. reducing costs (gerund phrase)
3. the improvement of customer service (noun phrase)

Three different grammatical forms in one list.

**Correct (all infinitive phrases):** *The company plans to expand its operations, to reduce costs, and to improve customer service.*

**Correct (all gerund phrases):** *The company plans on expanding its operations, reducing costs, and improving customer service.*

### 6.9.4 — Parallelism with Adjective Phrases

**Incorrect:** *The movie was thought-provoking, visually stunning, and it made people cry.*

Analysis: The three items are:
1. thought-provoking (adjective)
2. visually stunning (adjective phrase)
3. it made people cry (independent clause)

**Correct:** *The movie was thought-provoking, visually stunning, and emotionally devastating.*

---

## 6.10 — Parallelism with Correlative Conjunctions

Correlative conjunctions are pairs that work together to connect parallel elements. The most common pairs are:

- **either...or**
- **neither...nor**
- **not only...but also**
- **both...and**
- **whether...or**

The rule is absolute: **whatever grammatical form follows the first conjunction must also follow the second conjunction.**

### 6.10.1 — Either...Or

**Incorrect:** *Either you leave now or to call the police.*

Analysis: After "either," we have a clause ("you leave now"). After "or," we have an infinitive ("to call the police"). These are not parallel.

**Correct:** *Either you leave now, or I call the police.* (Both clauses)

**Correct:** *Either leaving now or calling the police is an option.* (Both gerunds as subjects)

### 6.10.2 — Not Only...But Also

**Incorrect:** *She not only is a talented singer but also plays the guitar.*

Analysis: After "not only," we have a verb phrase ("is a talented singer"). After "but also," we have a verb phrase ("plays the guitar"). These are actually parallel (both are verb phrases), but the placement of "not only" can create awkwardness. The ACT prefers:

**Correct:** *She is not only a talented singer but also a skilled guitarist.*

Now both sides are noun phrases: "a talented singer" / "a skilled guitarist."

**Incorrect:** *He not only scored the winning goal but the game-winning assist also.*

This breaks parallelism because the second part has a different structure.

**Correct:** *He scored not only the winning goal but also the game-winning assist.*

Now both sides are noun phrases: "the winning goal" / "the game-winning assist."

### 6.10.3 — Both...And

**Incorrect:** *The program is both innovative and it costs a lot of money.*

Analysis: After "both," we have an adjective ("innovative"). After "and," we have a clause ("it costs a lot of money"). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *The program is both innovative and expensive.* (Both adjectives)

**Correct:** *The program both innovates and costs a lot of money.* (Both verbs)

### 6.10.4 — The "Mirror Image" Test

For any correlative conjunction, apply the **Mirror Image Test**: draw an imaginary line at the conjunction pair. The grammatical structure on the left must **mirror** the structure on the right.

**Example:**

> *She is known **not only** for her academic excellence **but also** for her athletic achievements.*

Left of "not only...but also": "for her academic excellence" (prepositional phrase)
Right of "not only...but also": "for her athletic achievements" (prepositional phrase)

Parallel. Both sides are prepositional phrases with the same internal structure.

**Incorrect Example:**

> *She is known **not only** for her academic excellence **but also** she is a great athlete.*

Left: "for her academic excellence" (prepositional phrase)
Right: "she is a great athlete" (independent clause)

Not parallel.

---

## 6.11 — Parallelism in Comparisons

When comparing two things using "than" or "as," the items being compared must be in the same grammatical form.

**Incorrect:** *Running is healthier than to swim.*

Analysis: "Running" (gerund) is being compared to "to swim" (infinitive). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *Running is healthier than swimming.* (Both gerunds)

**Correct:** *To run is healthier than to swim.* (Both infinitives)

**Incorrect:** *She works faster than me works.*

While this is technically grammatical, the ACT prefers:

**Correct:** *She works faster than I do.* (Both clauses with subject + verb)

**Incorrect:** *The population of China is larger than the United States.*

Analysis: "The population of China" is being compared to "the United States." You cannot compare a population to a country.

**Correct:** *The population of China is larger than that of the United States.*

**Correct:** *China has a larger population than the United States.*

---

## 6.12 — Parallelism with "And," "Or," and "Nor"

Even without correlative conjunctions, simple coordinating conjunctions demand parallelism:

**Incorrect:** *He enjoys reading, writing, and to paint.*

Analysis: "reading" (gerund), "writing" (gerund), "to paint" (infinitive). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *He enjoys reading, writing, and painting.* (All gerunds)

**Correct:** *He enjoys to read, to write, and to paint.* (All infinitives)

**Incorrect:** *The teacher asked us to read the chapter, taking notes, and that we should write a summary.*

Analysis: "to read the chapter" (infinitive), "taking notes" (gerund), "that we should write a summary" (noun clause). Three different forms.

**Correct:** *The teacher asked us to read the chapter, to take notes, and to write a summary.* (All infinitives)

---

## 6.13 — Parallelism with "Rather Than" and "Other Than"

These phrases also require parallelism:

**Incorrect:** *He prefers running rather than to swim.*

**Correct:** *He prefers running rather than swimming.* (Both gerunds)

**Incorrect:** *She did nothing other than to complain.*

**Correct:** *She did nothing other than complain.* (Both bare infinitives—"other than" takes the base form)

---

## 6.14 — The "List Audit" Diagnostic Method

When you encounter a sentence with a list or series, perform a **List Audit**:

1. **Identify the list.** Find all items connected by commas and a conjunction (usually "and" or "or").
2. **Label the form of each item.** Is each item a noun, gerund, infinitive, adjective, clause, prepositional phrase, etc.?
3. **Check for consistency.** Are all items in the same form?
4. **If not parallel, find the answer choice that makes them parallel.**

**Example:**

> *The hiker enjoyed climbing the mountain, the view from the top, and to rest in the shade.*

List:
1. climbing the mountain (gerund phrase)
2. the view from the top (noun phrase)
3. to rest in the shade (infinitive phrase)

Three different forms. Not parallel.

**Correct:** *The hiker enjoyed climbing the mountain, admiring the view from the top, and resting in the shade.* (All gerund phrases)

---

## 6.15 — Parallelism with Mixed Parts of Speech

The ACT sometimes tests parallelism with mixed parts of speech—for example, mixing adjectives and adverbs in a list:

**Incorrect:** *She spoke softly, with kindness, and used patience.*

Analysis: "softly" (adverb), "with kindness" (prepositional phrase), "used patience" (verb + noun). Three different forms.

**Correct:** *She spoke softly, kindly, and patiently.* (All adverbs)

---

## 6.16 — Parallelism in "From...To" Constructions

The "from...to" construction requires parallel elements on both sides:

**Incorrect:** *The temperature ranged from hot to freezing cold.*

Analysis: "hot" (adjective) vs. "freezing cold" (adjective + adjective). While this might seem acceptable, the ACT prefers strict parallelism.

**Correct:** *The temperature ranged from hot to cold.* (Both simple adjectives)

**Incorrect:** *She went from running a company to retirement.*

Analysis: "running a company" (gerund phrase) vs. "retirement" (noun). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *She went from running a company to enjoying retirement.* (Both gerund phrases)

---

## 6.17 — The "Parallelism Trap" in Answer Choices

The ACT uses several predictable traps in parallelism questions:

**Trap 1: The "Almost Parallel" Choice**
One answer choice will have items that are *almost* parallel but not quite. For example, two gerunds and one infinitive. Eliminate this immediately.

**Trap 2: The "Different Meaning" Choice**
One answer choice will be parallel but change the meaning of the sentence. Parallelism is necessary but not sufficient—the correct answer must also preserve the original meaning.

**Trap 3: The "Grammatically Correct but Not Parallel" Choice**
Sometimes the original sentence is grammatically acceptable in informal English but violates strict parallelism. The ACT always enforces strict parallelism.

**Trap 4: The "Overcorrection" Choice**
One answer choice will make the items parallel but introduce a different error (e.g., subject-verb agreement, wrong word choice). Always check the entire sentence, not just the list.

---

## 6.18 — Parallelism with "As Well As," "Along With," and "In Addition To"

These phrases do **not** create compound subjects (they don't make a singular subject plural), but they **do** require parallelism in the elements they connect:

**Incorrect:** *She is a talented musician as well as excels in mathematics.*

Analysis: "a talented musician" (noun phrase) vs. "excels in mathematics" (verb phrase). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *She is a talented musician as well as a brilliant mathematician.* (Both noun phrases)

---

## PART III: COMPARATIVE LOGIC — THE RULES OF FAIR COMPARISON

---

## 6.19 — The Core Principle of Comparative Logic

Comparisons on the ACT must be **logical, clear, and grammatically parallel**. The test checks for three main types of comparative errors:

1. **Illogical comparisons** (comparing unlike things)
2. **Double comparatives/superlatives** (using "more" + "-er" or "most" + "-est")
3. **Incomplete comparisons** (leaving the comparison ambiguous)

We examine each in exhaustive detail.

---

## 6.20 — Illogical Comparisons: Comparing Unlike Things

### 6.20.1 — The "Apples and Oranges" Problem

The most fundamental rule of comparative logic is that you must compare **like things to like things**. You cannot compare a person to a place, a quality to an object, or a group to a different category.

**Incorrect:** *My car is faster than you.*

Analysis: "My car" (an object) is being compared to "you" (a person). A car cannot be faster than a person in a literal sense, and the comparison is illogical.

**Correct:** *My car is faster than yours.* (Comparing car to car—"yours" means "your car.")

**Incorrect:** *The climate of Florida is warmer than New York.*

Analysis: "The climate of Florida" is being compared to "New York" (a state). You cannot compare a climate to a state.

**Correct:** *The climate of Florida is warmer than that of New York.* (Comparing climate to climate—"that" stands for "the climate.")

**Correct:** *Florida has a warmer climate than New York.* (Comparing Florida to New York—now both are states, and the comparison is logical because "climate" is implied.)

### 6.20.2 — The "That/Those" Substitution Rule

When comparing a singular noun to another singular noun, use **"that"** to stand for the repeated noun. When comparing a plural noun to another plural noun, use **"those."**

| Comparison | Substitution Word |
|---|---|
| Singular noun vs. singular noun | **that** |
| Plural noun vs. plural noun | **those** |

**Examples:**

- *The price of gasoline is higher than **that** of diesel.* (Singular: price → that)
- *The students in this class are more motivated than **those** in the other class.* (Plural: students → those)
- *Her singing voice is more beautiful than **that** of her sister.* (Singular: voice → that)

**ACT Note:** The words "that" and "those" in these constructions are **not** relative pronouns. They are **demonstrative pronouns** standing in for the noun that would otherwise be repeated. The ACT tests this distinction regularly.

### 6.20.3 — The "One of the" Rule

The construction "one of the [superlative]..." requires a **plural noun** after the superlative:

**Incorrect:** *She is one of the most intelligent **student** in the program.*

Analysis: "One of the" implies she is one member of a group. The group must be plural.

**Correct:** *She is one of the most intelligent **students** in the program.*

**Incorrect:** *This is one of the most beautiful **painting** I've ever seen.*

**Correct:** *This is one of the most beautiful **paintings** I've ever seen.*

**Diagnostic Method:** When you see "one of the [superlative]," check whether the noun immediately following the superlative is plural. If it is singular, it is wrong.

### 6.20.4 — The "Any Other" Rule

When comparing a member of a group to the rest of the group, you must use **"any other"** to exclude the subject from the group it is being compared to:

**Incorrect:** *He is smarter than any student in his class.*

Analysis: He is a student in his class. If he is smarter than "any student in his class," he is smarter than himself, which is illogical.

**Correct:** *He is smarter than **any other** student in his class.*

By adding "other," we exclude him from the group he is comparing himself to.

**Incorrect:** *This painting is more valuable than any painting in the gallery.*

**Correct:** *This painting is more valuable than **any other** painting in the gallery.*

**Key Insight:** This rule applies whenever the subject is a **member of the group** being compared to. If the subject is **not** a member of the group, "any" (without "other") is correct:

**Correct:** *This painting is more valuable than any painting in the museum.* (If "this painting" is not in the museum, no "other" is needed.)

---

## 6.21 — Double Comparatives and Superlatives

The English language has two ways to form comparatives and superlatives:

- **Short words (one syllable, some two syllables):** Add -er / -est
  - tall → taller → tallest
  - fast → faster → fastest
  - happy → happier → happiest

- **Long words (two or more syllables):** Use more / most
  - beautiful → more beautiful → most beautiful
  - interesting → more interesting → most interesting
  - careful → more careful → most careful

**You may NEVER combine both methods.**

**Incorrect:** *She is more taller than her brother.*

**Correct:** *She is taller than her brother.*

**Incorrect:** *This is the most easiest problem on the test.*

**Correct:** *This is the easiest problem on the test.*

**Incorrect:** *He is the most happiest person I know.*

**Correct:** *He is the happiest person I know.*

**ACT Note:** This error is so obviously wrong that the ACT rarely uses it as the sole error in a question. However, it frequently appears as **one of multiple errors** in an answer choice, and recognizing it quickly helps you eliminate wrong options.

### 6.21.1 — Irregular Comparative and Superlative Forms

Some common adjectives have irregular comparative and superlative forms that must be memorized:

| Positive | Comparative | Superlative |
|---|---|---|
| good/well | better | best |
| bad/badly | worse | worst |
| little | less | least |
| much/many | more | most |
| far | farther/further | farthest/furthest |
| old | older/elder | oldest/eldest |

**Incorrect:** *This is the most best solution.*

**Correct:** *This is the best solution.*

**Incorrect:** *She performed more worse than expected.*

**Correct:** *She performed worse than expected.*

---

## 6.22 — Incomplete Comparisons

An incomplete comparison occurs when one side of the comparison is missing, making it unclear what is being compared.

**Incorrect:** *She works harder than anyone.*

Analysis: Harder than anyone? Anyone in what context? The comparison is incomplete.

**Correct:** *She works harder than anyone else in the department.*

**Incorrect:** *This car is more expensive.*

Analysis: More expensive than what? The comparison has no endpoint.

**Correct:** *This car is more expensive than that one.*

**ACT Note:** The ACT sometimes tests incomplete comparisons in the context of "than" vs. "then." If the sentence involves a comparison, use "than." If it involves time or sequence, use "then."

---

## 6.23 — Comparative Logic with "Than" vs. "Then"

This is a word-choice error that overlaps with comparative logic:

- **Than** is a conjunction used in comparisons.
- **Then** is an adverb referring to time or sequence.

**Incorrect:** *She is taller then her brother.*

**Correct:** *She is taller than her brother.*

**Incorrect:** *I went to the store, than I went home.*

**Correct:** *I went to the store, then I went home.*

**Diagnostic Method:** If the word introduces a comparison (one thing being more/less than another), use "than." If it indicates what happens next in a sequence, use "then."

---

## 6.24 — Comparative Logic with "Between" vs. "Among"

- **Between** is used for **two** distinct items or groups.
- **Among** is used for **three or more** items or groups.

**Incorrect:** *The prize was divided between the five winners.*

**Correct:** *The prize was divided among the five winners.*

**Incorrect:** *She had to choose among two options.*

**Correct:** *She had to choose between two options.*

**ACT Note:** The ACT sometimes tests this in combination with other errors. Always check whether the number of items matches the preposition used.

---

## 6.25 — Comparative Logic with "Different From" vs. "Different Than"

- **Different from** is always correct on the ACT.
- **Different than** is acceptable only when followed by a full clause (subject + verb).

**Incorrect:** *His approach is different than mine.*

**Correct:** *His approach is different from mine.*

**Correct (with clause):** *His approach is different than it used to be.* (Here, "than" introduces a clause.)

**ACT Note:** The ACT consistently prefers "different from" and will almost always treat "different than" as incorrect when it is followed by a simple noun or pronoun.

---

## 6.26 — Comparative Logic with "As...As" and "So...As"

The "as...as" construction is used for equality comparisons:

**Correct:** *She is as tall as her brother.*

**Correct:** *He works as hard as anyone.*

The "so...as" construction is used in **negative** comparisons:

**Correct:** *She is not so tall as her brother.*

**Correct:** *He doesn't work so hard as he used to.*

**ACT Note:** In affirmative comparisons, use "as...as." In negative comparisons, both "not as...as" and "not so...as" are acceptable, but the ACT rarely tests this distinction.

---

## 6.27 — Comparative Logic with "Less" vs. "Fewer"

- **Fewer** modifies **countable** nouns (things you can count individually: apples, students, dollars, books).
- **Less** modifies **uncountable** nouns (things you cannot count individually: water, time, money, information).

**Incorrect:** *There are less students in this class.*

**Correct:** *There are fewer students in this class.*

**Incorrect:** *I have fewer water in my bottle.*

**Correct:** *I have less water in my bottle.*

**ACT Note:** The ACT frequently tests this rule, especially in contexts where the noun could be ambiguous. Remember: if you can put a number before it (three students, five apples), use "fewer." If you cannot (you cannot say "three waters"), use "less."

**Exception:** "Less" is used with plural nouns referring to time, money, and distance:
- *less than five miles* (distance)
- *less than ten dollars* (money)
- *less than two hours* (time)

These are treated as collective quantities, not individual units.

---

## 6.28 — Comparative Logic with "Amount" vs. "Number"

- **Number** is used with **countable** nouns.
- **Amount** is used with **uncountable** nouns.

**Incorrect:** *The amount of students in the class has increased.*

**Correct:** *The number of students in the class has increased.*

**Incorrect:** *The number of water in the tank is decreasing.*

**Correct:** *The amount of water in the tank is decreasing.*

---

## 6.29 — Comparative Logic with "Like" vs. "As"

- **Like** is a preposition followed by a **noun, pronoun, or noun phrase**.
- **As** is a conjunction followed by a **clause** (subject + verb).

**Incorrect:** *She sings like her mother does.*

**Correct:** *She sings as her mother does.* (Clause: "her mother does")

**Incorrect:** *She sings as her mother.*

**Correct:** *She sings like her mother.* (Noun: "her mother")

**ACT Note:** This is a subtle but frequently tested distinction. The key is to check what follows "like" or "as." If it has a subject and verb, use "as." If it is just a noun, use "like."

**Extended Rule:** "As if" and "as though" introduce clauses and are always followed by subject + verb:

**Correct:** *He talks as if he knows everything.*

**Correct:** *She looked as though she had seen a ghost.*

---

## 6.30 — Comparative Logic: The "Compared To" vs. "Compared With" Distinction

While the ACT rarely tests this distinction as a primary error, it is worth noting:

- **Compare to** = liken one thing to another (metaphorical comparison)
  - *The poet compared her eyes to stars.*
- **Compare with** = analyze similarities and differences (analytical comparison)
  - *The study compared the results with previous findings.*

---

## 6.31 — Comparative Logic: The "Superior To" / "Inferior To" Pattern

Certain adjectives derived from Latin do not use "more" or "-er" and require "than" replaced by "to":

- **superior to** (not "superior than")
- **inferior to** (not "inferior than")
- **prior to** (not "prior than")
- **junior to** (not "junior than")
- **senior to** (not "senior than")
- **preferable to** (not "preferable than")

**Incorrect:** *This method is superior than the old one.*

**Correct:** *This method is superior to the old one.*

**ACT Note:** The ACT occasionally tests "prefer...to" (not "prefer...than"):

**Incorrect:** *I prefer coffee than tea.*

**Correct:** *I prefer coffee to tea.*

---

## 6.32 — Comparative Logic: The "Other" and "Else" Requirement

When comparing one member to the rest of a group, use **"other"** (as discussed in 6.20.4) or **"else"** to exclude the subject:

**Incorrect:** *He is cleverer than anyone in his class.*

**Correct:** *He is cleverer than anyone **else** in his class.*

**Incorrect:** *This restaurant is better than any in the city.*

**Correct:** *This restaurant is better than any **other** in the city.*

**Diagnostic Method:** If the subject is a member of the group being compared to, you must include "other" or "else" to exclude the subject from the comparison group.

---

## 6.33 — Comparative Logic: The "Those of" and "That of" Construction

When comparing possessive forms, use "that of" or "those of" to avoid illogical comparisons:

**Incorrect:** *Her voice is louder than her sister.*

Analysis: "Her voice" is being compared to "her sister" (a person). You cannot compare a voice to a person.

**Correct:** *Her voice is louder than **that of** her sister.* (Comparing voice to voice)

**Incorrect:** *The colors of the sunset are more vibrant than the ocean.*

**Correct:** *The colors of the sunset are more vibrant than **those of** the ocean.* (Comparing colors to colors)

---

## 6.34 — Comparative Logic: The "Do" Substitution in Comparative Clauses

In formal English, when the main verb is repeated in the comparison, we often substitute "do" (does, did) for the verb:

**Correct:** *She works as hard as I **do**.*

**Correct:** *She works harder than I **do**.*

**Correct:** *She worked harder than I **did**.*

This is not required, but it is grammatically correct and sometimes appears in ACT answer choices. Do not eliminate an answer choice solely because it includes "do" or "did" after the subject in a comparison.

---

## 6.35 — Comparative Logic: The "Than Who" and "Than Whom" Construction

When "than" is followed by a clause, it can function as a relative pronoun:

**Correct:** *She works harder than I do.*

**Correct:** *He is more talented than I am.*

**Correct:** *No one is more dedicated than she is.*

When "than" is followed by a relative pronoun, use "who" (subject) or "whom" (object):

**Correct:** *She is someone **than whom** no one is more qualified.* (Formal; "whom" is the object of "than")

**ACT Note:** This construction is rare on the ACT, but you should recognize it as grammatically correct.

---

## 6.36 — Comparative Logic: Summary of Common Errors

| Error | Incorrect | Correct |
|---|---|---|
| Comparing unlike things | *My car is faster than you.* | *My car is faster than yours.* |
| Missing "that/those" | *The climate of Florida is warmer than New York.* | *The climate of Florida is warmer than that of New York.* |
| Missing "other/else" | *He is smarter than any student in his class.* | *He is smarter than any other student in his class.* |
| Double comparative | *more better* | *better* |
| Double superlative | *most easiest* | *easiest* |
| Wrong preposition | *superior than* | *superior to* |
| Less/fewer confusion | *less students* | *fewer students* |
| Like/as confusion | *She sings like her mother does.* | *She sings as her mother does.* |
| Different from/than | *different than mine* | *different from mine* |
| Between/among | *between five winners* | *among five winners* |
| Amount/number | *amount of students* | *number of students* |

---

## PART IV: INTEGRATED DIAGNOSTIC METHODS

---

## 6.37 — The "Three-Pass" Diagnostic System

When you encounter an underlined portion that could involve modifiers, parallelism, or comparisons, use this three-pass system:

**Pass 1: Identify the Error Type**
- Is there a modifier? → Check for misplaced or dangling modifiers.
- Is there a list, series, or paired elements? → Check for parallelism.
- Is there a comparison? → Check for comparative logic.

**Pass 2: Apply the Specific Diagnostic**
- For modifiers: Use the Golden Proximity Principle and the Dangling Modifier Test.
- For parallelism: Use the List Audit and the Mirror Image Test.
- For comparisons: Use the Like-Things-to-Like-Things Test and the "Other/Else" Check.

**Pass 3: Verify the Answer**
- Does the correct answer fix the specific error?
- Does it preserve the original meaning?
- Does it introduce any new errors?

---

## 6.38 — The "Answer Choice Comparison" Technique

Before selecting an answer, compare all four choices side by side. Identify what changes between them:

- If the choices differ in **modifier placement**, the question tests modifier rules.
- If the choices differ in **grammatical form** (gerund vs. infinitive, noun vs. clause), the question tests parallelism.
- If the choices differ in **comparison words** (than vs. then, fewer vs. less, that vs. those), the question tests comparative logic.

This technique allows you to identify the **rule being tested** before you even begin analyzing the sentence, giving you a significant strategic advantage.

---

## 6.39 — The "Read Beyond" Rule

For all three categories in this chapter, you must read **beyond the underlined portion** to determine the correct answer:

- **Modifiers:** Read the entire sentence to identify what the modifier is supposed to describe.
- **Parallelism:** Read the entire list or series to identify all items that must be parallel.
- **Comparisons:** Read both sides of the comparison to ensure the items being compared are logical and parallel.

Never make a decision based solely on the underlined portion. Context is everything.

---

## 6.40 — The "Shorter is Better" Caveat Does NOT Apply Here

In wordiness and redundancy questions, the shortest answer is usually correct. However, in modifier, parallelism, and comparison questions, the correct answer is sometimes **longer** than the incorrect ones because it includes necessary words (like "that," "those," "other," "else," or parallel structures) that make the sentence logical and complete.

**Example:**

> *The population of China is larger than New York.*

The correct answer is:

> *The population of China is larger than **that of** New York.*

This answer is longer, but it is correct because it includes the necessary "that of" to make the comparison logical. Do not apply the "shorter is better" rule to these question types.

---

## 6.41 — The "Grammar vs. Logic" Distinction

Modifier placement, parallelism, and comparative logic all involve **both grammar and logic**. A sentence can be grammatically correct (no rule violations in terms of parts of speech) but logically flawed (comparing unlike things, modifying the wrong word).

**Example:**

> *Walking through the park, the flowers were beautiful.*

This sentence is grammatically well-formed: it has a subject ("the flowers"), a verb ("were"), and a participial phrase ("walking through the park"). But it is **logically flawed** because flowers cannot walk through a park.

The ACT tests this distinction constantly. Always check **both** grammar and logic.

---

## 6.42 — The "Redundancy in Parallelism" Trap

Sometimes the ACT presents a sentence where the parallelism error is disguised by redundancy:

**Incorrect:** *The project requires careful planning, detailed research, and it must be executed with precision.*

The third item ("it must be executed with precision") is an independent clause, breaking the parallelism with the first two items (noun phrases). The correct answer:

**Correct:** *The project requires careful planning, detailed research, and precise execution.*

Now all three items are noun phrases. Notice that the correct answer is also **more concise**—this is one of the rare cases where parallelism and conciseness align.

---

## 6.43 — The "Implied Parallelism" Problem

Sometimes the parallelism is implied rather than explicit:

**Incorrect:** *She is talented, intelligent, and works hard.*

The first two items are adjectives ("talented," "intelligent"). The third is a verb phrase ("works hard"). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *She is talented, intelligent, and hardworking.* (All adjectives)

**Correct:** *She has talent, intelligence, and dedication.* (All nouns)

The key is to ensure that **every item in the series** is the same part of speech or grammatical form.

---

## 6.44 — The "Parallelism in Subordinate Clauses" Problem

Parallelism errors can also occur within subordinate clauses:

**Incorrect:** *He said that he would arrive early and to bring the documents.*

Analysis: "that he would arrive early" (clause) and "to bring the documents" (infinitive phrase). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *He said that he would arrive early and that he would bring the documents.* (Both clauses)

**Correct:** *He promised to arrive early and to bring the documents.* (Both infinitives)

---

## 6.45 — The "Comparative Logic in Complex Sentences" Problem

In complex sentences with multiple clauses, comparative logic errors can be harder to spot:

**Incorrect:** *The report concluded that the new drug was more effective than the placebo, which surprised the researchers.*

Analysis: The comparison is between "the new drug" and "the placebo," which is logical. But the relative clause "which surprised the researchers" is ambiguous—does "which" refer to the conclusion, the effectiveness, or the comparison?

**Correct:** *The report concluded that the new drug was more effective than the placebo—a finding that surprised the researchers.*

This revision eliminates the ambiguous pronoun and clarifies the comparison.

---

## PART V: ADVANCED CONCEPTS AND EDGE CASES

---

## 6.46 — Stacked Modifiers

Sometimes multiple modifiers apply to the same noun, creating a "stack":

> *The tall man in the blue coat with the red hat walked quickly.*

Here, "tall," "in the blue coat," and "with the red hat" all modify "man." The order of stacked modifiers follows a general convention:

1. **Determiners:** a, the, this, my, three
2. **Opinion/Quality:** beautiful, tall, interesting
3. **Size:** tall, small, large
4. **Shape:** round, square, thin
5. **Age:** old, new, young
6. **Color:** blue, red, green
7. **Origin:** American, French, Asian
8. **Material:** wooden, silk, metal
9. **Purpose/Qualifier:** writing (as in "writing desk"), sleeping (as in "sleeping bag")
10. **Noun being modified**

While the ACT rarely tests modifier order directly, being aware of this hierarchy can help you identify when a modifier is in an unusual position.

---

## 6.47 — The "Only" Placement Paradox

In some sentences, "only" can be placed in multiple positions without changing the meaning:

- *I only told John.* = *I told only John.* (In both cases, the meaning is that John is the sole person I told.)

But in other sentences, the placement changes the meaning:

- *I only eat meat on Sundays.* (I do nothing else with meat on Sundays.)
- *I eat only meat on Sundays.* (I eat nothing else on Sundays.)
- *I eat meat only on Sundays.* (I eat meat on no other day.)

**ACT Strategy:** When "only" appears in the underlined portion, read the surrounding context to determine the intended meaning, then select the placement that matches.

---

## 6.48 — Parallelism with "To" in Infinitive Lists

When listing infinitives, the "to" can be repeated for emphasis or omitted for conciseness:

**Correct (repeated):** *She likes to swim, to run, and to bike.*

**Correct (omitted after first):** *She likes to swim, run, and bike.*

Both are grammatically correct. The ACT will not test this as an error. However, if the "to" is omitted from the first item but included in later items, that is an error:

**Incorrect:** *She likes swim, to run, and to bike.*

---

## 6.49 — The "Comparative with Possessive Pronouns" Problem

When comparing possessive forms, be careful to compare like things:

**Incorrect:** *My car is faster than you.*

**Correct:** *My car is faster than yours.*

**Incorrect:** *Her ideas are more creative than you.*

**Correct:** *Her ideas are more creative than yours.*

**Incorrect:** *Our house is bigger than your.*

**Correct:** *Our house is bigger than yours.*

The possessive pronouns (yours, hers, ours, theirs, its) stand for the noun they modify, ensuring the comparison is between like things.

---

## 6.50 — The "Comparative with Possessive Nouns" Problem

The same rule applies to possessive nouns:

**Incorrect:** *My car is faster than John.*

**Correct:** *My car is faster than John's.*

**Incorrect:** *Her voice is more beautiful than her sister.*

**Correct:** *Her voice is more beautiful than her sister's.*

---

## 6.51 — The "Parallelism with Mixed Voice" Problem

Parallelism can be broken by mixing active and passive voice in a series:

**Incorrect:** *The team designed the product, tested the prototype, and the final version was launched by them.*

Analysis: The first two items are active voice ("designed," "tested"). The third is passive voice ("was launched"). Not parallel.

**Correct:** *The team designed the product, tested the prototype, and launched the final version.* (All active)

---

## 6.52 — The "Comparative with Collective Nouns" Problem

When comparing collective nouns, ensure the comparison is logical:

**Incorrect:** *The team in New York is larger than Los Angeles.*

**Correct:** *The team in New York is larger than the team in Los Angeles.*

**Correct:** *The team in New York is larger than that in Los Angeles.*

---

## 6.53 — The "Modifier with Negative Constructions" Problem

Modifiers can interact with negative constructions to create meaning shifts:

**Incorrect:** *She hardly never misses a practice.*

Analysis: "Hardly" and "never" are both negatives, creating a double negative.

**Correct:** *She hardly ever misses a practice.*

**Correct:** *She never misses a practice.*

---

## 6.54 — The "Parallelism with Relative Clauses" Problem

When relative clauses are part of a series, they must be parallel:

**Incorrect:** *I met a man who is a doctor, a woman who teaches, and a lawyer.*

The third item ("a lawyer") lacks a relative clause, breaking the parallelism.

**Correct:** *I met a man who is a doctor, a woman who teaches, and a lawyer who won the case.*

---

## 6.55 — The "Comparative with Elliptical Clauses" Problem

In elliptical clauses, some words are omitted but understood:

**Correct:** *She works harder than I do.* (Full: *than I do work hard*)

**Correct:** *She works harder than I.* (Formal: *than I work hard*)

**Incorrect:** *She works harder than me work hard.*

**Correct:** *She works harder than me.* (Informal but accepted on the ACT in some contexts)

**ACT Note:** The ACT generally prefers the formal version with "do" or the verb repeated, but it will not treat the informal version (with "me") as a primary error. The key is to ensure the comparison is logical and the grammatical forms are parallel.

---

## PART VI: COMPREHENSIVE SUMMARY AND QUICK-REFERENCE TABLES

---

## 6.56 — Modifier Placement: Quick-Reference Table

| Error Type | Description | Example (Incorrect) | Correction |
|---|---|---|---|
| Misplaced modifier | Modifier too far from the word it describes | *She almost drove her kids to school every day.* | *She drove her kids to school almost every day.* |
| Dangling modifier | Modifier has no logical subject | *Running down the street, the trees were beautiful.* | *Running down the street, I admired the beautiful trees.* |
| Squinting modifier | Modifier could describe two different words | *Students who study occasionally get good grades.* | *Students who occasionally study get good grades.* |
| Limiting word misplacement | "Only," "almost," etc. in the wrong position | *I only eat pizza on Fridays.* | *I eat only pizza on Fridays.* |

---

## 6.57 — Parallel Structure: Quick-Reference Table

| Error Type | Description | Example (Incorrect) | Correction |
|---|---|---|---|
| Mixed gerund/infinitive | Different verb forms in a list | *She likes swimming, to run, and biking.* | *She likes swimming, running, and biking.* |
| Mixed parts of speech | Different grammatical forms in a list | *He is kind, intelligent, and works hard.* | *He is kind, intelligent, and hardworking.* |
| Broken correlative | Non-parallel elements after correlative conjunctions | *She not only sings but also is playing guitar.* | *She not only sings but also plays guitar.* |
| Mixed voice | Active and passive in the same series | *He wrote the report, edited it, and it was published.* | *He wrote the report, edited it, and published it.* |

---

## 6.58 — Comparative Logic: Quick-Reference Table

| Error Type | Description | Example (Incorrect) | Correction |
|---|---|---|---|
| Illogical comparison | Comparing unlike things | *My car is faster than you.* | *My car is faster than yours.* |
| Missing "that/those" | Omitting the demonstrative pronoun | *The climate of Florida is warmer than New York.* | *The climate of Florida is warmer than that of New York.* |
| Missing "other/else" | Not excluding subject from comparison group | *He is smarter than any student in his class.* | *He is smarter than any other student in his class.* |
| Double comparative/superlative | Using "more" + "-er" or "most" + "-est" | *more better* | *better* |
| Less/fewer confusion | Using "less" with countable nouns | *less students* | *fewer students* |
| Like/as confusion | Using "like" before a clause | *She sings like her mother does.* | *She sings as her mother does.* |
| Different from/than | Using "different than" with a noun | *different than mine* | *different from mine* |
| Between/among | Using "between" with more than two | *between five winners* | *among five winners* |
| Amount/number | Using "amount" with countable nouns | *amount of students* | *number of students* |

---

## 6.59 — The "Big Three" Diagnostic Questions

When you encounter any underlined portion, ask yourself these three questions:

1. **Is there a modifier?** If yes, is it next to the word it describes? Is the subject the logical doer?

2. **Is there a series or paired elements?** If yes, are all items in the same grammatical form?

3. **Is there a comparison?** If yes, are the items being compared logically and in the same form?

If the answer to any of these questions is "no," you have identified the error category and can focus your attention on finding the correct answer.

---

## 6.60 — Final Strategic Reminders

1. **Read the full sentence.** Never evaluate an underlined portion in isolation.

2. **Read the answer choices first.** Identify what changes between them to determine the rule being tested.

3. **Trust the rules, not your ear.** While your ear can help you identify obvious errors, the ACT tests formal grammar rules that may sound acceptable in informal speech.

4. **Check for multiple errors.** Some answer choices may fix one error while introducing another. The correct answer must fix the error without creating new ones.

5. **Don't overthink.** If you identify the error category and apply the correct diagnostic method, the answer will usually be clear. If you're stuck between two choices, re-read the sentence with each option inserted and ask: "Which version is both grammatically correct and logically sound?"

6. **Practice pattern recognition.** The more you practice these question types, the faster you'll be able to identify the rule being tested and eliminate wrong answers.

---

*This chapter has provided an exhaustive treatment of modifier placement, parallel structure, and comparative logic. These three categories of error are among the most frequently tested on the ACT English section, and mastery of them is essential for a high score. Review this chapter thoroughly, and you will be equipped to handle any question the ACT throws at you in these areas.*

---


# Chapter 7: Word Choice, Diction, Idioms, and Conciseness — The "Shorter is Better" System and Redundancy Elimination

---

## 7.1 — The Philosophy of Conciseness on the ACT English Section

The ACT English section is, at its core, a test of **editorial precision**. The test makers are not merely checking whether you can identify a grammatical error — they are evaluating whether you can recognize the *most effective* way to express an idea. This means that on many questions, multiple answer choices may be technically grammatically correct, but only one is the best. The ACT's editorial standard is governed by a single, overarching principle:

> **Express the intended meaning in the fewest words possible without sacrificing clarity, tone, or precision.**

This principle is not a minor tip or a secondary strategy. It is the **dominant rhetorical value** of the ACT English section. Understanding this — truly internalizing it — is one of the highest-leverage things you can do to improve your score.

### Why Conciseness Matters So Much

Consider what the ACT is testing: your ability to function as an editor. In real-world editing, one of the most common problems in writing is **bloat** — unnecessary words, redundant phrases, and inflated constructions that pad sentences without adding meaning. A skilled editor recognizes this bloat and eliminates it ruthlessly. The ACT rewards you for doing exactly that.

When you encounter a question where two or more answer choices are grammatically correct and convey the same core meaning, the correct answer will almost always be the **shortest** one. This is not a coincidence or a heuristic — it is a deliberate design feature of the test. The ACT has built conciseness into the scoring architecture of the section.

### The "Shorter is Better" Decision Tree

When evaluating answer choices, apply this decision tree:

1. **Are all choices grammatically correct?** If yes, proceed to step 2. If no, eliminate the grammatically incorrect ones first.
2. **Do all choices convey the same essential meaning?** If yes, proceed to step 3. If no, eliminate choices that distort or change the meaning.
3. **Which choice is the most concise?** That is almost always the correct answer.

This system works because the ACT deliberately includes distractors that are wordier versions of the correct answer. These distractors are designed to *sound* impressive or formal, which makes them tempting. But the ACT does not reward impressiveness — it rewards precision and economy.

### When "Shorter is Better" Does NOT Apply

There are specific situations where the shortest answer is **not** correct:

- **When the shorter choice omits essential information.** If a longer answer includes a qualifying detail, a specific example, or a necessary clarification that the shorter choice lacks, the longer answer may be correct.
- **When the shorter choice changes the meaning.** If a shorter answer is ambiguous, vague, or alters the logical relationship between ideas, it is wrong regardless of its brevity.
- **When the shorter choice is too informal for the passage's tone.** If the passage is formal and the shortest choice uses slang, contractions, or overly casual language, it may be incorrect.
- **When the question specifically asks for elaboration or detail.** Some rhetorical questions ask you to *add* information. In these cases, a longer answer that fulfills the stated goal is correct.

These exceptions are important, but they are **exceptions**. The default rule — the one that should guide your thinking on every single question — is that shorter is better.

---

## 7.2 — Redundancy: The ACT's Favorite Target

Redundancy is the single most common conciseness issue tested on the ACT. A redundant expression is one that **repeats the same idea within itself** — the words themselves, without any context, contain built-in duplication. The ACT loves to include redundant phrases in underlined portions and then offer a concise alternative as an answer choice.

### The Anatomy of Redundancy

Redundancy occurs when a modifier or qualifier is applied to a word that **already contains the meaning of that modifier**. The modifier is therefore unnecessary — it adds words without adding information.

Consider the phrase **"past history."** The word *history* already refers to events that occurred in the past. Adding *past* does not change or refine the meaning — it simply repeats what is already inherent in the word *history*. The correct, concise version is simply **"history."**

Similarly, **"future plans"** is redundant because *plans* are, by definition, about the future. You cannot have "past plans" (those would be *memories* or *records*). The correct version is simply **"plans."**

### Comprehensive Redundancy List

The following is an exhaustive catalog of redundancies that appear on the ACT. You should memorize each one:

| Redundant Phrase | Correct Version | Why It's Redundant |
|---|---|---|
| absolutely essential | essential | "Essential" already means absolutely necessary |
| advance planning | planning | Planning is inherently done in advance |
| added bonus | bonus | A bonus is, by definition, something added |
| basic fundamentals | fundamentals | Fundamentals are already basic |
| brief summary | summary | A summary is inherently brief |
| close proximity | proximity | Proximity means closeness |
| collaborate together | collaborate | Collaborate already means to work together |
| completely finish / completely destroy | finish / destroy | "Finish" and "destroy" are absolute states |
| consensus of opinion | consensus | Consensus already refers to a shared opinion |
| continue to remain | remain | "Remain" implies continuing to be |
| cooperate together | cooperate | Cooperate already means to work together |
| each and every | each OR every | Both words mean the same thing |
| end result | result | A result is the end product |
| exact same | same | "Same" already means identical |
| final conclusion | conclusion | A conclusion is inherently final |
| first and foremost | first OR foremost | Both indicate priority |
| free gift | gift | A gift is, by definition, free |
| general consensus | consensus | Consensus already implies general agreement |
| joint collaboration | collaboration | Collaboration implies joint effort |
| mutual cooperation | cooperation | Cooperation is inherently mutual |
| new innovation | innovation | An innovation is, by definition, new |
| past history | history | History is inherently about the past |
| personal opinion | opinion | An opinion is inherently personal |
| plan ahead | plan | Planning is inherently about the future |
| reason why | reason | "Reason" already explains why |
| refer back | refer | "Refer" already means to look back to something |
| repeat again | repeat | "Repeat" means to do again |
| revert back | revert | "Revert" means to go back |
| still remains | remains | "Remains" already implies continuation |
| sum total | sum OR total | Both words mean the same thing |
| true fact | fact | A fact is, by definition, true |
| unexpected surprise | surprise | A surprise is, by definition, unexpected |
| very unique / completely unique | unique | "Unique" is an absolute — there are no degrees |

### The "Absolute" Category

A special subcategory of redundancy involves **absolute adjectives** — words that represent an extreme or binary state and therefore cannot logically be modified by degree words like *very, completely, more, most, quite, rather*, or *somewhat*.

Common absolute adjectives include:

- **unique** (there is no "very unique" — something either is unique or it isn't)
- **perfect** (nothing can be "more perfect" or "very perfect")
- **essential** (something is either essential or it isn't)
- **impossible** (something cannot be "very impossible")
- **dead** (there are no degrees of death)
- **pregnant** (a person is either pregnant or not)
- **fatal** (an outcome is either fatal or it isn't)
- **universal** (something is either universal or it isn't)
- **infinite** (cannot be "more infinite")
- **equal** (things are either equal or they aren't)
- **opposite** (things are either opposite or they aren't)

The ACT occasionally tests this concept. If you see an answer choice that pairs a degree word with an absolute adjective, that choice is almost certainly wrong.

### Wordy Phrases and Their Concise Equivalents

Beyond pure redundancies, the ACT tests **wordy phrases** — multi-word constructions that can be replaced with a single word or a shorter phrase without any loss of meaning:

| Wordy Phrase | Concise Alternative |
|---|---|
| due to the fact that | because |
| in order to | to |
| at this point in time | now / currently |
| in the event that | if |
| for the purpose of | to / for |
| in spite of the fact that | although / despite |
| on account of | because |
| with regard to / with respect to | about / regarding |
| in the near future | soon |
| at the present time | now / currently |
| has the ability to / is able to | can |
| a large number of | many |
| a majority of | most |
| a number of | some / several |
| the reason is because | the reason is that / because |
| it is important to note that | [delete entirely] |
| it should be noted that | [delete entirely] |
| there is no doubt that | undoubtedly / clearly |
| until such time as | until |
| in the process of | while / currently |
| make a decision | decide |
| take into consideration | consider |
| come to the realization | realize |
| give consideration to | consider |
| have a tendency to | tend to |
| is indicative of | indicates |
| is reflective of | reflects |
| make an adjustment to | adjust |
| conduct an investigation | investigate |
| make an improvement | improve |
| provide assistance to | assist / help |
| have an effect on | affect |
| is representative of | represents |
| in close proximity to | near / close to |
| has a preference for | prefers |
| is in possession of | has / possesses |
| is reminiscent of | resembles |
| is characteristic of | characterizes |
| a sufficient amount of | enough |
| in excess of | more than / over |
| prior to | before |
| subsequent to | after |
| in conjunction with | with / along with |
| in the absence of | without |
| in the vicinity of | near / around |
| with the exception of | except / besides |
| in the amount of | for |

### How Redundancy Questions Appear on the Test

Redundancy questions typically appear in one of three formats:

**Format 1: The underlined portion contains the redundancy, and a shorter answer choice eliminates it.**

- *Underlined:* "The scientists conducted an investigation in close proximity to the fault line."
- *Correct answer:* "The scientists conducted an investigation near the fault line."

**Format 2: The "OMIT" option is correct because the entire underlined portion is redundant.**

- *Underlined:* "It is important to note that the experiment yielded unexpected results."
- *Correct answer:* OMIT — the phrase "it is important to note that" adds no information.

**Format 3: Two answer choices are grammatically correct, but one is more concise.**

- *Choice A:* "The report was due to the fact that the data was incomplete."
- *Choice B:* "The report was because the data was incomplete."
- *Choice B is correct because it is more concise.*

---

## 7.3 — Diction: Choosing the Right Word

Diction questions test whether you can select the word that **most precisely and appropriately** conveys the intended meaning in context. These questions are distinct from grammar questions — the issue is not whether a word is grammatically correct, but whether it is the *right word* for the situation.

### The Hierarchy of Word Choice

When evaluating diction questions, think in terms of a hierarchy:

1. **Correct meaning:** Does the word accurately convey the intended meaning? (This is non-negotiable.)
2. **Appropriate connotation:** Does the word carry the right emotional or associative weight?
3. **Appropriate register:** Does the word match the formality level of the passage?
4. **Precision:** Is the word more specific and exact than the alternatives?

### Commonly Confused Word Pairs

The ACT frequently tests pairs of words that are commonly confused. Mastering these pairs is essential:

#### Affect vs. Effect

- **Affect** (verb): to influence or produce a change in something.
  - *"The drought **affected** crop yields throughout the Midwest."*
- **Effect** (noun): a result or consequence.
  - *"The **effect** of the drought was widespread crop failure."*

**Exceptions:** *Effect* can occasionally function as a verb meaning "to bring about" or "to cause to happen" (e.g., "to **effect** change"). *Affect* can function as a noun in psychology, referring to emotional expression (e.g., "a flat **affect**"). However, on the ACT, the verb/noun distinction described above is the primary rule tested.

#### Its vs. It's

- **Its** (possessive pronoun): belonging to it.
  - *"The company changed **its** policy."*
- **It's** (contraction): it is or it has.
  - *"**It's** been a challenging year."*

**Test:** Try substituting "it is" or "it has" into the sentence. If the substitution works, use *it's*. If it doesn't, use *its*.

#### Their vs. There vs. They're

- **Their** (possessive pronoun): belonging to them.
  - *"The students submitted **their** assignments."*
- **There** (adverb/existential): in that place; used to introduce a sentence.
  - *"**There** are three reasons for this trend."*
- **They're** (contraction): they are.
  - *"**They're** planning to attend the conference."*

#### Then vs. Than

- **Then** (adverb): refers to time or sequence.
  - *"She finished her homework, and **then** she went to bed."*
- **Than** (conjunction): used in comparisons.
  - *"She works faster **than** her colleague."*

#### Who's vs. Whose

- **Who's** (contraction): who is or who has.
  - *"**Who's** responsible for this decision?"*
- **Whose** (possessive): belonging to whom.
  - *"**Whose** jacket was left in the conference room?"*

#### Your vs. You're

- **Your** (possessive): belonging to you.
  - *"**Your** presentation was excellent."*
- **You're** (contraction): you are.
  - *"**You're** going to be impressed by the results."*

#### Passed vs. Past

- **Passed** (verb, past tense): went by; succeeded.
  - *"She **passed** the exam with flying colors."*
- **Past** (noun/adjective/preposition): earlier time; beyond.
  - *"In the **past**, this process took weeks." / "He walked **past** the building."*

#### Lead vs. Led

- **Lead** (verb, present tense): to guide.
  - *"She will **lead** the delegation."*
- **Led** (verb, past tense): guided.
  - *"She **led** the delegation last year."*

**Note:** *Lead* is also a noun referring to the metal (pronounced like "led"), but the ACT primarily tests the verb forms.

#### Lose vs. Loose

- **Lose** (verb): to misplace; to fail to win.
  - *"Don't **lose** your keys."*
- **Loose** (adjective): not tight; not firmly fixed.
  - *"The screw was **loose**."*

#### Accept vs. Except

- **Accept** (verb): to receive or agree to.
  - *"I **accept** your apology."*
- **Except** (preposition): excluding; other than.
  - *"Everyone attended **except** Maria."*

#### Principal vs. Principle

- **Principal** (noun/adjective): the head of a school; most important.
  - *"The **principal** announced the new policy." / "The **principal** cause was identified."*
- **Principle** (noun): a rule, belief, or fundamental truth.
  - *"She refused to compromise her **principles**."*

#### Complement vs. Compliment

- **Complement** (noun/verb): something that completes or goes well with something else.
  - *"The wine was a perfect **complement** to the meal."*
- **Compliment** (noun/verb): praise or an expression of admiration.
  - *"She received a **compliment** on her presentation."*

#### Stationary vs. Stationery

- **Stationary** (adjective): not moving; fixed in place.
  - *"The car remained **stationary** at the red light."*
- **Stationery** (noun): writing materials (paper, envelopes, etc.).
  - *"She wrote the letter on elegant **stationery**."*

#### Allude vs. Elude

- **Allude** (verb): to refer to something indirectly.
  - *"The speaker **alluded** to the controversy without naming names."*
- **Elude** (verb): to escape or avoid capture.
  - *"The suspect **eluded** the police for weeks."*

#### Elicit vs. Illicit

- **Elicit** (verb): to draw out or provoke a response.
  - *"The question **elicited** a strong reaction."*
- **Illicit** (adjective): illegal or forbidden.
  - *"The investigation uncovered **illicit** financial transactions."*

#### Precede vs. Proceed

- **Precede** (verb): to come before.
  - *"The opening remarks will **precede** the keynote address."*
- **Proceed** (verb): to continue or move forward.
  - *"After the interruption, the speaker **proceeded** with her presentation."*

#### Ensure vs. Insure

- **Ensure** (verb): to make certain.
  - *"Please **ensure** that all doors are locked."*
- **Insure** (verb): to provide insurance for.
  - *"The company **insured** the building against flood damage."*

#### Disinterested vs. Uninterested

- **Disinterested** (adjective): impartial; unbiased.
  - *"A **disinterested** mediator facilitated the negotiation."*
- **Uninterested** (adjective): not interested; indifferent.
  - *"He was **uninterested** in the topic."*

#### Infer vs. Imply

- **Infer** (verb): to conclude or deduce (done by the reader/listener).
  - *"From her tone, I **inferred** that she was upset."*
- **Imply** (verb): to suggest indirectly (done by the speaker/writer).
  - *"Her tone **implied** that she was upset."*

#### Farther vs. Further

- **Farther** (adverb): refers to physical distance.
  - *"The airport is **farther** than I thought."*
- **Further** (adverb): refers to figurative distance or degree; additionally.
  - *"We need to discuss this **further**." / "No **further** action is required."*

#### Between vs. Among

- **Between** (preposition): used when referring to **two** distinct items.
  - *"The agreement was signed **between** the two companies."*
- **Among** (preposition): used when referring to **three or more** items in a group.
  - *"The decision was made **among** the five committee members."*

#### Amount vs. Number

- **Amount** (noun): used for uncountable things.
  - *"A large **amount** of water was consumed."*
- **Number** (noun): used for countable things.
  - *"A large **number** of students attended."*

#### Less vs. Fewer

- **Less** (adjective/adverb): used for uncountable nouns.
  - *"There is **less** water in the reservoir this year."*
- **Fewer** (adjective/adverb): used for countable nouns.
  - *"There are **fewer** students enrolled this semester."*

#### Whether vs. If

- **Whether** (conjunction): used to express a choice between two possibilities or to indicate that something is uncertain.
  - *"She hasn't decided **whether** to attend the conference."*
- **If** (conjunction): used for conditional statements.
  - *"**If** it rains, the event will be moved indoors."*

#### Comprise vs. Compose

- **Comprise**: The whole comprises the parts. *"The committee **comprises** ten members."*
- **Compose**: The parts compose the whole. *"Ten members **compose** the committee."*
- **Is composed of** is correct; **is comprised of** is incorrect on the ACT.

#### Momentary vs. Momentous

- **Momentary**: lasting for a very short time.
- **Momentous**: of great importance or significance.

#### Tortuous vs. Torturous

- **Tortuous**: twisting, complex, or winding.
- **Torturous**: relating to or causing torture; extremely painful.

#### Continuous vs. Continual

- **Continuous**: uninterrupted; without stopping.
- **Continual**: recurring frequently; repeated at intervals.

#### Ingenious vs. Ingenuous

- **Ingenious**: clever, original, and inventive.
- **Ingenuous**: innocent, unsuspecting, and naive.

#### Notable vs. Noticeable

- **Notable**: worthy of attention or remark (implies importance).
- **Noticeable**: capable of being seen or noticed (implies perception).

### The Substitution Test for Diction Questions

When you're unsure which word is correct, try this approach:

1. **Identify the meaning** the sentence requires. What concept is the author trying to express?
2. **Substitute each answer choice** into the sentence and read it mentally.
3. **Eliminate choices** that don't match the required meaning.
4. **Among the remaining choices**, select the one that is most precise and appropriate for the context.

---

## 7.4 — Tone and Register: Matching the Passage's Voice

Every passage on the ACT has an established **tone** — a consistent attitude or emotional quality that pervades the writing. Your job as an editor is to ensure that every word, phrase, and sentence is consistent with that tone.

### The Tone Spectrum

ACT passages fall along a spectrum from **formal** to **informal**:

**Formal Tone Characteristics:**
- No contractions (*don't → do not, can't → cannot*)
- No slang or colloquialisms
- Precise, sophisticated vocabulary
- Complex sentence structures
- Third-person or impersonal perspective
- Common in: Social Sciences, Natural Sciences, Humanities, Argument passages

**Informal Tone Characteristics:**
- Contractions are acceptable
- Conversational vocabulary
- Simpler sentence structures
- First-person perspective is common
- Common in: Literary Narrative passages, personal essays

### How Tone Questions Appear

Tone questions typically ask something like:
- "Which choice best maintains the tone of the passage?"
- "Which choice is most consistent with the style of the essay?"

**Strategy:** Read the surrounding sentences (or the entire paragraph) to establish the tone. Then evaluate each answer choice against that tone. Eliminate choices that are too casual for a formal passage or too stiff for an informal one.

### Register-Specific Word Choices

| Too Casual (Eliminate in Formal Passages) | Formal Alternative |
|---|---|
| a lot | many / much / considerably |
| kind of / sort of | somewhat / rather |
| stuff | material / elements / components |
| get (as a general verb) | obtain / acquire / receive |
| big | significant / substantial / considerable |
| good | effective / beneficial / advantageous |
| bad | detrimental / adverse / unfavorable |
| thing | element / factor / aspect |
| like (as a filler) | such as / for example |
| okay / OK | acceptable / satisfactory |
| kids | children / adolescents |
| guy | individual / person |

---

## 7.5 — Idioms and Prepositions: The Collocation System

Idiomatic expressions are fixed phrases whose meanings cannot be deduced from the individual words. The ACT tests **idiomatic preposition usage** — the correct preposition to use after a specific verb, adjective, or noun.

### Why Idioms Matter

In English, certain words "demand" specific prepositions. There is no logical rule that explains why we say "interested **in**" rather than "interested **about**" — it is simply the conventional, idiomatic usage. The ACT tests your familiarity with these conventions.

### Comprehensive Idiom List

The following is a thorough list of idiomatic preposition pairings that appear on the ACT:

#### Verbs + Prepositions

| Idiom | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| account for | explain | "The report **accounts for** the discrepancy." |
| adapt to | adjust to | "She **adapted to** the new environment." |
| agree with (person) | share an opinion | "I **agree with** the author." |
| agree to (plan/terms) | consent to | "He **agreed to** the proposal." |
| agree on (matter) | reach consensus | "They **agreed on** a strategy." |
| appeal to | request / attract | "The group **appealed to** the court." |
| apply to / for | be relevant / request | "This rule **applies to** all cases." / "She **applied for** the position." |
| approve of | support | "The board **approved of** the plan." |
| attribute to | credit to | "She **attributes** her success **to** hard work." |
| belong to | be owned by | "The book **belongs to** me." |
| blame for | hold responsible | "He was **blamed for** the error." |
| care about | be concerned | "She **cares about** the environment." |
| care for | look after | "He **cares for** his elderly mother." |
| compare to (metaphorical) | liken to | "The poet **compares** love **to** a rose." |
| compare with (analytical) | examine similarities/differences | "The study **compares** the results **with** previous data." |
| complain about | express dissatisfaction | "Residents **complained about** the noise." |
| concentrate on | focus on | "The report **concentrates on** key issues." |
| consist of | be made up of | "The team **consists of** five members." |
| contribute to | help cause | "Several factors **contributed to** the decline." |
| deal with | handle | "The committee must **deal with** this issue." |
| decide on | make a choice | "They **decided on** a course of action." |
| depend on | rely on | "Success **depends on** preparation." |
| differ from | be different from | "The two approaches **differ from** each other." |
| differ in | be different in a specific way | "The models **differ in** size." |
| dream of/about | imagine | "She **dreamed of** traveling the world." |
| escape from | get away from | "He **escaped from** the building." |
| excel in/at | be excellent at | "She **excels in** mathematics." |
| exclude from | leave out | "They were **excluded from** the study." |
| insist on | demand | "The manager **insisted on** compliance." |
| invest in | put resources into | "The company **invested in** new technology." |
| listen to | pay attention to | "Please **listen to** the instructions." |
| long for | desire deeply | "She **longed for** a simpler life." |
| object to | oppose | "Several members **objected to** the proposal." |
| participate in | take part in | "Students **participated in** the experiment." |
| persist in | continue despite difficulty | "He **persisted in** his efforts." |
| prevent from | stop | "The policy **prevents** companies **from** polluting." |
| prohibit from | forbid | "The law **prohibits** employers **from** discriminating." |
| protect from/against | shield | "The vaccine **protects against** infection." |
| provide with | supply | "The hotel **provides** guests **with** amenities." |
| refer to | mention / direct attention to | "The author **refers to** several studies." |
| rely on | depend on | "Patients **rely on** their doctors." |
| remind of | cause to remember | "This song **reminds** me **of** my childhood." |
| result in | cause | "The policy **resulted in** significant changes." |
| search for | look for | "Researchers **searched for** a cure." |
| spend on | use resources for | "They **spent** millions **on** research." |
| subscribe to | support / receive | "She **subscribes to** the journal." |
| succeed in | achieve a goal | "He **succeeded in** passing the exam." |
| suffer from | experience (illness/problem) | "She **suffers from** allergies." |
| take advantage of | use an opportunity | "They **took advantage of** the situation." |
| wait for | expect | "We are **waiting for** the results." |
| warn about/of | caution | "The agency **warned about** the risks." |
| watch for | be alert for | "Investors should **watch for** changes." |
| work on | engage in effort | "The team is **working on** a solution." |
| worry about | be anxious | "She **worries about** her health." |

|---|---|---|

| result of | outcome | "The **result of** the experiment was unexpected." |
| rise in | increase | "There was a **rise in** temperatures." |
| risk of | danger | "The **risk of** infection is low." |
| room for | space/opportunity | "There is **room for** improvement." |
| search for | quest | "The **search for** a cure continues." |
| solution to | answer | "The **solution to** the problem was elegant." |
| success in | achievement | "Her **success in** the field is well-known." |
| supply of | provision | "The **supply of** water is limited." |
| sympathy for | compassion | "She expressed **sympathy for** the victims." |
| threat to | danger | "The **threat to** biodiversity is real." |
| understanding of | comprehension | "His **understanding of** the subject is thorough." |

### The "Might Of" Trap

One of the most common diction errors on the ACT is the substitution of **"of"** for **"have"** in modal verb constructions:

| Incorrect | Correct |
|---|---|
| could of | could have |
| would of | would have |
| should of | should have |
| might of | might have |
| must of | must have |

This error arises from the fact that "have" is often pronounced like "of" in rapid speech (e.g., "could've" sounds like "could of"). On the ACT, **"could of" is always wrong**. The correct form is always **"could have"** (or the contraction **"could've"**).

### Strategy for Idiom Questions

1. **Trust your ear first.** If you've read enough standard English, many idiomatic errors will "sound wrong."
2. **If your ear fails you,** consider whether the preposition logically connects to the word it follows. While idioms are conventional (not logical), some prepositions have consistent spatial or relational meanings that can help.
3. **When in doubt, choose the most standard, formal option.** The ACT prefers formal, standard English over colloquial usage.
4. **Memorize the high-frequency idioms** listed above. The ACT recycles the same idioms repeatedly.

---

## 7.6 — Logical Comparisons: Ensuring Apples-to-Apples

A **logical comparison** ensures that the things being compared are of the same type and that the comparison makes sense. The ACT frequently tests this concept.

### The "Other" Rule

When comparing a member of a group to the rest of the group, you must use **"other"** to exclude the member from the group:

- **Illogical:** "She is smarter than **anyone** in her class." (She is in her class, so this would mean she's smarter than herself.)
- **Logical:** "She is smarter than **anyone else** in her class."

Similarly:
- **Illogical:** "This car is faster than **any car** on the lot."
- **Logical:** "This car is faster than **any other car** on the lot."

### Comparing Like to Like

You must compare things of the same type:

- **Illogical:** "My car is faster than **you**." (Comparing a car to a person.)
- **Logical:** "My car is faster than **yours**." (Comparing a car to a car.)

- **Illogical:** "The climate of Florida is warmer than **New York**." (Comparing a climate to a state.)
- **Logical:** "The climate of Florida is warmer than **that of New York**." (Comparing a climate to a climate.)

### The "One of the" Construction

When using the phrase "one of the [superlative adjective]," the noun that follows must be **plural**:

- **Incorrect:** "She is one of the smartest **student** in the program."
- **Correct:** "She is one of the smartest **students** in the program."

The logic: If she is "one of" the smartest, there must be multiple smart students — she is one member of a group.

### Double Comparatives and Superlatives

Never combine "more" with "-er" or "most" with "-est":

- **Incorrect:** "She is **more smarter** than her brother."
- **Correct:** "She is **smarter** than her brother."

- **Incorrect:** "This is the **most easiest** problem on the test."
- **Correct:** "This is the **easiest** problem on the test."

---

## 7.7 — The "Would Of" and Double Negative Rules

### The "Would Of" Rule (Expanded)

As discussed in the idiom section, **"would of," "could of," "should of," "might of,"** and **"must of"** are **always wrong** on the ACT. The correct forms are **would have, could have, should have, might have,** and **must have**.

This rule is so consistently tested that you should treat it as an automatic elimination criterion. If any answer choice contains "of" after a modal verb, eliminate it immediately.

### The Double Negative Rule

A **double negative** occurs when two negative words appear in the same clause, creating a positive meaning (or simply a confusing, ungrammatical construction). On the ACT, **double negatives are always wrong.**

Common negative words to watch for:

- **never**
- **no**
- **not**
- **neither**
- **nobody**
- **nothing**
- **nowhere**
- **nor**
- **hardly** (already negative)
- **barely** (already negative)
- **scarcely** (already negative)

**Examples of double negatives:**

- *"I **hardly** never see him."* → **Incorrect** (hardly + never = double negative)
- *"She **couldn't** find **no** one to help."* → **Incorrect** (couldn't + no = double negative)
- *"There **wasn't** **nothing** to do."* → **Incorrect** (wasn't + nothing = double negative)
- *"He **neither** called **nor** emailed."* → **Correct** (neither...nor is a correlative conjunction, not a double negative)

**Note:** The construction "neither...nor" is **not** a double negative — it is a standard correlative conjunction that expresses a negative relationship between two items. It is grammatically correct and commonly tested.

---

## 7.8 — Conciseness in Action: The OMIT Question

The "OMIT" question is a special type of conciseness question that appears frequently on the ACT. When "OMIT the underlined portion" is an answer choice, you must decide whether the underlined text should be deleted entirely.

### When OMIT is Correct

OMIT is correct when the underlined portion is:

1. **Redundant** — it repeats information already stated in the sentence or passage.
2. **Irrelevant** — it introduces information that is off-topic or unrelated to the paragraph's main idea.
3. **Wordy without adding meaning** — it uses multiple words to express an idea that could be expressed more concisely (or that doesn't need to be expressed at all).
4. **Grammatically unnecessary** — removing it would not break the sentence's grammar.

### When OMIT is Incorrect

OMIT is incorrect when the underlined portion:

1. **Adds essential information** — the sentence would lose important meaning without it.
2. **Provides a necessary transition** — removing it would disrupt the logical flow.
3. **Serves a specific rhetorical purpose** — it supports the paragraph's main idea, provides evidence, or clarifies a point.
4. **Is grammatically necessary** — removing it would create a fragment or other grammatical error.

### The OMIT Probability

Research and test analysis show that **OMIT is the correct answer approximately 40–50% of the time it appears** as an option. This is a remarkably high probability. Many students are reluctant to choose OMIT because it feels counterintuitive to delete text entirely. Do not let this reluctance cost you points. Evaluate OMIT on its merits, just like any other answer choice.

### Common OMIT Scenarios

**Scenario 1: The underlined phrase is a wordy restatement of the preceding text.**
- *"The experiment was a complete and total failure. It was an **absolute disaster**."*
- OMIT "absolute disaster" — it's redundant with "complete and total failure."

**Scenario 2: The underlined phrase is a filler expression that adds no information.**
- *"The results, **it should be noted**, were statistically significant."*
- OMIT "it should be noted" — it's a filler phrase.

**Scenario 3: The underlined phrase is an unnecessary qualifier.**
- *"The **somewhat** surprising finding challenged existing assumptions."*
- If the passage has already established that the finding was surprising, "somewhat" is unnecessary and may even contradict the established tone.

**Scenario 4: The entire sentence is off-topic.**
- If a sentence in a paragraph about economic policy suddenly discusses a personal anecdote about the author's vacation, the entire sentence should be OMIT'd.

---

## 7.9 — The "Due To" vs. "Because Of" Distinction

This is a subtle but testable distinction:

- **"Due to"** functions as an **adjective** — it modifies a noun.
  - *"The delay was **due to** weather."* ("Due to weather" modifies "delay.")
  - Test: Can you replace "due to" with "caused by"? If yes, "due to" is correct.

- **"Because of"** functions as an **adverb** — it modifies a verb.
  - *"The game was canceled **because of** weather."* ("Because of weather" modifies "canceled.")
  - Test: Can you replace "because of" with "due to"? If the sentence still makes grammatical sense, "because of" is correct.

**The ACT's preference:** When the phrase modifies a verb (which is most of the time in the constructions tested), **"because of"** is correct. When it modifies a noun, **"due to"** is correct.

---

## 7.10 — Putting It All Together: The Conciseness Decision Framework

When you encounter a question where multiple answer choices are grammatically correct, apply this systematic framework:

### Step 1: Identify the Core Meaning
What is the essential information that must be conveyed? Strip away all embellishment and identify the bare minimum.
